# 坐花志果

**Seated Among Flowers, Mindful of Fruits: A Record of Karmic Retribution**
*By Wang Daoding of the Qing Dynasty*

**Preface**

*The Record of Seated Blossoms and Ripe Fruits* is a work by my friend, Wang Diaosheng. Diaosheng, a renowned scholar of western Zhejiang, followed his official post to Jiangzuo, repeatedly serving as a key aide in prominent yamen, his reputation resounding throughout the literary world. He took what he had absorbed in daily life and committed it to writing. Those unfamiliar with Diaosheng may be daunted by the breadth of his observations, deeming the book a mere source of witty tales to provoke laughter. Yet those who know him say: “This is Diaosheng’s苦心 to save the world, using his pen as his tongue. Even the morning bell that awakens from dreams or the precious raft that guides through the ford of delusion cannot surpass it.”

Since the *Nine Hundred Tales* of Yu Chu, fiction from the Han and Wei dynasties onward has favored extravagant and fantastical words, with narratives spun from trivial matters and reckless speech proliferating daily. Such works, like the frivolous banquets of *Fuxiu* or the absurd tales of *Qi Xie*, are shallow and lack substance, wild yet without foundation. Even when compiled into volumes, they do little to edify the world. Song scholars, by contrast, excelled in chronicling dynastic affairs, court rituals, and the deeds of the past, often serving as sources for historians.

In our present dynasty, *Strange Tales from Liaozhai* by Pu Liuxian has been widely emulated. Its brushwork rivals that of Sima Qian and Ban Gu, showcasing the utmost skill of a literary master. Yet half of its accounts involve ghosts and foxes, while the rest are interspersed with playful anecdotes, leaving discerning readers with some regret. During the Qianlong era, the eminent scholar Ji Xiaolan of Hejian, endowed with vast and refined talent, compiled the *Siku Quanshu* catalog. In his spare time, he produced *Luanyang’s Summer Records*, *Thus Have I Heard*, and *Huai Xi’s Miscellany*, which circulated widely across the land. His works, though meticulous in textual research and argumentation, are accessible only to those deeply learned and thoughtful. By contrast, *baiguan* novels—from scholars to farmers and merchants—are universally read with delight. Thus, embedding moral lessons within their narratives ensures their easy dissemination and reception. Truly, the morning bell that awakens from dreams and the precious raft that guides through delusion find their embodiment in Xiaolan.

Now, Diaosheng, possessing refined talent, aspired to advance through imperial examinations but repeatedly stumbled at the gates of the examination hall, his ambitions thwarted. With no alternative, he entered officialdom as a minor clerk. The lament of talented men serving as petty officials is a sorrow shared across the ages. This book’s narratives are concise and elegant, its judgments deliberate. Moreover, it adheres to factual accuracy, verifying details through extensive inquiry, avoiding baseless fabrications or repetitive clichés. Whether it rivals Xiaolan’s work, I dare not say. Yet its苦心 to save the world only deepens my admiration for Diaosheng.

Recently, Diaosheng has resided in my hometown, and our interactions have grown closer, our bond deeper. Entrusted with the task of proofreading, I, despite my humble abilities, offer this preface.

*Written in the fourth month of summer, the year Ding Si of the Xianfeng reign, by Jing Lvji of Danyang.*

---

**Phonetic and Explanatory Notes on the Preface to *The Record of Seated Blossoms and Ripe Fruits***

*The Record of Seated Blossoms and Ripe Fruits* presents a tapestry of karmic retribution, blending exhortation and admonition—truly a virtuous work. Yet it has long lacked phonetic annotations, much to the regret of its readers. I wished to provide them but hesitated due to my own inadequacies. Perhaps, given the vastness of the world, there are like-minded individuals who might undertake this task. For now, I can only wait.

In the year *Wu Yin*, while residing in Shanghai, I gathered with fellow scholars to pool resources for printing virtuous books. Among them was a volume I had long wished to annotate: *The Record of Seated Blossoms and Ripe Fruits*. Earlier, Mr. Hu Mengxiang had also been a guest here. In his spare time from studies, he took up this book and provided phonetic and explanatory notes. Morning and evening, he researched and verified, completing the task over two seasons. His苦心 is evident. Moreover, he chose not to reveal his name, attributing the work to the “Woodcutter of Vulture Peak,” a choice that further demonstrates his noble intent.

From this, we see that the reservoir of goodness throughout history remains ever-present in the world. I dare not claim Mr. Hu’s heart mirrors my own. Yet his love for virtue aligns with my long-held, unfulfilled aspiration—what fortune it is to find such resonance! Now, Mr. Hu has suddenly passed away, unable to witness the completion of this book. Truly, this is a great pity. As for the precision of his research and the accuracy of his philological explanations, they are evident to all who read them—what more need be said?

*Written in the third month of spring, the year Wu Yin of the Guangxu reign, by the Master of Wenzhu Studio in Shanghai.*

---

*The Record of Seated Blossoms and Ripe Fruits* is the work of Mr. Wang of Renhe, who, with the extraordinary talent of a blind left eye, chronicled the true events of the Xianfeng and Daoguang eras. He vividly depicted what he had seen and heard in daily life, rendering scenes with such clarity that they seem to unfold before the reader. How could this be merely a rigid exercise in promoting virtue?

Yet in its meticulous recounting of events, the principle that "goodness leads to prosperity, while evil brings ruin" is made manifest without the slightest deviation. Those who read this book may first find it strange and take delight in it, but soon recognize its truth and feel a chill of fear. Its power to uphold the moral order and transform human hearts operates invisibly yet irresistibly, turning the course of things beyond all bounds.

From ancient times to the present, sacred scriptures, worthy biographies, and books of admonition and encouragement have filled countless chests and boxes. Yet though their solemn pronouncements and earnest exhortations are more than sufficient to strike deep fear into the heart, over time they become mere objects of idle curiosity. Some dismiss them as distant and irrelevant, others as stale and hackneyed, and most consign them to the highest shelves, never to be opened again. For the more urgent the admonition, the greater the aversion it breeds; the stricter the supervision, the more vehement the resistance.

This book alone, with its fresh and striking approach, invites eager circulation, while its vivid accounts of karmic retribution awaken the innate goodness of human nature and sever the roots of selfish desire. Its subtle yet profound transformative power defies full description. In its artful use of indirect remonstrance, Mr. Wang’s craftsmanship is truly masterful, and his intentions deeply earnest.

We, his peers, all recognize the great significance of this book for the moral order and the human heart, and thus hasten to entrust it to the printers, that it may be transmitted for generations to come.

*Respectfully inscribed by fellow scholars in the twelfth lunar month of the year Ji Chou, the fifteenth year of the Guangxu reign.*

**General Principles**
1. This book records only events of admonition and encouragement from the past thirty years, as witnessed or heard by the author. Matters from more distant times, already documented by earlier worthies, are not redundantly included, to avoid the charge of plagiarism or mere repetition.
2. This book aims to be accessible to both the refined and the common, intelligible even to women and children. Thus, the prose is deliberately detailed and clear, neither overly concise at the risk of obscurity nor excessively novel at the expense of authenticity.
3. As karma demands that one’s deeds be recounted in person, and as the ancient teaching of concealing faults while praising virtue remains luminous, this compilation records the full names and native places of those whose good deeds merit inclusion, provided they can be verified. For evil deeds, only the events are described, without naming the individuals, so that filial descendants need not sigh in shame while reading.
4. All events in this compilation are based on factual evidence; none are fabricated or follow the fantastical style of the *Qi Xie* or *Zhi Guai*. However, as many accounts derive from friends’ and acquaintances’ retellings, discrepancies may arise. Should any inconsistencies be found, we humbly ask knowledgeable readers for their forbearance.

**Notes on Pronunciation and Exegesis**
1. The annotations in this book aim to ensure its wide circulation. Wherever a phrase or character might pose difficulty, explanations are provided in detail, so that even those with only basic literacy may grasp the meaning with ease.
2. For citations from various sources, if the full context is necessary for clarity, only the title and passage are noted. If the citation is a well-known phrase, only its source is indicated, for the sake of simplicity.
3. For citations whose origins cannot be immediately traced, the phrase is marked “source unknown,” followed by a tentative explanation based on the compiler’s understanding. If the meaning remains obscure, it is left unannotated, awaiting the insights of learned scholars.
4. If a citation has been fully annotated in a previous section, later references simply note “see annotation in [section name].”
5. Characters requiring phonetic or semantic clarification are annotated in every section where they appear. No omissions are made on the grounds that a character has been annotated earlier, so that readers may understand the text even when consulting it at random. However, if a character appears multiple times within the same section, its annotation is not repeated.
6. In annotating difficult characters, earlier works of exegesis (such as the *Tang Yun*, *Ji Yun*, *Zheng Yun*, and *Yun Hui*) are followed strictly, without substituting homophonous characters. For example, the character 嗫 in 嗫嚅 is glossed as 日涉切 in the *Ji Yun*, and here we retain the pronunciation of ; the character 魇 in 梦魇 is glossed as 幺琰切 in the *Yun Hui*, and here we retain the pronunciation of . Yet such annotations may still prove cumbersome for readers. Thus, in such cases, we replace them with more familiar characters to better serve the original intent of the annotations.
7. For difficult characters in the level tone where no common equivalent exists, a character from the rising, departing, or entering tones with a similar pronunciation is chosen, following the rules of *xieyun* (rime-matching). Though not all readers may distinguish the four tones, and some may still read a rising-tone character as level or an entering-tone character as departing, the pronunciation remains sufficiently close to avoid gross error—far preferable to glossing a difficult character with another difficult one.
8. Book titles, original texts, and difficult characters within original annotations are not further glossed. Explanatory notes likewise avoid difficult characters wherever possible.

**Case in Point: Filial Son He**
*Filial piety stands foremost among virtues—its rewards are no lie,*
*Else how could such a paragon of manhood arise?*

Wealth and nobility—both are decreed by Heaven’s dispensation;
Yet how much more joy when the art does not betray the Green Crow’s lore.

A certain scholar from Jiangyou (that is, Jiangxi) was skilled in the Green Crow’s art (the *Baopuzi* records the Yellow Emperor’s *Book of Earth-Inspection* under the name of Green Crow. *Note:* Green Crow was a disciple of Peng Zu. *Observation:* As Green Crow excelled in earth-inspection, the craft came to be called the Green Crow’s art). While traveling as a guest in Daozhou of Chu (Hunan, in the southern reaches of Chu), he found a propitious site. As he stood gazing and looking up, two men approached—one clad in resplendent robes, the other holding a luopan compass and scanning left and right, declaring, “This site is inauspicious.” The scholar secretly scoffed at his presumption and hastened to speak with them, each inquiring of the other’s homeland and clan (to inquire: *xún*, rising tone, meaning “to ask.” *Mutual inquiry* refers to both parties questioning one another. *Homeland* denotes one’s native place; *clan*, one’s surname). The finely dressed man was the son of a wealthy family in the city; the one with the compass, a geomancer. Hearing the scholar was from Jiangyou, the geomancer said, “Jiangxi abounds with renowned *kanyu* masters (the *Yiwen Zhi* of the *Former Han History* records *Kanyu Jindu* in fourteen scrolls. *Kan* pertains to the Way of Heaven; *yu*, to the Way of Earth. *Observation:* Those versed in fengshui are called *kanyu* masters, deriving from this). You must be most accomplished.” The scholar demurred modestly while subtly displaying his art (to display: *xuàn*, *hùxiàn* cut, *xuán*, departing tone. *Subtly displaying* means to reveal slightly). The geomancer was deeply impressed and, addressing the wealthy man’s son, invited the scholar to return with them. The scholar lodged as a guest in their home (as recorded in the *Song History: Biography of Ding Wei*: “The host should not speak further”).

The scholar, having lodged as a guest in their home (as the term *jūtíng* denotes residing in another’s household), was about to reveal the auspicious site to them when he silently reflected: “This land cannot be borne save by one of great virtue and power.” After some time, observing that their conduct and principles were not those of one destined for such fortune, he kept the matter secret.

It so happened that the wealthy family’s relative, Squire Xiao, wished to inter his parent and sought a geomancer through the family’s connections. The scholar accepted the invitation and went. Squire Xiao was a true elder (as recorded in the *Records of the Grand Historian: Annals of Xiang Yu*: “Chen Ying was ever trustworthy and prudent, known as an elder.” *Elder* here denotes one of loyalty and generosity), delighting in charity and regarded by his village as a virtuous man. The scholar thought, “This man may well be worthy of the site,” and fully disclosed it to him. Squire Xiao acquired it at a high price, and the scholar marked the burial spot and opened the grave pit.

When the day of interment arrived, the scholar said to Squire Xiao, “This land cannot bear one without profound virtue. Though you are indeed an elder, the will of Heaven remains unknowable. To defy it would invite great misfortune. You ought to spend the night in the grave pit to divine its suitability—if this is not your destined land, there will be an ominous sign.” The squire followed his advice.

That night, the squire took his son and lodged together in the grave pit, covering themselves with a reed mat. At midnight, they heard the sound of *he-dian* (the shouts of attendants clearing the way). Peering through a gap in the reeds, they saw an escort of ceremonial guards—banners, weapons, and retainers—ushering forward a towering man on horseback. The squire thought to himself, “How could a man of high rank pass through these wild mountains at such an hour?” Just as his confusion peaked, the rider reined in his horse beside the pit and rebuked his retinue: “Whose filial son’s burial ground is this? How dare Xiao presume to seize it? Seize him at once and cast him out!” Trembling, the squire kowtowed within the pit and cried aloud, “I feared this site was not my due and might incur Heaven’s punishment, so I spent the night here to divine its fate. Now that you have deigned to instruct me, I shall at once relinquish it.” Then they heard the man on horseback say, “In consideration of your long-standing virtue, I shall pardon you. If you are willing to bury this filial son’s parent here, I shall grant you another auspicious site in its stead.”

The rider added, “This grave must be sealed at once—do not let the qi escape.” With these words, he vanished like the wind or a flash of lightning, and in the blink of an eye, all fell silent. At daybreak, father and son returned home and told the scholar what had transpired. They sealed the grave and together sought out the filial son, but none could identify him. One day, the scholar wandered alone beyond the city. Having ventured some distance, he reached a market town just as a sudden downpour forced him to take shelter beneath the eaves of a rice shop. As dusk approached, the huskers had all ceased their work, save for one young man who lingered. Struck by this, the scholar inquired, and the youth, still pounding the grain, replied, “My mother is old and will not eat unless there is meat. I rise early and rest late so I may earn more husking wages to provide for her.”

The scholar asked his surname, and the youth replied, "He." The scholar thought to himself, *Could this be the filial son named He?* He wished to observe whether the young man truly treated his mother with devotion. Waiting until the youth finished his husking, he claimed the rain had made the road too long and requested lodging for the night. He agreed.

The scholar took out five silver coins and handed them to He, saying, "Prepare an evening meal for me." He was astonished and said, "The cost of a single meal—why so much money?" The scholar replied, "If there is any left over, use it to provide for your mother’s table." He refused, saying, "I do my utmost to serve my mother, and that brings me peace. To accept so much from a guest without earning it would be wrong." He insisted on taking only one coin, with which he purchased wine and dried meat for his guest.

Returning together to his home, the house consisted of only two bays. Within was his mother’s chamber; He and his wife dwelt outside, partitioning the front half for the hearth. Though cramped and low-lying, it was kept quite clean. He entered first to inform his mother of the guest seeking lodging. She promptly called for his wife to brew tea and cautioned her not to slight the visitor. He soon returned, ushering the scholar inside, saying, “This poor home has no spare room. I have already instructed my wife to sleep with my mother, so you and I must share a bed—pray do not mind the intimacy.”

Once seated, he again went to fetch tea, then brought out a single dish of wine and one of food, setting them on the table with the words, “Forgive me for not keeping you company.” He then hurried inside. The scholar peered through the door’s gap and saw on the table two bowls, each with its own spoon and chopsticks. He and his wife together helped their mother to her proper place. As the mother ate, the couple attended her on either side, stirring the broth and offering meat, their faces suffused with gentle contentment. When she had eaten her fill, the wife cleared the dishes, and He personally assisted his mother in washing her hands. Only then did they sit down to eat themselves, their meal consisting of nothing more than a small portion of pickled greens.

The scholar ate while watching through the gap, his admiration growing ever deeper. Before long, He emerged to find his guest had finished his meal, then brought tea once more, saying to the scholar, “The bedding and pillow are all prepared. You have traveled far and must be weary—please retire first and do not wait for me.” The scholar nodded in assent. He then went back inside, and the scholar again peered in. He saw He sitting beside his mother, recounting in detail the vulgar and commonplace happenings of the streets and markets to amuse her, her expression full of delight. Soon, she began to yawn and stretch, showing signs of fatigue, so he personally helped her settle onto the pillow, smoothed the mat, and assisted her in removing her outer garments before she relieved herself. His wife stood by her side, her own face showing not the slightest weariness.

When his mother had fallen asleep, He proceeded to knead and massage her—gently striking her back and legs, then soothing the places that ached or itched. Only when he heard her breathing deepen did he rise, stepping as lightly as if afraid to wake her. The scholar, moved by such sincere filial devotion, recalled the immortal’s words had not been false.

Once He emerged, the scholar asked how many years had passed since his father’s death and whether he had been interred. He replied in tears, “It has been four years. This unworthy son, working as a laborer to support my mother, has had no means to arrange a proper burial. To this day, his remains lie in temporary shelter at the village shrine—just speaking of it breaks my heart.” Seeing him overcome with grief, the scholar consoled him, “Do not grieve. My host, Old Man Xiao, owns a small plot of auspicious land, which I shall beg on your behalf. I will also contribute toward your burial expenses.”

He exclaimed in astonishment, “I have never met you before, sir—how could I presume upon such generosity? Moreover, since the land already has an owner, even if you take pity on me, I fear your words will be of no avail.” The scholar replied, “Have no concern. I have long known Old Man Xiao to be magnanimous and fond of bestowing kindness, delighting in bringing others’ good deeds to fruition. When he hears of your filial devotion, he will surely consent. Three days hence, do not go out. I shall come with him.” He wept in gratitude, saying, “If your words prove true, I shall be indebted to your great virtue for the rest of my days.” The scholar once more offered him comfort.

That night, they retired to rest. Before dawn, the scholar awoke to find He gone without a trace. When morning came, he saw him returning from outside with a bowl in hand. Upon inquiry, he learned that his mother had craved sweet dumplings in broth, so he had set out for the city at the fourth watch, making a forty-li round trip. The scholar’s admiration deepened.

Upon returning home, he told Old Man Xiao, who rejoiced, saying, “The immortal’s decree has come to pass! Now that we have found such a man, how could I dare be stingy?” Three days later, they set out together, bearing the land deed. As they neared the door, they heard He and his wife wailing in bitter grief. Startled, they entered to ask what had happened. They learned that after the scholar’s departure, He’s mother had suddenly fallen ill with an epidemic disease. With no means to secure medicine, she had died abruptly the previous evening. Seeing his guests arrive, He struck his head against the ground in anguish, weeping uncontrollably. Moved by compassion, Old Man Xiao provided funds for her coffin and burial, then presented him with the land deed.

The scholar selected an auspicious day for the burial, covering all expenses himself. After the interment, He and his wife came together to express their gratitude and offered to work as servants to repay the value of the land. Old Man Xiao, astonished, said, “Your filial piety has moved Heaven, and you have received divine protection. How could I dare claim credit for Heaven’s work?” He then recounted the earlier events and added, “You are a filial son—how could I seek your friendship and fail, yet presume to reduce you to servitude? My home has many vacant rooms. If you do not disdain them, why not bring your family to live with us? I will ensure you need not worry about daily provisions.” He declined, insisting he was unworthy, but Old Man Xiao firmly insisted, and so He remained in his household, overseeing its accounts and comings and goings.

A month later, Old Man Xiao said to the scholar, “At first, the immortal promised me that after the filial son buried his parent, he would grant me an auspicious site. Now his words are about to be fulfilled—why not seek it?” The scholar replied, “Very well. I am no geomancer who feigns skill for a living. Were it not for the unfinished matter with you, why would I linger in a foreign land? Since the immortal has already promised you, there must be an auspicious site. Yet none has met my expectations thus far. I ask that you grant me more time.” From then on, he daily roamed the fields, valleys, and mountains, searching for burial caves and dragon veins, but found nothing. After more than a month of this, his body and spirit were utterly exhausted.

One day, as he passed by He’s grave, he lingered, gazing into the distance, when suddenly he glimpsed a dragon vein faintly emerging several *zhang* away. Following its trace, he discovered a true dragon vein, for it shared the same source as He’s grave. After careful examination, he saw that while its nobility was slightly inferior, its wealth could reach a million. He reported this to Old Man Xiao, who purchased the land, and the scholar selected an auspicious day for the burial.

Once the matter was concluded, the scholar prepared to return home. Old Man Xiao offered him a thousand gold pieces in gratitude, but the scholar firmly declined, saying, “I told you before that I am no geomancer seeking a livelihood. I ask that you keep this to aid the poor.” Unable to refuse further, Old Man Xiao arranged a farewell feast with music. He and his wife also came, prostrating themselves in tearful gratitude.

The scholar returned home and promptly passed the imperial examinations, becoming a *jinshi*. After Old Man Xiao buried his parent, his family’s fortunes rose daily, growing so prosperous that they became the wealthiest in the prefecture. Before long, his son entered the Hanlin Academy as a *jinshi* and later rose to the rank of *Fangbo*. As for the filial son’s grandson, He Wen’an, Lord Linghan, he placed third in the *yichou* imperial examination and served as *Dazongbo*, earning renown as a Neo-Confucian statesman of his time. His son, Shaoji, again topped the provincial examination in the *yiwei* year, passed the metropolitan examination, and entered the Hanlin Academy, repeatedly holding positions overseeing literary affairs. The prosperity and eminence of both families showed no sign of abating.

The Master of Sitting-Among-Flowers remarked: “Filial piety stands first among the Five Constants and is the wellspring of all conduct. When He the Filial Son was mingled among hired laborers—no extraordinary talent or uncommon integrity to stir the world’s notice—yet Heaven and Earth protected him, spirits revered him; in the end his gate and courtyard shone with splendor, blessings flowing to his descendants. How wondrously swift and divine is the response to the power of filial devotion! This scholar valued righteousness over profit, and Old Man Xiao was sincere, kind-hearted, and delighted in goodness—rightly did both receive abundant fortune!”

Two Affairs of the Venerable Father Wan Yanzhai
*He scorned reward-silver and the theft of fame,*
*Yet cherished kin with all a man’s true feeling.*
*Who aids the needy while his wife stays meek—*
*Such is the Way the azure vault refines.*

**I**
In Yangxian, the Venerable Father of Provincial Governor Wan Limen—Master Yanzhai—was a *xiucai* in his youth. Though his family was very poor, he was upright and never took what was not his, yet bold in doing what was right. When others faced urgent hardship, he would go to great lengths to aid and comfort them, even if it meant sullying his own reputation. Once, while serving as a private tutor in the countryside, he set out on foot at year’s end to return home. Midway, he encountered a middle-aged woman walking and weeping. Struck by her distress, he asked what was wrong, but she remained silent. Pressing her, she snapped: “Wayfarers each have their own cares—why trouble strangers with endless talk?” Seeing her words sharp but her expression sorrowful, he reassured her: “I do not ask idly. If you are in dire straits, tell me; perhaps I can help.” The woman then said: “My husband is the village headman. He has fallen short of thirty-odd taels of official silver, and now lies imprisoned, daily subjected to the rod and cudgel as they demand repayment.”

Upon visiting her husband, he told her he was exhausted from the beat

After walking several paces, he turned back and said to her, "Come early tomorrow—do not delay yourself." The woman, weeping, replied, "Yes." Then she took the silver and left. When the gentleman returned home, breakfast was not yet prepared. His wife asked for the payment to buy rice, but he hesitated, stammering, "I’ve wasted a year’s hard work. The mountain paths were treacherous, and I nearly fell several times—how could I have kept hold of the silver pouch?" His wife, knowing his habit of aiding those in distress, smiled faintly and said, "If you truly lost it in a fall, it might still be found. I fear you’ve once again played the fool who jumps into the well to save another!" The gentleman replied, "Indeed."

Still short of the sum, what was to be done?" He told her the truth, and his wife, ever virtuous and kind, uttered no word of reproach. Instead, she encouraged him, saying, "This is a great and good deed. Yet the year is nearly over, and where are we to find twenty taels of silver? Still, since you have already promised, you cannot turn back now. We can pawn our household goods to get through the New Year—go quickly and see to her needs, and do not worry about us here at home!" The gentleman left in high spirits, borrowing from relatives and friends to raise over ten taels, though still short of the amount.

In the town there was a usurer who lent money only against collateral, refusing even close kin a single coin without it. With the matter now urgent and no other recourse, he realized that in his haste there was little of value left to pledge. As the keeper of the ancestral hall’s keys, he took the tables, chairs, doors, and windows from the shrine and pawned them to the usurer for ten taels. Under cover of night, he had them carried to the man’s house. None of the clansmen knew of this. The next day, he took the silver to the woman, who had already arrived at the appointed place.

He handed the silver to her and hurried home, but the woman secretly followed him. She saw the gentleman speaking with someone. Upon inquiring, she learned his name and address. Soon after, she arrived at his home with her husband and daughter, bowing deeply to the gentleman and his wife in gratitude. They begged to leave their daughter as a servant, but the gentleman, seeing the girl was not yet ten years old and quite lovely, said to them: "This is a fine girl—she should be matched with a good husband. Take her home quickly, lest you lead others into wrongdoing." He firmly refused their offer, and they left after repeated bows.

When the clansmen entered the ancestral hall on New Year’s Day to offer sacrifices, they found it utterly empty. All were shocked, suspecting theft. The old man hastily explained to the assembly: "At year’s end, with no funds, I temporarily pawned these things for money. I beg you to wait until I receive my salary to redeem them." The clansmen were furious and all rose to reproach him.

The gentleman remained silent, offering no defense—his face showed neither shame nor anger. At that moment, an elder clansman, who had long known the gentleman to be a man of integrity and one who aided others in distress, suspected there must be another reason. He urged the assembly to return home for the time being and reconvene in three days, and so the crowd dispersed. The elder alone visited the gentleman’s home and discreetly questioned his wife. Upon learning the truth, he rejoiced and said, “This is also a great act of kindness. He will surely pass the examinations this autumn. Yet for having taken the ancestral hall’s items without permission, he must not go unpunished, lest others follow his example.”

He then gathered the clansmen and revealed the reason for the pawning. He declared, “It is fitting that he be temporarily expelled from the ancestral hall. The autumn examinations draw near.”

awaiting the golden announcement of his success, whereupon he would be permitted to rejoin. The assembly assented.

The gentleman remained untroubled, giving no thought to the matter. That autumn, he indeed passed the examinations and returned home to preside over the *Deer Cry Banquet*. The clan elder hosted a feast in celebration. Later, he was appointed principal of Tongzhou Academy. He fathered two sons, the younger of whom became Prefect Li of Lizhou. People regarded this as the reward for his kindness in aiding others in distress.

**II**

After passing the provincial examination, Master Wan Yanzhai set out for the capital with his fellow candidates. They arrived at a certain county in Shandong.

Master Wan remained resolute. "To abandon a sick man in an inn without anyone to attend to his medicine is to hasten his death," he declared. "This journey of mine is merely to accompany the provincial candidates to the capital—my sole purpose is to gaze upon the imperial city, not to pursue fame or rank. You may all proceed; I shall stay and await his recovery."

When Lu heard this, he grew uneasy and urged Master Wan to go with the others, but Wan stood firm. Alone, he sought out physicians, prepared decoctions, and kept vigil morning and evening for twenty days. Yet Lu’s illness proved fatal. As he lay dying, he clasped Wan’s hand and said, "Though we hail from the same village, we were never close. Yet your kindness to me surpasses the depths of the sea. This life is at its end, but if there is rebirth, I shall beg the underworld judges to let me be your son, that I may repay this great debt." With these words, he passed away.

By then, the two had exhausted their travel funds. Wan pawned his robes and furs to pay for the funeral, then considered Lu’s impoverished family and young children. If he did not take this opportunity to escort the coffin home, he feared it might never return. So he left the coffin at the inn and set out on foot, begging for food as he made his way to the capital. When he finally reached the lodgings of his fellow candidates, the examination period had already ended. They were surprised by his tardiness and, seeing his tattered clothes, worn shoes, and sun-blackened face, took pity on him. Wan recounted all that had transpired since their parting and entreated them to help return Lu’s coffin to his homeland, saying, "I hope you will remember our shared journey to the capital and grant this final act of kindness, that he may rest in his native earth. If his spirit knows, he will surely find a way to repay your generosity."

Moved by his righteousness, the group eagerly opened their purses to assist.

The matter was again discussed in Changzhou Prefecture, and those who had traveled to the capital together with him collected several dozen taels of silver to give to Wan. On the day he departed the capital, he resumed his journey on foot, begging for food as before, and did not recklessly spend a single coin. Upon reaching the inn, he laid out the silver before the coffin and called out to Lu, saying, “Through the kindness of so-and-so, I have received this sum of money, which will allow me to escort you home. If you are aware, why not accompany me on the journey?” He then carried the coffin back with him. Upon arriving home, he still had over ten taels remaining, which he gave to Lu’s wife and children.

Several years later, on a summer day, Wan happened to be strolling along the city wall at dusk with his friends Dai and Zhu, his own home lying just below. Suddenly, they saw a man hurrying along with a fan in hand, entering Wan’s house directly. Dai pointed and said to Wan, “Is that not Lu Yining?” Wan looked and indeed recognized him, forgetting for a moment that he had already died. He called out hastily, but Zhu, who arrived later, heard this and exclaimed in shock, “Lu has been dead for years—how could he come here?” Wan then realized his mistake and looked again carefully. The figure had already entered his gate. He quickly descended the city wall with his two friends to trace his footsteps.

Just as they reached the gate, a servant came out to report that the mistress was about to give birth. Wan hurried home to see her, and the child had already been delivered—his appearance strikingly resembled Lu’s. Only then did Wan suddenly realize that the figure he had seen on the city wall was indeed Lu. For Lu had come to fulfill his vow and be reborn, manifesting his form to demonstrate the unerring nature of cause and effect!

As the child grew, he proved exceptionally intelligent. He soon passed the imperial examinations, entered the Ministry of Rites as an observer, and later became Prefect Lizhen of Lizhou. The *Master of Sitting-Among-Flowers* remarked:

"Only true heroes of surpassing talent can act beyond conventional bounds. How could the deeds of Master Yanzhai be matched by those who cling to petty integrity and cautious restraint? To seek fame is ultimately shameful, yet he felt no disgrace; to attain the honor of *jinshi* is the height of glory, yet he did not rush toward it. Instead, he devoted himself tirelessly to relieving disasters and aiding those in distress—was this not the courage of a truly benevolent man?"

And the Creator, in turn, repaid him according to his measure. Now, the heart of compassion is innate in all, yet世 people often excuse themselves, saying, “My strength is insufficient.” Witnessing the deeds of Master Yanzhai, might they not be stirred to emulation?

---

**III. Ten Taels for a Life**

*The diviner’s precision, the physiognomist’s marvel—*
*Who knew of the life saved at Jiangjin?*
*The *jieyuan* was but a ghost beneath crumbling walls,*
*For fortune and misfortune have no gate—one invites them oneself.*

A certain eminent scholar in his youth suffered extreme poverty, often going days without kindling a fire. In the year he sat for the provincial examination, a diviner in his village—renowned for his uncanny accuracy—declared that before the White Dew festival, he would die an unnatural death. The scholar was deeply troubled. As the examination date neared, several classmates urged him to join them, but he demurred, citing the diviner’s warning and pleading lack of funds. Among them was a certain Mr. Wang, wealthy and of noble character, who had long been on good terms with the scholar. He insisted on the journey, saying, “Why heed such empty words? If you fear destitution, let me take care of it.” With that, he pressed ten taels of silver into the scholar’s hand: “Use this for your family’s needs. For travel expenses, you may ask freely—I will provide.” Moved by his generosity, the scholar joined the group. They set out several days after the Beginning of Autumn.

Upon reaching Jinling, they heard of a physiognomist at Cheng’en Temple whose judgments were often strangely accurate. His chamber was as crowded as a marketplace. The scholar and his six fellow lodgers went together. The physiognomist surveyed them all: one was a *lin* scholar, another a *zeng*, a *fu*, a *jian*; some enjoyed the blessing of both parents, others mourned their loss—his assessments were unerring in every case.

Among them, only one was said to have a chance at securing a *fujia* position in the current examination, while the rest were all deemed unlikely to pass. When it came to the scholar, the physiognomist first asked which county he hailed from and how far it was from there. Then, counting on his fingers, he exclaimed, “Hurry—you may still make it in time!”

Perplexed, the scholar pressed for an explanation. The physiognomist replied, “Your complexion is withered, your spirit adrift, and dark lines have already appeared on your *tian ting*—by fate, you are to die an unnatural death within five days. You must return at once, though even then, your face bears the marks of a death on the road, and I fear you may not arrive in time.”

Master Wang and the others were stunned. “Sir,” they urged, “please examine him again—might there be some saving grace in his stars?”

The physiognomist shook his head. “Life and death are governed by great destiny; only immense hidden virtue could alter heaven’s decree. The appointed hour is nearly upon him—what can be done? If this gentleman is still alive six days from now, I shall never read fortunes again!”

Silently, the group returned to their lodgings. The scholar turned to Master Wang: “At first, you insisted I come, and now the physiognomist’s words echo those from before—there must be truth to them. Death comes to all; it is not death itself I fear. But to die here would burden you all greatly. I should leave at once, hoping to breathe my last at home.”

The others agreed.

Master Wang, moved by compassion, arranged for a boat and prepared travel provisions. He also gave the scholar ten taels of silver, saying, “Keep this for any urgent needs.” The scholar, understanding his intent, smiled and declined, “You have already helped with my funeral expenses; I dare not refuse. If there is awareness after death, I shall beseech the underworld authorities to grant you high success in the examinations, that I may repay your great kindness.” With that, he bid the others farewell and boarded the vessel.

After sailing more than ten *li* down the river, the wind grew fierce, and the boat could not proceed. They moored and waited idly, like the farmer who abandoned his plow to watch the tree stump. In the blink of an eye, four days passed. The wind raged even more violently, and the scholar thought silently, *The fifth day’s deadline is nearly upon me, yet the boat cannot depart. The physiognomist’s words of dying on the road seem about to come true.* At that moment, his mind was fixed on death, all other concerns dissolved, and only the agony of loneliness remained, with no means of distraction. He disembarked to wander aimlessly along the shore.

After meandering for over a *li*, he saw no sign of human habitation. Suddenly, he noticed a pregnant woman of middle age, carrying three young children—one in her arms, the others clutched in her hands—weeping as she walked, as if overcome with sorrow. They passed each other like arms crossing in opposite directions, and she had already gone several paces ahead when the scholar suddenly thought, *The riverbank is desolate, with no dwellings in sight. Where can this woman be going?* Her appearance struck him as suspicious. He hurried after her, calling out, but she did not respond. He followed at a distance, and when she cursed and insulted him, he pressed her even more earnestly, saying, “If you are in dire straits, I beg you to tell me. Perhaps I can help in some way.”

Unable to refuse, the woman finally confessed: “I was unlucky enough to marry a butcher, a man of violent temper. He beats me constantly, leaving no part of my body unmarked. Today, he went to the market, leaving two pigs at home. He told me, ‘Sell them only for ten taels.’ Soon after, a man came to buy them, and indeed, he offered ten taels. Fearing the silver was counterfeit, we went together to a silversmith to verify its purity, and it proved genuine. When we returned, the buyer suddenly claimed the price was too high and took back his silver. A moment later, he returned, still wishing to exchange the original silver for the pigs. Seeing no difference in the silver, I suspected nothing and gave him the pigs. Then a neighbor from the west came by, saw the silver, and exclaimed in surprise, ‘This is copper!’ I rushed after the man, but he had vanished without a trace. I took the silver to the silversmith again, and it was copper. I went to several other shops, and all confirmed it was copper. Trapped in this swindle, I know my husband will beat me to death when he returns. If death is inevitable, better to die in the water than by his whip. These three children are my own; I will take them with me to drown, so they will not suffer their father’s abuse.”

The scholar, moved by her plight, summoned the copper and examined it—indeed, it was copper. Just then, the gold Wang had given him happened to be in his sleeve. Thinking of his impending death, he mused, *What use is gold to me now?* So he secretly exchanged it for the copper and said to the woman, “You nearly died in vain. This is true silver—why call it copper?”

The woman flared with anger. “Every silversmith I went to said it was copper! Why would you deceive me?”

“Not so!” he replied. “Those silversmiths took advantage of you as a woman. Come with me, and they will not dare call it copper again. If it is truly copper, you may still choose death later.”

She followed his advice, and together they walked three or four *li* before reaching the silversmith’s shop. The scholar produced the silver, and the smith declared it genuine. They visited several more shops, and all confirmed it was silver. Overjoyed, the woman exclaimed, “Fortune favored me in meeting you, or I would have perished in error!” She took the silver, bowed in gratitude, and departed.

The scholar hurried back to his boat, but by then the sun had set, and dusk had deepened into twilight. Before he had gone a *li*, he lost his way, with no one to ask for directions. As he wandered hesitantly, he suddenly saw a cluster of buildings ahead. Drawing nearer, he saw crumbling doors and ruined walls—it was an abandoned temple. With no other choice, he sheltered beneath its eaves to await dawn.

As he sat in the desolate night, he thought, *If a fox spirit or ghost should seize me, this will be my deathbed.* Yet exhaustion overtook him, and he soon fell asleep. In his half-dreaming state, he heard voices shouting to clear the way. Looking up, he saw the temple hall ablaze with light, attendants standing in solemn rows, and a king seated at a table—dimly, he recognized the figure as Lord Guan. Suddenly, he heard the deity say, “Today, by the river, someone saved five lives. We must examine this person and grant them due reward.”

A purple-robed official stepped forward, holding a document. “The local earth god has just reported: it was a scholar from such-and-such a county.”

Lord Guan ordered the register of fortunes checked, along with the autumn examination roster. Another official in embroidered robes presented a ledger. “This man’s fate is spent; he is destined to die tonight at the hour of *zi*, crushed beneath the temple’s collapsing wall.”

“How can we encourage goodness if this is so?” the deity mused. “His fate must be rewritten. Yesterday, the Wenchang Palace informed us that the *jieyuan* of this year’s Jiangnan examinations was stripped of his title for defiling a maiden. Let this scholar take his place.”

Someone else spoke: “The gold he used came from Wang, who gave it freely in righteousness. Since this act allowed the scholar to achieve merit, we should trace its source and record Wang as well.”

“Well said,” Lord Guan replied. He ordered the ledger checked: Wang was to rank fifty-third in the middle-level examinations of a future year. The embroidered-robed official stepped forward. “The current fifty-third candidate is to be penalized for slander and deferred for one examination. The replacement has not yet been decided by the Wenchang Palace. Let Wang take his place.”

As the scholar listened intently, a sudden shout near his ear cried, *“Go! Go!”* Startled awake, he found himself still crouched beneath the eaves. All around was darkness, nothing to be seen—only the sound of crumbling plaster pattering from the wall. He staggered to his feet and fled.

Just as he hurried out, the wall suddenly collapsed, crashing down precisely where he had been sitting. Thus, he stood upright and waited until dawn. When day broke, he ascended the hall to gaze upward—sure enough, it was the Temple of Lord Guan. He bowed in reverence and departed, then sought his way back to the boat. Silently pondering the deity’s words, he felt certain they would prove true. He consulted the boatman and returned to Jinling. With a fair wind filling the sails, they arrived in no time.

Upon reaching his lodging, the others were astonished to see him. The senior scholar merely explained that the wind had prevented their journey, and since the five-day deadline had passed, he had returned. The group asked, “Did you encounter any ill omens during those five days?” The senior scholar fabricated a reason: “There was an incident. Yesterday, I strolled along the riverbank and wandered too far from the boat. By the time I returned, dusk had fallen. The reeds grew thick and tangled, and I stumbled twice, nearly falling into the river. Fortunately, the boatman came with a lantern to search for me, and I made it back—but the silver pouch in my sleeve had vanished, as if turned into *qingfu* and flown away!”

Wang laughed and said, “To escape great peril is to be destined for great fortune. Stumbling along the riverbank—could this be an omen of passing the imperial examination?”

“As we had prepared wine to celebrate, the next day, all those lodging with us remarked that seven days had passed and you remained unharmed—why not go and mock the physiognomer for his falsehoods?” The senior scholar was reluctant, but the group insisted, and so they went. Upon arrival, they found the physiognomer seated amidst a bustling crowd. Pushing through, they brought the senior scholar to stand before him. The physiognomer was in the midst of animated discourse when he lifted his gaze, saw the senior scholar, and exclaimed in surprise, “Are you not the one I said would die within five days?” The group answered in unison, “Indeed. Now seven days have passed—what say you to that?” The physiognomer replied, “You will not die now. In just these few days, your bone structure has transformed entirely, and your complexion has improved remarkably. You must have performed some extraordinary act of kindness, saving several lives—thus altering your fate.” The senior scholar laughed and said, “How absurd, sir! With my poverty, how could I save anyone?” The physiognomer replied, “Do not deceive me. I had previously said that only a great act of hidden virtue could reverse heaven’s decree. Now, your face bears the marks of profound *yin zhi*. This year, you will surely top the imperial examination; next year, you will pass the palace exam and enter the Hanlin Academy. You will rise to the rank of first-grade official and live eighty years more.” He added with a smile, “This is no coincidence. Half a month ago, I read the face of a scholar, whose *mingtang* shone with extraordinary brilliance, and I declared him certain to be this year’s top candidate. But when I saw him again yesterday, a *suspended needle* had appeared on his forehead—his radiance had vanished entirely. He must have committed some grave transgression, forfeiting his place in the register of fortune. Unexpectedly, you have taken his place.” Then, pointing to Wang, he said, “Your face also bears the marks of *yin zhi*—you will pass the examination alongside this gentleman.” Wang laughed and said, “I know nothing of my friend’s deeds, but as for myself—what good have I done?” The physiognomer replied, “It is precisely because you act without seeking reward that it is called *yin zhi*.” The crowd rose up, mocking his evasive words. The senior scholar smiled and said, “Foolish words met with foolish belief—why all this clamor? We would do better to return and rest.”

Upon returning, the senior scholar quietly told Wang, “That man was truly a divine physiognomer! His words were not false—you are destined to place fifty-third.” Earlier, when the senior scholar had first returned, Wang had noticed his radiant countenance and felt something was amiss. Upon hearing the physiognomer’s prediction, he suspected the scholar had used the money Wang had given him to aid someone in need, then fabricated a story about losing it. Thus, he questioned him closely about the details. The senior scholar recounted everything and added, “Had you not given me those ten taels of silver, I would have witnessed his death with my own eyes. Today, I owe my undeserved divine favor entirely to your kindness.” Wang exclaimed in astonishment, “You possessed such magnanimity! If this is true, then I should be the one indebted to you. Yet how could that physiognomer be so divine!”

That examination, the senior scholar indeed passed as the top candidate, and Wang also succeeded. The following year, both entered the Hanlin Academy.

The Master of Sitting-among-Flowers said: “My close friend, Elder Jiang Yiting, recounted this tale to me and sighed, saying, ‘People often claim that poverty or prosperity, longevity or early death, are all bound to fate alone.’”

Yet how could one know that the Creator’s rewards and retributions are entirely determined by what one brings upon oneself? Consider the trifling sum of ten taels: the spirits must trace back to its source and bestow blessings in return. In the end, the licentious, though selected for the cinnamon palace, were swiftly stripped of their rank; the virtuous, though their names were soon to be entered in the ledgers of the dead, suddenly gained emolument and longevity. Thus, poverty and prosperity are reversed in an instant, and the span of life and death is altered in a moment. Even the friend who valued righteousness over wealth and practiced generosity received the recompense of his good deeds. For "misfortune and fortune have no gate; they are summoned only by oneself." From this, one’s conviction should grow ever firmer: can one not strive to do good? Can one not guard against evil?

"IV. In My Remaining Years, Stripped of Rank—
A Star Lord Descends with Banners and Streamers,
Yet Two Affairs Deceived a Widow and Cost Me the Title of Provincial Graduate

Repeatedly accumulating karmic entanglements, repeatedly stripped of rank,
A full-cheeked, square-jawed man bearing a noble countenance—

In my remaining years, I was a native of Yin County in Zhejiang. At my birth, an extraordinary fragrance filled the room, and rosy clouds illuminated the sky. That very night, my maternal grandfather, an elder named So-and-so, dreamed he entered the Palace of Wenchang, where he saw the Emperor Lord personally escorting a towering man out, with banners leading the way and ceremonial flags and standards following in his train, the majesty and splendor most grand. Beside them, a green-robed clerk holding a register glanced at my grandfather and said, 'This is a certain Star Lord descending to the mortal realm—he is your grandson.' Thereupon, he raised the yellow register in his hands to show my grandfather. Flipping to the first page, he saw written in large characters a certain name, followed by a list of official ranks and merits. The fine script filled page after page without end: at nine, he would enter the Confucian school; at seventeen, he would attain the title of Provincial Graduate; soon after, he would pass the metropolitan examination and place first in the palace examination; he would ascend to eminent and honorable positions, serving in both the capital and the provinces, rising to the rank of Grand Tutor, ennobled as a Duke of the Highest Order, his civil and military achievements resplendent and illustrious."

As he had not yet finished reading, he was awakened by a family member—his son-in-law’s household had sent word of a son’s birth! At daybreak he went to see the child, and as each recounted their strange omens, all were astonished and delighted, divining him to be a vessel of state. From infancy the boy was precocious and remarkable. As he grew older, his intelligence surpassed all others; when he read, ten lines met his eye at once. At seven he could compose essays, and at nine he entered the county school, earning the reputation of a prodigy. It so happened that his maternal uncle, a distinguished metropolitan graduate, had been appointed magistrate in eastern Guangdong. He took the boy with him, and after several years in Guangdong, the boy turned seventeen. His learning was vast, his talent abundant, and all who met him were filled with admiration. His uncle’s two sons studied alongside him, yet they fell far short of his abilities. That year, the autumn examination approached, and his uncle, considering his nephews’ studies incomplete, planned to wait for the next examination. He wavered over whether to send the boy home with his cousins. One night, he dreamed that the Martial Sage summoned him and declared, “If this young man does not return, Zhejiang Province will have no top scholar—send him back at once.”

Upon waking, his uncle immediately prepared the boy’s luggage and urged him to depart, presenting him with a generous sum of gold. The young man, ever confident in his own abilities, had a fondness for pleasure and dissipation, and along the way, he squandered his gold like dirt. No sooner had he crossed Mount Meiling than his purse was already empty. Passing through a certain county in Jiangxi, he came upon a magistrate who was his uncle’s fellow graduate and had long admired the young man. The young man paid him a visit, and the magistrate received him with the utmost favor and generosity, arranging lodging for him outside the yamen. The host of the lodging, however, was a man of long-standing cunning. In the county lived a wealthy widow who, though pregnant, had not yet given birth when her husband died. Her clansmen coveted her wealth and falsely accused her of adultery, bringing the case before the magistrate. The lodging host had in fact instigated the scheme, but thanks to the magistrate’s discernment, the clansmen’s plot nearly failed. Seeing that the young man was a favored guest of the magistrate, they offered him a substantial bribe, entreating him to intercede on their behalf. The young man, now in dire financial straits, embellished his words and reported to the magistrate, saying, “The woman’s scandalous reputation is widely known throughout the county. Yet when Your Honor first examined the case, you strove to protect her, and now people suspect you accepted her gold and deliberately favored her. Though I am deeply honored by your kindness and know Your Honor to be upright and incorruptible, I could not bear to see you deceived by petty officials and tarnished with an unclean reputation. Thus, I venture to relay what I have heard.”

The magistrate, swayed by his words, immediately summoned both parties and completely overturned his earlier ruling. He decreed the woman’s full repudiation, and the magistrate’s clansmen established an heir for her. The clansmen then divided all her property among themselves and bestowed a thousand taels of silver upon the young man. As he departed, the widow returned home and hanged herself.

While passing through Quzhou, the prefect—a fellow townsman and former examination peer of his uncle—held the young man in high esteem. The young man paid his respects, and the prefect likewise lodged him in the guest quarters outside the yamen. In the prefecture, a wealthy widow had recently been left a young widow, pregnant with a posthumous child. Her clansmen falsely accused her of adopting the child and brought the case to the county court, where it remained unresolved. They then escalated the matter, accusing her of disrupting the clan lineage, and the prefect initially sided against them. The young man again accepted their bribes, embellished his words, and reported to the prefect. The prefect, once more deceived, ruled that the widow must abandon her child and allow a clansman’s son to inherit. Having secured two substantial bribes, the young man grew increasingly complacent. Yet when the examinations concluded, he failed once more. Returning home, his conduct grew ever more lawless.

He devoted himself entirely to litigation, his schemes growing ever more cunning and his written arguments ever more formidable. Like a mirage of sea markets and towering clam-spirits, he conjured illusions at will, bringing ruin to many. Yet in the examinations, he met repeated defeat, and at forty, he remained mired in the humble garb of a scholar. Eventually, his uncle retired and returned home. Upon learning of his nephew’s conduct, he flew into a rage and confined him to the house.

One night, the uncle dreamed he wandered into the City God’s temple. There, he saw two clerks seated beneath the corridor. One said, “For twenty years, I have repeatedly removed his name from the examination rolls—why?” The other smirked and replied, “This year, his name was struck again for such-and-such a matter. Fortunately, his uncle has taken responsibility for him in this affair, so he may yet live.” The uncle, astonished, bowed and inquired further.

The clerk handed him the register to examine. On the first page appeared the name of the young scholar, and the official honors listed matched what the uncle had seen in his dream. Yet the charges of misconduct were piled high, nearly exhausting all merit, leaving but a single examination attempt—only for that too to be struck from the record on the Clear and Bright Day for some offense, committed, it seemed, while sweeping his ancestors’ graves. His allotted lifespan, too, matched the clerk’s words. The uncle awoke in alarm and summoned the young man to his bedside, enumerating his misdeeds and recounting the dream. The scholar wept and confessed his guilt; from that day forward, he somewhat restrained his conduct. Over twenty years later, he ended his days still clad in the humble garb of a scholar.

When Master Jiang Yiting served as a guest instructor in Ningbo, he once met the man in person. His face was full, his brow broad, his jaw square, his beard long—hardly the appearance of one who had grown old in the scholar’s gown. The Master of the Flower Seat remarked:

“There have been those in the world endowed with talent surpassing the ages, for whom scaling the clouds seemed as effortless as plucking a mustard seed from the ground. Yet they spent their lives in the examination halls, never gaining even an inch of advancement, and one cannot but sigh at how rare true talent is—and how cruelly Heaven crushes and thwarts those it does bestow. But when we observe this young scholar: born with exceptional gifts, diligent in study from youth, had he but bound himself to self-discipline and restraint, would the words of the gods in his uncle’s dream have proven entirely groundless? The honors recorded in that register might yet have been his. Instead, he gave free rein to his tongue, wielding his words like a brush dipped in orpiment, reversing right and wrong at will; he filled his greedy purse at the expense of others’ reputations and moral standing.”

The end came with the stripping of his official rank and emoluments, leaving him to perish in the garb of a student—only then did one realize that Heaven does not spurn talent, but rather that men fail to wield their gifts with goodness. Alas, how perilous this is!

---

**V. The Scholar Wu**

*True kin indeed are only brother and brother-in-law—*
*Would they risk their lives to save his whole household?*
*Sealed in his chamber, he grieved the stolen primacy,*
*Yet owed a debt deeper than even his examination master’s grace.*

Wu of Yixing, a scholar renowned in the academy, sat for the examinations time and again, yet never succeeded. On New Year’s Eve, his brother-in-law dreamed of a placard posted at the county temple, announcing Wu as the next provincial graduate. Moments later, the name was struck away. Startled and perplexed, he heard an attendant beside him say, “He is to commit a grave transgression in the coming year, so his name was removed.” The brother-in-law asked, “Can this still be averted?” The attendant replied, “You must petition the master.” He was led inside to an audience.

The City God sat facing south. Prostrating himself, the brother-in-law pleaded on Wu’s behalf.

The deity cast down a ledger, upon which was inscribed that Wu was destined to become a Triple First Minister, but all had been erased due to the sins of his speech and writing. The record of the current examination matched the attendant’s words. The brother-in-law kowtowed and pleaded desperately, saying, “What is past is past, but future misfortune may yet be prevented. I vow on my entire family’s lives to ensure he does not commit this act.” The deity nodded in assent and ordered the attendant to lead him out—whereupon the placard hung as before.

Awakening, he sought to tell his wife, but she suddenly cried out as if in a nightmare. Rousing her, he asked the reason for her frantic shouting. She recounted seeing a messenger announce her brother as the provincial graduate, only for another to snatch it away, saying, “Wu’s name has been struck—do not report it in error.” Thus she had cried out in alarm. The brother-in-law then recounted his dream, and both were astonished. They deliberated how to handle Wu.

His wife said, “This is no difficulty. My brother is now widowed and childless, living alone in solitude. If we lure him to our home, confine him in an empty chamber, and explain the matter, we may admonish and safeguard him.”

The next morning, he dressed in formal attire and went to visit Wu. After exchanging New Year’s greetings, he deceived him, saying his sister had suddenly fallen ill and longed to see her brother, and thus pulled him home. Once Wu ascended the stairs and saw his sister in good health, he was about to ask the reason when she hastily led him into a secluded chamber, closed the door, and locked it. Through the door, she recounted the dream. Wu exclaimed in surprise, “The thought has indeed crossed my mind, but I have yet to act—how could the spirits and gods so swiftly mete out punishment!”

His sister and brother-in-law said, “All necessities are provided in the room. You may use this time to study diligently and must not leave.” When the examination period arrived, they finally opened the door. Uncle and nephew then traveled together to Jinling. Apart from entering the examination hall, they took not a single step without each other’s company. When the results were announced, Wu indeed placed first.

The Master of *Sitting Among Flowers* remarked: “To punish a man for a deed not yet committed may seem harsh, but I deem it an act of great kindness. Why? Had the deed been done, even if retribution were swift and manifest, what good would it serve? By intercepting his intent and thwarting it, they awakened him to caution, preventing him from reckless action. Thus, the man avoided the burden of evil, and Wu escaped the fate of being struck from the rolls—was this not a fortunate outcome?

Yet Wu was indeed lucky. In the darkest chamber, with a guilty conscience, the eyes of the gods are as piercing as lightning. I fear that in the vast expanse of the world, not all who slumber in the shadows of wrongdoing can be roused by the gods’ call to awakening.”

Six: The Retribution of Minister Qian Wencheng for Mass Slaughter

*In military affairs, nurturing Heaven’s harmony—*
*Yet "evil seeds must not remain" was too harsh a decree.*
*Behold how Cao Bin spared lives,*
*And Jiangnan’s later troubles were few indeed.*

In Changzhou, many were the great clans—such as Prime Minister Lü Gong, Chief Examiner Yang Tingyi, Minister Zhao Shenqiao, and Grand Coordinator Pan Siju—whose descendants achieved successive degrees in the imperial examinations, their literary legacy unbroken. Only the lineage of Minister Qian Wencheng Weicheng has withered most severely, now on the verge of extinction.

It is said that during the campaign to suppress the Miao rebels, Minister Qian, then serving as Minister of Justice and military advisor, joined the expedition. After the rebel chieftains were executed, the commanding general sought to slaughter all adult males while sparing the young and weak. Consulting Minister Qian, he declared resolutely: *"Evil seeds must not remain!"* Thus, all were put to death, not a single child left alive, and the Miao bloodline was extinguished. Soon after the army’s return, Minister Qian passed away, his son followed in death, and many of his grandsons were afflicted with disabilities. The Qian ancestral sacrifices ultimately ceased.

---

Seven: The Midwife’s Unyielding Chastity

*A midwife’s life was one of hardship,*
*Yet widowed young, she served with diligence.*
*Though her mother-in-law urged remarriage, she refused,*
*And in grief, cut off her ear—her purity unblemished.*

The City God of Shanghai was renowned for his divine efficacy. In the year *bingxu* of the Daoguang reign, the county undertook an inquiry into exemplary chastity and filial piety.

All those unable to afford individual commendation had their names collectively submitted to the authorities—whether the *Dao*, prefecture, subprefecture, or county—where a general memorial arch was erected. At the outset of the endeavor, a central bureau was established at Ruizhu Academy, overseen by several gentry. The assembly agreed that this undertaking bore directly upon public morals and brooked no personal bias. Thus, they invited the City God’s processional statue into the bureau, and all administrators swore oaths before the deity to ensure absolute impartiality.

One day, a chaste widow was reported. Upon inquiry into her background, it was revealed she was a midwife. Director Wang laughed and said, *"Set her aside! How could a midwife possibly preserve her chastity?"* The others concurred.

That night, Wang dreamed he was summoned by a servant in black to the county temple’s floral hall. There, he saw the City God seated in plain robes, who rebuked him sternly: *"Young fool! Discerning neither truth nor falsehood, you speak recklessly, slandering the virtuous. You deserve immediate punishment!"*

The deity, however, took pity on his thoughtless error and instructed, *"Go at once to the Confucian temple. There, you will find an elderly man holding a broom at the gate—he will tell you the full truth."* Then he warned, *"Be more cautious in future. Should you repeat such offenses, I shall not forgive you."* Wang kowtowed in submission.

The deity commanded the attendant spirit to lead him out. As he reached the gate, he awoke, immediately rose, and sat waiting for dawn. At first light, he hurried to the Confucian temple. There, he indeed saw an old man sweeping the ground—it was the temple’s caretaker. Wang inquired about the midwife’s chastity, and the old man started in surprise: *"Sir, you ask the right person—no one else would know. She is a woman of true integrity and bitter suffering. For forty years, I have lived next door to her. Her family has long practiced midwifery. She was fair of face, widowed young, and childless. Her parents-in-law urged her to remarry, but she swore she would not. As her mother-in-law grew too old to deliver babies, the widow took up the trade to support her in-laws, fulfilling her filial duty. Once, while attending a wealthy household, the master’s son coveted her beauty and tried to force himself upon her. She refused to yield, even at the cost of her life, and escaped by a stratagem. Later, the young man bribed her parents-in-law with gold, and they both pressed her to comply. In defiance, she cut off her ear and sent it to him—only then was she left in peace. I have been her neighbor since her youth, and in all these years, I have never seen her jest with any man. Such steadfast virtue—ought she not to be honored with an official commendation?"*

King Wang, having heard the full account, hastened to the bureau and recounted to his colleagues his nocturnal dream and the old man’s words, then inscribed the widow’s name in the register. That a woman of such chastity should be revered even by the gods—how can her virtue not be esteemed!

Master of the Flowery Seat observes: "Loyalty, filial piety, integrity, and chastity—these take root in the deepest nature. Heaven bestows them upon all without exception, unlike wealth, rank, or worldly success, which it may withhold. Yet Heaven values loyalty, filial piety, integrity, and chastity a hundredfold more than wealth, rank, or worldly success. Why? Because men, in their pursuit of wealth, rank, and worldly success, often abandon loyalty, filial piety, integrity, and chastity. Yet within the meanest hovels—where doors are woven of thorn and bramble, and walls are pierced for narrow side-passages—there arise deeds that even scholar-officials cannot match. Thus Heaven, finding such virtue rare and difficult, cannot but hold it in the highest regard.

That poor woman beneath her leaking eaves, who preserved her chastity through hardship—even spirits and gods hasten to honor her, to shame those who, though clad in robes and sashes, are no better than beasts of burden."

Yet to suppress such deeds with reckless slander and leave them unacknowledged—how fitting that the gods should hold such men to account!"

VIII. A Beggar’s Blessing
*Returning the Gold*

This beggar was fine timber indeed—
To win so much yet crave not a grain!
A jade found its buyer with joy unfeigned:
Wife, wealth, and children came all in train.

Jia Ayu was a native of Yangxian. In his youth, he begged for food in the village and often sheltered in a local earth-god shrine outside the city. One New Year’s Eve, as dusk fell, he noticed a roll of indigo cloth on the ground. Upon picking it up, he found a woman’s garment wrapped around a packet of silver—some thirty taels in all. Overjoyed, he then thought: *"I am but a beggar—what use have I for this? The one who lost it may yet come to harm in despair."* So he sat and waited for the owner to appear.

After some time, a middle-aged woman emerged from the city, wailing and searching the ground. Knowing her to be the rightful owner, he called out to her. Seeing only a beggar, she paid him no heed and wept all the more bitterly. Ayu said: *"What are you searching for? I have sat here long and may have seen it."* The woman then told him of the lost garment and silver, adding: *"My husband was imprisoned for unpaid taxes. I sold our daughter to raise the money—now it is gone, and I have nothing left but death."* Ayu asked about the color of the garment and the wrapping of the silver; all matched. He immediately produced them and returned them to her.

Overcome with gratitude, the woman offered him a portion of the silver in thanks, but he steadfastly refused. That night, as he lay in the shrine, he saw the hall ablaze with lamps and candles. *"Surely it is not yet dawn—why would worshippers come so early?"* Suddenly, he heard voices from the hall: *"Today, a beggar did not conceal lost gold but saved a couple from death. His merit is great—he deserves a blessing."* Another voice replied: *"This man is fated to remain a beggar and live only to thirty."* The first voice said: *"We should petition the Eastern Peak to amend his register of blessings and grant him a wife and children to encourage virtue."* Then came the command: *"First, bestow upon him a chest of silver."* At once, someone placed a large chest before Ayu, saying: *"The gods above have granted this to you."*

He awoke in ecstasy—only to find the shrine as dark as before. As he stretched and rose, his foot struck something with a metallic clang.

He picked it up to examine it and found six large coins tied with a cord. Chuckling to himself, he said, *"Could this truly be the gift from the gods?"* He tucked them into his robe. In Yangxian, it was the local custom that at New Year’s dawn, wealthy households would prepare cakes and sweets to distribute to beggars. Throughout the city and its outskirts, gambling stalls were plentiful. There were also sugar vendors who set out six dice on their carrying poles and wagered with passersby—those who won could claim sugar or coins as they pleased. These vendors always gathered on the Long Bridge.

The Long Bridge marked the spot where Zhou, the Filial Marquis of Jin, had slain the flood dragon. Zhou Xu, posthumously known as the Filial Marquis, was a native of Yangxian. Orphaned young, he possessed extraordinary strength and loved hunting, though he neglected proper conduct, much to the distress of his community. An elder once sighed, *"With the three scourges still unchecked, what is to be done?"* When Zhou asked what he meant, the elder replied, *"The dragon beneath the Long Bridge, the white-browed tiger of South Mountain, and you—these are the three scourges."* Zhou then entered the mountains to slay the tiger and plunged into the river to behead the dragon, thereafter mending his ways and cultivating integrity. Lu Shilong urged him to devote himself to learning. Later, he served as Palace Aide to the Censor-in-Chief and became renowned for his moral character.

At dawn the next day, Ayu begged and received a great many cakes. After eating his fill, he took six coins to the Long Bridge and gambled with the sugar vendors, winning every match. After besting ten or more vendors, he had won a thousand coins. He then went to the gambling den and won again. From morning till dusk, he amassed tens of thousands of coins. Nearby, an old merchant surnamed Jia ran a noodle shop beside the Long Bridge. Whenever Ayu begged for food or money, he would go buy noodles, and the old man had long taken pity on him. That day, Ayu entrusted all his winnings to the shop. The next day, he gambled again and won without fail. After four or five days, his coins had grown to over a hundred thousand. Suddenly, he reflected: *"The money I win at gambling is all ill-gotten gain. Though I have been blessed by the gods, those I defeat are not all wealthy. To seize their money so swiftly is to invite misfortune. Moreover, as a beggar, I have suddenly come into over a hundred thousand coins—if I work hard and live frugally, I can build a family. Why gamble further? Winning brings no peace of mind, and losing would reduce me to beggary again."* He then swore to the gods never to gamble again.

At the time, the old merchant’s noodle shop was on the verge of closing for lack of capital. Ayu consulted with him and offered all his winnings to replenish the shop’s funds, working there as a hired hand. The old man was delighted and chose an auspicious day to reopen. When the shop reopened, customers flocked to it like ducks to water, and profits multiplied several times over. Within a few years, the shop’s capital grew to several thousand, and business thrived. The old merchant had no sons, only a daughter, who held Ayu in high regard. He gave her to Ayu in marriage. Ayu, in turn, served the old man as a father and remained devoted to him for the rest of his life. Later, his wife bore two sons in succession, and Ayu lived to the age of seventy before passing away. His descendants still run the noodle shop as before, and the family enjoys modest prosperity.

Ayu never knew his own surname and took Jia as his own after relying on the old merchant. The Master of the Flower Seat remarked: *"Ayu, though a lowly beggar, did not pocket the money he found—already a rare virtue! Yet one might say this was but a single good impulse. When he dreamed of divine favor and won great wealth through gambling, who among others in his place would not have treated his luck like a treasure bowl, hoping to fill great chests with gold from endless wagers? Yet even in the midst of unbroken victory, he considered the hardship of those he defeated and swore to the gods to stop.
