This was truly a case of ‘retiring bravely from the rapids’—both benevolent and wise!"*

The family lived to a ripe old age and met a virtuous end—was it not fitting that blessings should flow to their descendants?

---

**9. The Thief’s Reward**

In dead of night, he stole by the hidden beam,
Then chanced upon a rapist fierce as wolf or tiger.
Had the woman refused, two lives would have been lost—
He wrested the blade and struck the black-hearted rogue.

Thrice he cried, *"I’m a thief!"*—yet his spirit was ever bold.
The bundle returned, the debt repaid, gain and loss as fleeting as Chu’s lost bow.

At first, a beggar, then a thief, a certain Jia later amassed great wealth. His descendants entered the official register, and he was honored as a venerable patriarch.

When he was still a thief, there lived in the same town a family surnamed Mou, long prosperous, where three generations of widows had dwelt. Three sisters-in-law—all childless, their husbands dead—faced the extinction of their line. Only the youngest sister-in-law carried a posthumous child, and all hoped for a son to continue the ancestral rites.

On the Clear and Bright Festival, they went to sweep the graves in the countryside. The two elder sisters-in-law made the journey, which would take three days for the round trip. The youngest, being with child, stayed behind, attended by an old serving woman.

Jia, having scouted the house, seized the opportunity to burgle it. At dusk, he scaled the wall and entered, only to see the youngest sister-in-law and the old woman carrying a lamp to check the doors. He slipped into the woman’s chamber and hid.

The woman sat beneath the lamp, reading, while the old woman, feigning drunkenness, urged her to sleep. The woman said, *"Go close the door and rest. Do not disturb me."* The old woman left, leaving the door ajar.

Moments later, a young man pushed open the door. Jia, suspecting a fellow thief, was surprised he had not waited for the woman to sleep—and even more so by his fine, well-kept clothes. The woman, seeing the intruder, rose in alarm and tried to call for the old woman, who did not answer. The young man seized her, demanding her favors. She resisted fiercely, crying out, but the old woman paid no heed.

When the woman refused, the young man drew a knife from his boot and threatened, *"Yield, or I’ll bathe my blade in blood!"* The woman rebuked him: *"My family has always been honorable. I will not suffer the stain of a scoundrel’s touch. Kill me if you must—I will never submit!"* He pressed the knife to her throat.

Jia, watching in mounting fury, suddenly sprang out, wrested the knife from the young man’s hand, and struck him down with a blow to the forehead. The woman, taken by surprise, trembled violently, unable to utter a sound.

Jia rushed out, flung open the door, and shouted, *"Catch the thief!"* The neighbors gathered, asking, *"Where is the thief? Who are you?"* Driven by righteous indignation, Jia had forgotten he had come to steal. Only now did he realize, and he laughed: *"I am a thief—but there are those far more despicable than thieves. Why not follow me?"* And he led the crowd into the woman’s chamber.

At that moment, the woman had already fled to another room, leaving only one man lying in a pool of blood. They held up a light to see—it was the neighbor from the west, fortunately only lightly wounded and not yet dead. When asked how he came to be there, he remained silent. When questioned, Jia recounted all he had witnessed, and the crowd then bound the man together. At daybreak, they escorted him to the authorities.

The young man, in turn, falsely accused the woman and Jia of adultery, claiming he had gone that evening to catch them in the act. Jia said, *"I am a thief—who does not know this? Even if the woman were unchaste, how could she consent to adultery with a thief?"* He then detailed the events of the night and confessed to his years of burglary, confirming his identity as a thief. The magistrate, after verifying the records, subjected the young man to severe interrogation. Only then did he confess the truth: he had long coveted the woman’s beauty. That night, he had seized the opportunity to go to her.

The serving matron had accepted bribes to facilitate their scheme. The magistrate thus sentenced the young man and the matron according to the law, commended the woman’s chastity, and released Jia on grounds of righteousness. Upon his release, Jia resumed his thieving as before.

One night, he burgled a village shop. Discovered by the master of the house, he fled, pursued by a large crowd. In his haste, he blundered into a dead end with no escape. In his panic, he spotted a ruined temple, scaled its wall, and sought to hide beneath the altar table. In his rush, he collided with a clay statue standing beside it, knocking it to the ground—and himself with it. In his dazed state, he saw the clay figure he had struck rise from the floor: its face was green, its beard crimson, and it brandished a blade as it roared at Jia, *"How dare you knock me down?"* It lunged forward, seized Jia, and prepared to kill him. Jia strained with all his might to resist.

Suddenly, a voice thundered from the hall above: *"This man preserved a woman’s chastity and safeguarded another’s ancestral line—his hidden virtue is vast, and Heaven has already bestowed great fortune upon him. How dare you ghostly minions bring harm to him?"* At once, someone seized the green-faced figure by the hair and dragged him away, striking him hundreds of times. Then the voice summoned Jia, saying, *"Beneath the cinnabar steps lies a great treasure—it is granted to you."*

He bowed in gratitude and rose. In a daze, he saw beneath the cinnabar steps a mountain of gold; hastening down the steps, he stumbled and awoke. Looking up, he saw three or five scattered stars in the sky, the faint glow of dawn just breaking. Silently recalling the divine words, he followed the steps downward and searched the ground all around, finding only a single Kangxi-era coin. Thinking the ghost had mocked him, he nonetheless picked it up. As the light grew discernible, he set off. Soon he reached a village, where he saw by the roadside a vendor selling roasted sweet potatoes. With the coin he had found, he bought some to eat. Presently, an old man also came to buy food and sat beside him. After eating, the old man left at once, forgetting his satchel. As Jia was about to rise, he saw it and knew it belonged to the old man. Opening it, he found two large ingots of yellow gold, over a hundred foreign silver coins, several hundred copper cash, and four ledgers recording uncollected silver debts amounting to tens of thousands. Fearing the sweet potato vendor would see, he quickly closed it. He thought to himself, *"Could this be the treasure the deity bestowed? Yet if the old man loses these ledgers, how will he collect the silver? Even if it is a divine gift, I cannot accept it."* So he sat back down to wait for the old man’s return. After a long while, the vendor grew angry and urged him, *"If you’re not going to spend another coin, why sit here so long? Do you plan to stay the night?"* Jia replied, *"No, I still wish to buy more."* He took out a few coins from the satchel and bought more food to continue waiting.

The old man indeed came hurrying back in great haste, sweat streaming down like rain. Seeing Jia still seated and eating, he immediately asked, *"You haven’t left yet—did you happen to see a satchel I just lost?"* Jia smiled and said, *"Had it not been for your belongings, I would have left long ago."* With that, he lifted the satchel and returned it, saying, *"Everything is here as it was. I only borrowed a few coins to buy sweet potatoes—please do not blame me."* The old man neither opened it to check nor expressed gratitude, merely saying, *"My humble home is not far from here—why not accompany me?"* Jia followed him. After walking several *li*, they arrived at a grand residence where stacks of lumber outside the gate rose like a mountain. The old man led him inside to the main hall. Then he went in, adjusted his robes and cap, and emerged to bow to Jia, saying, *"I am from Chu and have run a lumber business here for many years. All the lumber shops in the various counties are branches of this one, with capital amounting to hundreds of thousands—most of it lent on credit, all recorded in the ledgers I just lost. Thanks to your returning them to me, I am saved from ruin! Please accept a thousand taels of silver as my gratitude."*

Jia firmly declined. Seeing his sincerity, the old man asked what trade he had once practiced. Jia, looking abashed, replied, *"I have no trade."* The old man then asked if he had any family, to which Jia answered, *"I am a man adrift, with neither home nor wife."* *"Then how do you make a living?"* Jia said, *"I dare not deceive you—I am a thief!"* When asked his name, he told him. The old man started in surprise and said, *"In the past, there was a righteous thief in such-and-such a county who killed a profligate to protect a chaste widow—was that you?"* *"It was,"* Jia replied. The old man said, *"This deed of yours could be sworn before the gods. Now, seeing you refuse to take what is not yours, you are resplendent in your integrity—something even the gentry rarely achieve. My family’s wealth amounts to a million, yet I have no one to trust. If you do not disdain me, why not stay with me? I will provide you with clothing, food, and money—would this not be better than being a ‘gentleman of the beam’?"*

Jia joyfully agreed and lived with the old man. As he was somewhat literate, the old man had him manage the accounts. For two years, Jia handled the business with diligence, prudence, and meticulous care, never cutting the slightest corner. The old man, who had no sons, eventually adopted Jia as his own and took him back to his hometown. Since he had been away for so long, none of the villagers knew the truth. When the old man died, Jia inherited his business. His descendants multiplied, some becoming local scholars, others rising to the ranks of provincial inspectors and prefectural governors. To this day, they remain one of the great families of Chu.

The Master of the Flower Seat said: *"The Way of Heaven shows no partiality, yet it ever favors the good. Though his past was that of a lowly thief, his heart was that of a gentleman. Thus, he was rewarded with abundance, for Heaven did not withhold its bounty on account of his former deeds. Yet those born to noble lineages who descend to the status of menials—how they came to such a fate may well be understood."*

"X. The Blessings of a Lowly Constable:
Virtue, Not Birth, Determines Fortune

A Constable’s Son Passes the Imperial Examination—
What Became of Magistrate Deng’s Descendants?
Did Their Noble Lineage Bring Them Equal Renown?

The ancestors of a certain Grand Secretary of Wuxing (present-day Huzhou) once served as a constable in the prefectural yamen. Though his post was base, his heart was loyal and kind, ever mindful of aiding others and benefiting all beings. He often told his peers: *'The public court is a fine place to cultivate the Way. If we who serve in the public court do no good, it is like entering a mountain of treasures only to return empty-handed.'* Thus, whenever villagers came to lodge complaints, he would mediate and urge reconciliation. For the poor whose cause was just, he would especially shield them with all his might. Each night, he would place his rod in the chamber pot, for urine could dispel stasis and promote regeneration. Even when the rod drew blood and flesh lay in disarray, it would not fester. At the time, a certain Magistrate Deng was known for his severity—he would not cease flogging until blood flowed. Yet by this constable’s practice, many lives were preserved. His peers, moved by his example, likewise curbed their greed and cruelty."

The elder had a son whose name is now lost, who from childhood was quick-witted and fond of reading. Once, returning from the village school, he inadvertently obstructed the path of a prefectural governor and was seized by the governor’s advance guard. Seeing his youth, the governor merely rebuked him and let him go. Upon returning home, the boy declared resolutely, “Who is to say I shall not one day become a governor myself?” The elder laughed, “You are foolish, child! I am but a bondsman—how could you sit for the examinations and become a governor?” Though the son dared not argue, he applied himself to his studies with even greater diligence. As he grew older, his literary reputation spread widely. Many of the local gentry held him in high esteem, and the elder, ever a man of upright character, had always shown the utmost respect for scholars. Thus, the students of the Three Schools not only refrained from making things difficult for him but even vied to urge the elder to allow his son to take the examinations. Reluctantly, the elder consented, and at his first attempt, the son was admitted as a government student. Later, he passed the provincial examination and rose through the ranks to become a prefectural governor himself.

Several generations later, his descendants included a grand secretary and a provincial governor, and to this day their caps and tassels remain unbroken, earning them the reputation of a distinguished clan.

The Master of Seated Flowers remarked, “The old bondsman, though of the lowest station, established a single good intention in his heart. Heaven then did not hesitate to bestow upon him worthy sons and virtuous grandsons, greatly prospering his posterity. Yet how do those who usurp high positions and enjoy lavish emoluments, knowing only to fleece the people for their own benefit, compare to this elder? And how, then, will Heaven repay them?”

11. The Yangxian Scholar
Avoiding misfortune and seeking fortune through good thoughts,
Fortunate to meet a physiognomist whose arts reached the divine.
The bamboo mat above and the bamboo mat below turned to dust—
Yet in joy, he was selected as a candidate in Yixing.

A certain scholar from Yangxian, a renowned figure in the academy and of modestly prosperous family, traveled in the summer of the *renchen* year of the Jiaqing reign (1802) with fellow students to Chengjiang to sit for the *bazhen* examination.

At the time, the scholar had excelled in both the annual and triennial examinations, repeatedly topping his cohort—so swiftly claiming first place that he felt certain of securing the *bazhen* selection. Carrying ample funds, he passed his days in the lodging house, reveling in poetry and wine with his fellow scholars, thoroughly content.

At the inn there was a skilled physiognomist who sat behind a curtain at the door, his readings often proving uncannily accurate. The scholar, sharing the same lodgings, grew quite familiar with him. One day, returning with a fish, he teased the physiognomist: “You claim to read fortunes—tell me, will I be able to eat this fish?” The physiognomist glanced at the fish, then at the scholar, and replied, “No.” The scholar went inside, hastily cooked the fish, and set it on the table. He then invited the physiognomist to join him, intending to mock his error. As they were about to sit down, he asked again, “Will I eat it now?” The physiognomist answered, “No.” Before the words had fully left his mouth, a massive serpent dropped from the beam, crushing the pot. The vessel shattered, and the crowd erupted in alarm. The serpent slithered away, tail dragging, and the fish remained uneaten.

Astonished by the man’s uncanny skill, the scholar pressed him. The physiognomist demurred: “What skill? You were only jesting with me, so I jested in return. Had it not been in play, what concern would a mere fish be to fortune-telling?” The scholar then asked whether he would indeed be selected for the *bazhen*. The physiognomist hesitated, saying, “I have long wished to speak frankly, but feared reproach and dared not.” The scholar urged, “What harm could it do?” Finally, the man replied, “You have no hope of selection. Your countenance already bears the shadow of misfortune—three days hence, at the third watch, you will die an unnatural death. Your home is not far; you should return at once to die in your own bed.” The scholar asked, “Can this be avoided?”

The scholar asked, “Can this be avoided?”

The physiognomist replied, “It cannot.”

Seeing the man’s resolute words, the scholar was seized with terror. He immediately wished to pack his belongings. His fellow examinees all reproached the physiognomist for his reckless claim and urged the scholar not to return home. Though he remained, his heart was never at ease. When the appointed time arrived, the new moon had just risen, and all those lodging with him were asleep. Overwhelmed by doubt and dread, he found neither sitting nor lying bearable. In a daze, he stepped outside. Wandering aimlessly to a desolate stretch of land, he heard faint sobbing. Following the sound, he traced it to a dilapidated hut. Pushing open the door, he saw a woman clutching two children, weeping with bitter sorrow. When he asked her reason, she explained that her husband, having borrowed fifty taels of silver from a powerful family, had been sued by them and thrown into prison. Beaten and bloodied under interrogation, he was now forced to sell her to repay the debt—an agreement already made. At daybreak, she would be taken away. Unable to bear parting from her children, she wept.

The student silently calculated that his purse held ample gold. "If, as the physiognomist said, I am to die soon, what use is this to me? Better to return it on their behalf and preserve this couple." He then asked, "Has the marriage contract been drawn up yet?" "Not yet," she replied. "Then if you receive the silver, can the sale still be stopped?" "It can." "Where is the matchmaker?" "Not far from here." "In that case, hurry and call the matchmaker to wait here. I will return home to fetch the silver for you." The woman suspected he had other motives and hesitated to comply. The student smiled and said, "I pity your family being torn apart, so I will open my purse to help. Go quickly without doubt." Only then did the woman happily agree.

The student hastened home, took seventy taels of silver, and returned. There, the woman sat with an old man. When asked who he was, she said, "The matchmaker for my sale." The student produced the silver and gave it to the woman, explaining to the old man. The old man, startled, exclaimed, "A stranger on the road shows such noble virtue—how much more should I, your husband’s neighbor! Thanks to your kindness, the sale need not be mentioned. We must only rush to repay the debt to the authorities and secure the prisoner’s release." He then examined the silver, surprised by its amount. The student said, "The remainder can be used for trade to sustain yourselves, so you need not incur further debt." The old man sighed deeply and said, "How thoroughly you have considered this! You are truly the reborn parents of this couple!" He then knelt and asked the student’s name and address in detail.

The student returned to his lodging, but the physiognomist’s words still troubled him, and he could not sleep.

Hearing the night watch sound its third drum, he thought to himself, "The hour has come!" As he sat lost in doubt, there came a knock at the door—someone seeking an audience. Rising, he saw the woman from before, who had used the silver to settle the debt with the authorities. Her husband, now freed, had come with her to express their gratitude. The student rose to receive them, offering words of comfort and reassurance. After seeing them out, he prepared to return to bed when he heard a tremendous crash from his bedchamber. Entering, he found the wall collapsed, crushing the very bed where he had lain. The reed mat and bamboo sleeping mat beneath were reduced to fine powder. He then moved to sleep in another room.

The next day, the student sought out the physiognomist, who scoffed at his claims. The physiognomist, unaware of the previous night’s events, examined him closely and laughed, saying, “Do not deceive me—you must have done something last night. Your face bears the marks of profound hidden merit, a great act of unseen virtue. If you had not, you would already be dead, crushed beneath that crumbling wall! Had my words been false, you would have perished beneath its collapse.” The student marveled and bowed in admiration.

That very year, he was selected as a *bagong* and later entered the Hanlin Academy. The Master of the Flower-Adorned Seat remarked, “The physiognomist was no mere mortal! Though the matter of a single fish seems trivial—what bearing could it have on one’s fate?—his words were but a subtle deflection. The noble-minded, observing how the great serpent destroyed the vessel, understand that every sip and bite is predestined, not to be forced. Witnessing the bed crushed beneath the collapsing wall, they know that life and death arrive at their appointed hour, beyond all chance or escape.”

Then he took the gold from his pouch and swiftly released the imprisoned debtor. Those who had gathered outside the gate saw the calamity dissolve in an instant. The man’s flesh and blood were preserved, and thus my own body was kept whole. At the threshold between man and ghost—where life and death hang in the balance—fortune and misfortune shift in the blink of an eye. How swift as echo follows sound! “Disaster and blessing have no gate; they are summoned only by man.” Is this not true?

---

**Twelve: Elder Tang the Enfeoffed**

The enfeoffed elder delighted in goodness, and his son cultivated virtue.
His success in the examinations came not from backdoor dealings.
Though he did not attend the ritual court, his name still rose to prominence—
Only then did one recognize the prime minister as no ordinary talent.

Tang Dunfu was the enfeoffed father of a grand councilor. He once transported goods between north and south, shrewd in commerce yet careless with wealth, fond of righteousness, bearing the spirit of an ancient knight-errant. On one occasion, returning from the capital, he lodged at an inn in Renping County.

Hearing the sound of weeping from the neighboring room, he inquired of the innkeeper. It was an old man who had brought his young daughter to the capital, only to fall ill upon reaching the inn. His illness had dragged on, depleting his travel funds, and he was now preparing to sell his daughter to continue the journey. The girl could not bear to leave her father, hence her tears. Moved by compassion, the elder summoned the innkeeper to call them over. Upon asking their origins, he learned they were also from Xiaoshan, traveling to the capital to seek refuge with a relative who served as a ministry clerk. When he pressed further about selling the girl, the old man wept and explained, “My prolonged illness has left me owing the innkeeper tens of thousands in coin. With no other recourse at this dead end, I had no choice but to resort to this desperate measure.”

The elder then opened his purse and gave him a hundred taels of silver, saying, “Take this to repay the innkeeper, and use the remainder for your travel expenses. You need not sell your daughter.” Overjoyed beyond expectation, the old man hurriedly called his daughter forward and said, “This kind gentleman from the Tang family has given us so much silver. You should go with him—we are fellow townsmen, not strangers here with no one to turn to.” The girl stepped forward to bow in gratitude, revealing herself to be a beauty of sixteen. The elder sternly declared, “I did this solely out of pity for your separation as father and daughter, not to take your girl for myself. When you reach the capital, find her a worthy match—do not sell her again!” Both father and daughter knelt in tearful thanks, inquiring in detail about the elder’s family before departing.

By this time, the grand councilor had already become a government student and was preparing for the autumn examinations. In the year *jiayin*, as he traveled to Hangzhou for the provincial exam, his teacher suddenly summoned him and secretly handed him a *guanjie*, saying, “The proctor has passed on the chief examiner’s instructions.” The grand councilor set it aside without looking, replying, “This must be a mistake. I share no acquaintance with the chief examiner—how could such a thing come to me?”

Moreover, I would not wish to succeed through such *guanjie*.” His teacher pressed it upon him, but he steadfastly refused. After the examination, he returned home at once. When the results were announced, he unexpectedly topped the list. The messenger arrived, and the county magistrate came as well, bearing the chief examiner’s summons to hasten to the celebratory feast. With no choice, he went to the provincial capital to pay his respects to his *zuoshi*. At the time, the chief examiner was Grand Secretary Wu of Nanhui. Upon seeing him, Wu immediately said, “Minister He has entrusted this to me—hurry to the capital. You may yet claim the *sanyuan*.”

The grand councilor, visibly displeased, replied, “I am but a humble scholar from the countryside—how could I be known to the minister? And to advance by such *yinyuan* would be against all propriety.” Grand Secretary Wu fell silent. As he left, the proctor summoned him again and inquired, “Did your esteemed father once rescue a father and daughter in dire straits on the road to Renping?” “I know nothing of this,” he replied. The governor then said, “Go home and ask your father—you will understand. That girl later entered the capital, only to be sold again by her father into Minister He’s residence, where she became his favored concubine. She told the minister of your father’s great kindness and mentioned that you were sitting for the autumn examinations. Thus, the minister instructed the chief examiner to seek out your paper, but it could not be found among the first selections. When the list was being finalized, even after unsealing all the discarded papers, yours still eluded them. Only upon reviewing the middle-ranked papers did they discover you had already placed first. This was surely fate, yet the minister’s regard for you was most sincere. You should hasten to the capital and not defy his wishes.”

The grand councilor politely declined and departed. Upon returning home and questioning his father, he learned the truth, yet he still did not proceed to the Ministry of Rites examination. Only in the year *jiwei*, the year after Minister He’s downfall, did he enter the capital. That same year, he passed the metropolitan examination and entered the Hanlin Academy.

The Master of the Flower Banquet observed, “With a father of such lofty virtue, his descendants were surely destined for success. Yet when Minister He held power, his influence blazed like fire, scorching all who drew near. How many officials and scholars, eager for glory and favor, did not treat his patronage as the swiftest path to advancement?”

Lu Zangyong once pointed to Mount Zhongnan and said to Simā Qianzhen, who had been summoned to the capital and was about to return to his mountain retreat, “There are many fine places within these peaks.” Simā slowly replied, “From my perspective, it is but the swiftest path to officialdom.” Lu was deeply ashamed.

Yet Xiekui, though but a mere scholar, alone refused to yield to such influence. His moral integrity stood so lofty. For decades, he maintained an unyielding demeanor in court, his conduct unwavering through peace and peril, earning the reverence of all under heaven. Truly, he was one of great refinement, towering above the common crowd. Such was Xiekui!

Even that young woman hastened to repay her great debt of gratitude. Compared to those gentry who betray their obligations, how vast is the difference between worthy and unworthy!

---

**13. Liu Huiyuan**

*Diligence deserved reward, debts of duty repaid—*
*Clear rewards and punishments command Heaven’s awe.*
*One flaw could not obscure his great virtue,*
*How could a drowned ghost be made a city god?*

Liu Huiyuan, styled Youqing, served as magistrate of Yushan in Jiangyou. Upright, discerning, and diligent, he left no cases unresolved. Unless a litigant was clearly guilty, he did not lightly detain them for trial.

At the time, a tailor named So-and-so was to serve as a witness in a certain case. As the hearing was about to convene, Magistrate Liu departed for the prefectural seat on urgent business. The constables detained both parties to the suit, along with the witnesses, at an inn to await his return.

The tailor’s wife, learning her husband had been taken into custody, grew fearful. She thought, *The magistrate does not lightly detain people—if he has done so, the offense must be grave.* In haste, she took her young son into the city to see him. A sudden flood swept them away, and both drowned.

When Magistrate Liu returned and concluded the case, the tailor returned home to find his family gone—and so he hanged himself. The magistrate remained unaware.

A month later, he again traveled to the provincial capital.

As soon as he boarded the boat, he suddenly rebuked his servants, saying, "Where did that woman in the cabin come from?" They looked but saw no one. When he descended into the cabin, he again demanded, "Who are you? How dare you bring a young girl and child into my cabin without permission?" The servants looked again but saw nothing. The company suspected the boat was haunted and urged the magistrate to change vessels, but he refused.

The boat set off. Magistrate Liu sat with his back to the window, reading. Suddenly, the window collapsed, and he toppled backward into the water, as if pulled by some force. They hastily fished him out, but it was not until three *li* downstream that they recovered his body—precisely where the tailor’s wife and child had drowned.

More than a year after his death, a Daoist at the City God Temple of Yushan dreamed that the new City God had arrived to take office—it was Magistrate Liu.

The Master of the Flower Seat remarked: "Alas! Diligence is a virtue. Yet Magistrate Liu, through his very diligence, inadvertently caused the deaths of three lives and ultimately suffered the consequences himself. As the Book says: *Is resentment ever plain to see? It is the unseen that must be guarded against.* For those in high office, to guard against unseen resentments—how very difficult it is!"

Though those three were indeed wronged, Magistrate Liu had always been wise and upright—yet through a single moment of oversight, he met with such a grievous retribution. Is this not even more unjust? Yet in death he became a deity, and the judgment of Heaven is never in error.

---

**14. Shen Hongfei**
*Harboring Grudges, Spreading Slander to Ruin Family Bonds—Even in Death, His Boasts Proved True*
*Brother and Sister-in-Law, Both Come to Plead—What Matter Is This, That No Mediator Could Resolve?*

In Wuxing, there was a man named Shen Hongfei, one of three brothers. Hongfei, the second-born, was treacherous by nature. His eldest brother traveled for trade, leaving his wife alone at home. As the youngest brother was still a child, no suspicion arose. Whenever she sent letters to her husband, she would call the youngest to her room to write them, sometimes rewarding him with wine and food for his trouble.

Hongfei had long harbored a slight grudge against his brother and sister-in-law. He falsely accused her of adultery with the youngest brother. He spread the slander to all he met, and soon the vile rumors spread far and wide. Even frivolous men composed *zhu-zhi* verses about it. With no way to clear her name, the sister-in-law ultimately hanged herself.

Hongfei was greatly pleased, believing his sister-in-law had taken her own life out of shame upon exposure of their illicit affair, thus confirming his words—heedless of the unbearable humiliation it brought upon the youngest brother. With no means to clear his name, the youngest soon fell ill from grief and died.

Before long, Hongfei returned from outside. He had just entered the central hall when he suddenly cried out, "Ghosts! Ghosts!" By the time others arrived, he had rushed out the main gate and stood in the middle of the street, slapping his own cheeks and shouting, "Black-hearted villain! You falsely accused me of adultery with my uncle—I hanged myself, yet even in death you slander me, driving my uncle to die with resentment. Now we two have brought our case before the courts of the underworld to claim your life!" Then he struck his own chest, crying, "You were on bad terms with your eldest brother, yet you framed me and my sister-in-law—where was your conscience?"

A crowd gathered like a marketplace. Knowing the spirits of his sister-in-law and youngest brother had possessed him, someone intervened to mediate: "The matter has come to this—what good would taking his life do? Why not have a high monk summoned to perform rites for you both, so you may soon attain rebirth? What do you say?" Hongfei first spoke in the youngest brother’s voice: "I committed no evil in life—why would I need a monk’s rites? If my sister-in-law is willing to let this go, I shall depart at once!" Then he spoke in his sister-in-law’s voice: "What kind of matter is this?"

"Can this even be mediated?" With that, he suddenly bit his own tongue, shredding it to pieces, then seized sharp stones and smashed his teeth until they all fell out, blood streaming down his body. He then leapt up and dashed his head against a stone pillar by the road, his brains splattering as he died.

---

**15. The Legalist’s Folly**
*He refused again and again to shield his own person—*
*How could he then chase favor as a guest in the yamen?*
*Three days after her hanging, her ghost came to claim his life—*
*Truly a pitiable man, this scholar.*

A scholar from Zhejiang, versed in the techniques of Shen Buhai and Han Fei, traveled to Jiangyou to serve as a legal advisor under a prefect. Once in the yamen, he and his superior got along exceedingly well. The prefect, a formidable official, insisted on personally interrogating all legal cases from the subordinate counties. At the time, a widow surnamed Mou was being harassed by a ruffian. Though she repeatedly rebuffed him, he persisted until, unable to endure the humiliation, she filed a complaint with the county. The ruffian, however, counter-accused her of consensual adultery. The county magistrate, unable to resolve the case, forwarded it to the prefectural court. The prefect, discerning the widow’s innocence, severely punished the ruffian, thus closing the case.

During the trial, the scholar stood behind the screen, gazing at the widow’s beauty with desire. He resolved to take her as his concubine. The widow firmly refused, but he forced the betrothal gifts upon her. Fearing she could not resist the yamen’s power and dreading violation, she hanged herself. Three days after her death, the scholar rose at dawn to find his door still barred. Breaking it open, they discovered him lying on the ground, a knife in hand, his throat slit and blood pooling around him.

---

**16. The Sin of Speech**
*Words so frivolous they chill the heart—*
*How much worse when traced in ink?*
*Such talent, all misused—*
*Karma self-made, redemption hard to seek.*

Yao Kangming was a younger clansman of my maternal great-uncle. Learned and richly endowed with talent, he had no other grave faults in life, save that his speech was often frivolous and he delighted in using his brush to mock and satirize others. Whenever matters touched upon the inner chambers of others—even if only by suspicion—he would invariably contrive clever connections to shape them into songs and verses. His diction was fresh and refined, and his works were often widely circulated. Yet after repeatedly failing the examinations, and further shunned for his frivolity, no one dared engage him to tutor their children. He eventually died in poverty and starvation, with neither clothes nor bedding at the time of his death, lying in tattered rags teeming with lice. My maternal great-uncle’s family arranged his burial. He left behind a son, who had nowhere to go and was raised by my great-uncle. The boy was rather honest and cautious, giving hope that he might establish himself. Just as plans were made to arrange his marriage, he suddenly died, and the line was thus extinguished. Could this not be the retribution for frivolity?

**17. Elder Hu of the Seal**
*The ringleader’s crime is grave, the coerced’s light—*
*Yet good men bear the name of corrupt clerks.*
*Seven words from the governor moved his heart—*
*"Within the public gate, one may cultivate the Way."*

Elder Hu, father of Prefect Hu Xiangshan, was a clerk in the criminal bureau of Jinshan. He was always loyal and honest, and throughout his life, he disdained to engage in the practice of "raising or lowering his hand" to manipulate cases. When a robbery case occurred in Jinshan, the victim was injured and later died. Over thirty principal and accessory offenders were arrested. At the time, the law was severe: any robber who injured a person, regardless of whether they were the ringleader or an accomplice, was to be executed. As it happened, Elder Hu was assigned to handle this case. Seeing that the thirty-odd men were all impoverished commoners with no livelihood, he could not bear the thought of them being executed side by side. Thus, he drafted a verdict in which only the two who had initiated the robbery and struck the fatal blow were sentenced to death, while the rest were sentenced to military exile. The magistrate suspected the ruling was too lenient, but Elder Hu insisted, "Though this was a robbery, their confessions show they were not habitual criminals. Even the injury to the victim occurred at night, in a sudden scuffle—no blades or weapons were involved. It seems permissible to apply a lighter sentence."

The magistrate, fearing harsh reprimand, said, "If this is questioned, I will be held accountable." Elder Hu replied, "If it is questioned, allow me to go to the provincial capital to answer for the error of leniency." The magistrate, composing himself, said, "If you are still willing to plead for the people’s lives, how could I alone lack a humane heart?" He then submitted the verdict as drafted. As expected, it was rejected, and a new ruling was demanded. Elder Hu again drafted a memorial to uphold the original decision. After three rejections and three resubmissions, the governor became furious, issuing stern reprimands and ordering the case to be brought before him for personal review. He further commanded the magistrate to bring the official seal to Suzhou, clearly intending to impeach him. Terrified, the magistrate blamed Elder Hu. Elder Hu volunteered to accompany him to the provincial capital, saying, "If you meet with the governor, please leave everything to me. If we succeed, it will be your good fortune. If not, I alone will bear the responsibility."

The magistrate thus brought Elder Hu with him to the capital. Upon their audience with the governor, Elder Hu waited outside the yamen gate. The governor rebuked the magistrate for his leniency, his words and tone severe. The magistrate kowtowed and admitted his fault. The governor then asked, "You are newly appointed—who taught you to do this?" The magistrate replied, "The clerk Hu from the criminal bureau." The governor asked, "Did he come with you?"

The magistrate answered, "He is waiting outside the yamen gate." The governor laughed and said, "I had suspected this cunning clerk of taking bribes and manipulating the law—my guess was not wrong. I shall interrogate him myself." He immediately ordered his adjutant to bring Elder Hu in. The governor greeted him with a rebuke: "As a clerk in the criminal bureau, do you not know that robbers who injure their victims to the point of death—whether principals or accessories—are all to be beheaded?" Elder Hu kowtowed and replied, "I am well aware. Yet though the law is thus, there are degrees of severity that must be weighed." The governor grew angry: "Robbers who injure their victims are all the same—what degrees of severity can there be?" Elder Hu answered, "The law refers to hardened bandits of many years, who commit robbery by force of arms and kill their victims outright. In this case, the offenders are all impoverished men who have lost their livelihoods, driven by hunger and cold into the snare of the law. The victim’s death resulted from a fall during a struggle. It would seem proper to apply a somewhat more lenient standard."

The governor’s voice grew stern: "How much bribe did you take from the robbers to dare speak so artfully in their defense? If you do not speak truthfully, I shall have you put to the rack." Elder Hu kowtowed again and replied, "If Your Excellency believes this humble official has deliberately sought to exonerate the robbers, I dare not deny my guilt. But as for taking bribes and bending the law—such conduct has always been beneath me. Not only in this grave case, but even in minor disputes over brawls, I would never betray my conscience."

The governor gave a forced laugh: "If you took no bribe from the robbers, then why do you so strenuously argue for a lighter sentence?" Elder Hu said, "I dare not say." The governor pressed him further. He answered, "It is nothing more than this: within the gates of officialdom, one may still cultivate the Way. Moreover, has Your Excellency never heard the words of Ouyang Wenzhong? *Ouyang Xiu, styled Yongshu, a native of Luling. During the reign of Emperor Renzong of the Song, he rose through the ranks to become Grand Preceptor, and was posthumously honored as Wenzhong.* ‘If I seek to spare their lives but cannot, then the dead will bear me no grudge, will they?’"

The governor, hearing this, was taken aback. He then ordered the accused in the recent case to be brought before him for closer inspection. Their demeanor was gentle and welcoming, and at a glance, one could see they were men of virtue. *Virtuous men: see annotation in the first chapter.* His anger subsided, and he asked, *Ji, pronounced "ji." (From the *Book of Han*, "Biography of Wei Xiang"): "to temper one’s severity." (Annotation): "Ji" means to cease. (Note): "Ji yan" refers to the cessation of an angry countenance.* "How many sons do you have?" Elder Hu replied, "I have four sons."

The governor asked solemnly, "What arts have your sons mastered?"
Elder Hu replied, "My eldest son, Lingyi, was fortunate to pass the imperial examination at the upper level. My next three sons are all county students, and my fourth was this year selected by Your Excellency as the top candidate in the prefectural examination."

The governor, with deep respect, said, "This is the reward for your fine conduct within the public sphere! In this case, I shall spare many lives thanks to you, and this also serves as an auspicious omen for your son’s future success in the *Qionglin* feast next year!"* *See annotation: (Tang *Treatise on Selection*): In the eighth year of Taiping Xingguo (983), the imperial examination began to be divided into three ranks, and from then on, the celebratory banquet was held at Qionglin Park. (Note): *Qionglin* as a harbinger refers to the precursor of passing the imperial examination.*

He then ordered them to withdraw and proceeded to review the case as originally determined. Two were executed, while the rest were spared. The magistrate was also reinstated to his original post.

The following year, Prefect Xiang of Mount Xiang indeed triumphed in the *Liwei* examinations,* *To pass the imperial examination is called "triumphing in the *Liwei*."*
while his next three sons were all recommended to the Imperial Academy and entered official service.* *See annotation: (Xuanhe *Catalogue of Calligraphy*): Zhang Hua was first recommended and thus entered official service. (Note): To enter official service is to be recorded on the official register by the prefectural governor.*
His fourth son, a government student, has to this day maintained an unbroken lineage of scholarly distinction.

The Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarked: “I have often said that if one wishes to do good, one must not only always harbor such an intention but also possess steadfast insight and resolve, so as not to be swayed by power and influence or shaken by dissenting views. In this world, many begin with the finest of intentions—none fail to take the relief of others and the benefit of all things as their aim—yet when confronted with overwhelming authority and personal gain or loss at stake, their initial resolve often wavers. How few among the worthy scholar-officials of past and present have not forfeited their lifelong principles in this way?

Yet Elder Hu, though but a humble county clerk, *Yuan* (pronounced *yuàn*). (See *Han shu: Biography of Jia Fu*): Before Wang Mang became a county clerk, he was appointed to oversee the Hedong region. (Note): *County clerk* refers to a scribe in the county yamen.
held fast to his convictions without wavering. *Nao* (pronounced *nào*, or *niāo* in the level tone): to disturb, to yield.
Though the Prefect’s rank was exalted, and though he was cowed by the threat of severe punishment and overawed by haughty demeanor, Elder Hu presented his arguments with unyielding forthrightness, *Kan* (pronounced *kǎn*). (See *Lunyu* commentary): *Kǎnkǎn* means resolute and upright.
neither yielding nor flattering.” *Not flattering*: not bending to curry favor.

The end result was that his own views prevailed, and the Prefect, too, set aside his stern authority to listen. Extending this principle further, even figures like Zhang Shizhi or Xu Yougong—what more could be said of them?

Zhang Shizhi of the Han, styled Ji, was a native of Yuzhou. Recommended by Yuan Ang, he was appointed Chief Justice and fulfilled his duties with utmost diligence. The court praised him, saying, *"With Zhang Shizhi as Chief Justice, the realm knows no wronged subjects."* Xu Yougong of the Tang, named Hongmin, served during Empress Wu’s reign. When Zhou Xing and Lai Junchen, divining the Empress’s will, vied to outdo one another in framing accusations, only Xu Yougong, in handling cases, upheld fairness and integrity, saving many lives.

Was this not because steadfast insight and unwavering resolve fortified his good heart?

---

18. Prefect Zhang: *Transforming a Worthless Life*

A life of vice and ruin—
Yet evil thoughts dissolved in an instant, giving rise to a good heart.
Sitting in the snow, silver in hand, awaiting the owner’s return;
Past transgressions pardoned, he was granted rank and honor.

Prefect Zhang was a native of Jingkou. In his youth as a scholar, he was poor and of ill repute, so that people feared him like a tiger. Yet his nature was rather bold and unrestrained; whatever wealth he extorted or seized, he scattered with his own hands. Many impoverished households in his village relied on his aid, and thus his home had no surplus grain. One New Year’s Eve, he could not even light a fire for cooking.

He reflected that his relatives and acquaintances all harbored old grudges, and most were petty, narrow-minded men—none to whom he could turn.

Yet he could not bear to grovel in supplication. With no recourse as dusk fell, he took his wife’s tattered cloth skirt, pawned it at a broker’s for a thousand coins, and bought a peck of rice, wine, meat, and spirit-money. He placed them in a basket and carried them home. His house stood in a narrow alley; the rain had made the path slick. As he neared home, he tripped and fell, spilling everything from the basket. Enraged, he returned with a lamp to inspect the scene and found a bag—heavy in his grasp. Taking it inside, he saw two silver ingots, dozens of taels of silver fragments, over a hundred foreign coins, several hundred copper coins, an account book, and several promissory notes. He realized it belonged to a silk shop. Overjoyed, he thought this windfall would bring modest prosperity. As he prepared to take it inside, he suddenly reflected that it must have been left by a shop clerk collecting debts—without it, the man would be ruined. Better to wait for the owner to return and seek it. So he hid the bag, used some of the loose change to buy rice, and instructed his wife to cook. He then took up a candle and sat outside his door in the wind and snow.

Before long, he saw an old man and two youths carrying a silk shop lantern, searching the path with frantic urgency. Observing them, he knew they were the ones who had lost the bag. As they drew near, he called out, “What are you looking for?” The old man recognized Zhang and, knowing his disreputable ways, dared not speak plainly, stammering and trying to slip away. Zhang’s expression darkened: “You’re wandering about with a lantern, not searching for what you lost—are you casing houses to rob at night? If you don’t tell me the truth, I won’t let you leave!” The old man had no choice but to confess: “I was collecting debts and passed this way. I rested briefly by the roadside, then hurried on in the rain and lost a cloth bag. I’ve come to look for it, but it’s gone—I suppose some passerby picked it up.” Zhang asked what was inside, and the old man listed the silver, coins, and account books in exact detail. Zhang smiled: “Then come sit in my humble home for a moment. I know who found your things.” The old man bowed and said, “If you know, sir, please tell me at once. I wouldn’t dare impose on your esteemed residence.” Zhang replied, “This isn’t something to discuss standing here. My home is right nearby—why hesitate to grace me with your presence?” The old man wavered, reluctant to go, but Zhang pulled him inside.

Once seated, the old man repeated, “If you know, sir, please tell me. My gratitude will be eternal.” Zhang murmured assent. He went inside, brought out tea, and asked what the old man did at the shop. “Collecting debts,” came the reply. “What will you do now that you’ve lost this?” The old man’s tears streamed down: “Even selling my family wouldn’t cover it. I’ll have to die.” He pleaded again, “If you know, sir, please tell me. My gratitude will be eternal.” Zhang murmured assent once more. Suspecting he was being mocked, the old man stood to leave. Zhang laughed: “Sit down, don’t be impatient. I don’t know who found your things. But I have some savings—I’ll repay you myself.” He brought out the bag and asked, “Is this enough to cover your loss?” The old man was stunned. Glancing at Zhang warily, he stammered, unable to speak. Zhang reassured him: “Don’t suspect me! If I wanted this money, I’d have already offered sacrifices to the gods and shut my door to drink my fill. Why would I sit alone in the wind and snow, waiting for you to arrive?” He emptied the silver and foreign coins onto the table: “The silver and coins are still here—the copper I used to buy rice.” The old man, overjoyed beyond hope, kowtowed endlessly. Rising, he urged Zhang to take half. Zhang refused sternly. The old man said, “If you won’t take it, I can’t leave.” Zhang smiled: “If you insist on giving me something, lend me two foreign coins so I can eat my fill in the New Year.” Seeing his sincerity, the old man dared not argue further. He gave Zhang two coins, bowed in thanks, and departed.

Zhang used the coins to buy more wine and dried meat, offering them to the gods and heaven. He and his wife drank together, then slept drunk. In his dream, he was bound and taken away. He saw a kingly figure resembling the Star Lord Wen Chang, who rebuked him: “Your many misdeeds leave you no time to reform—you’ll fall into the realm of hungry ghosts!” As he begged for mercy, someone entered with a document. The king’s expression softened at once: “This act of great goodness atones for your past transgressions. Your official register shall be restored, and you will pass the autumn examinations.”

The king further addressed Zhang: “When you return home, you must humble yourself and reform your conduct. Your future prospects cannot be measured.” Zhang awoke, realizing it was the act of returning the silver that had earned him divine protection. At daybreak, he immediately prepared a memorial and burned it before the Celestial Emperor, vowing to follow the ledger of merits and demerits to atone for his past transgressions.

Before long, the old man from before, now dressed in formal attire, came to express his gratitude: “Had it not been for you last night, my entire family—old and young—would have perished. I have informed my master, and he will surely reward you.” Zhang politely declined. From then on, he redoubled his efforts to do good, yet his poverty grew more severe, often going several days without lighting a fire to cook.

In the middle of the eighth lunar month, when scholars were preparing to travel to Jinling for the examinations, Zhang alone had not a single coin to his name. With no means even to feed himself, he had long abandoned any thought of entering the examination hall. Suddenly, he encountered the old man again, who asked: “Why have you still not gone to take the examination?” Zhang explained his poverty. The old man said: “You are a virtuous man—how can you not attend the provincial examination? Return home and wait for me there.” Zhang agreed. Upon arriving home, the old man soon followed, presenting him with twenty taels of silver: “This is my accumulated savings. You should board a boat and depart at once.” Zhang gratefully accepted. After the old man left, he privately thought that using the silver for the examination was uncertain, whereas keeping it for firewood and rice would secure him half a year without fear of cold or hunger. He was reluctant to go but worried about disappointing the old man. As he hesitated, he heard a knock at the door.

Opening the door, Zhang saw the old man had returned with a young man, who addressed him: “This is my master. Moved by your noble virtue, he has long wished to repay your kindness. Learning you were to sit the examination, he feared your family would lack for daily provisions in your absence, and so prepared twenty taels of silver and four *shi* of white rice as a gift, to relieve your concerns at home.” Overjoyed beyond expectation, Zhang boarded a boat and set off for the examination. When the results were posted, he indeed passed, and the old man again arrived with his master, presenting him with funds for the capital examination. Ultimately, he passed both levels in succession, attaining the rank of *jinshi* and rising to the post of *guancha*.

The Master of Sitting-Among-Flowers remarked: “A single virtuous thought can outweigh a hundred transgressions. From starvation’s brink to the register of official salary—how swift the transformation! Is this not because the Dao of Heaven holds no fixed judgment, but responds only to what one chooses for oneself? Yet Zhang’s ability to act thus also stems from his innate magnanimity, his joy in aiding the poor, and the good roots he has cultivated. How could those litigious pettifoggers, who abet the strong in bullying the weak and haggle over every penny, ever be mentioned in the same breath?”

Nineteen: A Foreign Venture Brings Wealth
Poverty Yet the Foundation of Aiding Others in Need
Sincerity and Thickness Alone Can Move Even the Barbarians
Had He Not Possessed the Elder’s Great Virtue
Finding Gold Tenfold Would Have Been of No Avail

Elder Liu, a native of Shanghang, was sincere and steadfast from youth, incapable of uttering a deceitful word. Though poor, he took great delight in aiding those in urgent need. In his twentieth year, he lost both parents and, with no means to support himself, had a relative serving as magistrate in eastern Guangdong. He resolved to seek refuge with him. Selling his house, he obtained several tens of taels of silver and set out. Upon reaching Jiangxi, he encountered a fellow townsman, who, fallen on hard times and unable to return home, was about to sell his son into servitude. Moved by compassion, Elder Liu divided half his funds and gave them to him.

When Elder Liu arrived in Guangdong, his relative had already passed away, and his wife and children were transporting the coffin home. With no place to stay, he lodged at an inn, finding himself in dire straits. He soon fell ill, and the innkeeper, pitying this young and solitary traveler, arranged for a physician to treat him. Though his illness abated, his purse was now empty, leaving him unable to return home or repay the innkeeper.

Pondering his solitary existence adrift in the world, with nothing to bind his heart, he thought it better to end his remaining years by his own hand. Thus, he left the City of Five Rams. Arriving at a secluded stretch of the Pearl River, he gazed down upon its surging waves and steeled himself to leap. Suddenly, he noticed a radiant gleam upon the riverbank. Drawing nearer, he saw it was a single foreign silver coin. His heart stirred: *"How could there be lost silver here upon the bank? Surely Heaven does not yet wish me dead!"* Recalling that in Guangdong’s *biao* gambling halls, a single stake could yield thirtyfold, and having seen a shop notice announcing three such halls open that very day, he resolved to try his luck. If fortune favored him, he could devise a way home; if not, he would follow the example of the Third Grandee of Chu.

Having deemed this a sound plan, he hastened back to Foreign Merchants’ Street. There he chanced upon an innkeeper, whom he invited to accompany him, and won the stake. The innkeeper said, “Not far from here there is another game.” They went together and won again. Then they entered the city. Half a *li* from the inn, they passed a grand residence where they saw people entering in a steady stream. Drawing near to inquire, he learned that it too was a *biao* hall. He went in with the crowd, staked all he had, and won once more. Three victories in three games brought him over twenty thousand taels of silver—joy beyond all expectation. Returning to his lodging, he told the innkeeper, “I am a solitary, impoverished stranger, nearly reduced to a starved corpse. Thanks to your kindness, I have come by this fortune. I now intend to remain here rather than return east. If I can amass greater wealth, I will share it with you. What business would be best?” The innkeeper demurred, “Wealth comes by fate; how dare I claim credit for Heaven’s favor? If you wish to settle here, two foreign sundries shops are about to change hands. For twenty thousand taels you could acquire one and turn a handsome profit.” The old man followed his advice, entrusting the entire sum to the innkeeper, who proved honest and reliable. Beyond the two sundries shops, he also engaged in *juji*—buying low and selling high—so that every venture yielded severalfold returns. In less than a decade, he amassed hundreds of thousands of taels and came to know every foreign merchant in Guangdong. By nature a man of utmost sincerity, he dealt with others without guile or reserve, never delayed in keeping his word, and was clear-minded and decisive in affairs. Whenever someone faced urgent need, he never refused aid, and foreign merchants all held him in high regard.

That year, foreign goods flooded the market at rock-bottom prices, and every foreign trading house suffered sluggish sales. A certain Guan-family trading house incurred heavy losses, owing hundreds of thousands of taels to its clients. Two foreign merchants from this house had shipped four vessels of goods to Guangdong, but the cargo had languished unloaded for months. Then word arrived from their home country, urging their swift return. They wished to consign their goods to the trading house but feared embezzlement. After deliberation, they concluded that only the old man’s integrity could be trusted. They consulted the comprador, a man named So-and-So who ran a shop next to the old man’s and had once been imprisoned for defaulting on official debts. The authorities pressed him relentlessly, his flesh torn and bloodied, until he was on the verge of selling his wife and daughters to settle the debt. Someone had praised his daughter to the old man, who, upon hearing this, paid the entire sum to clear the official arrears. So-and-So presented the deed and brought his daughter to offer her in gratitude, but the old man tore up the deed and sent the girl home, reuniting the family. So-and-So was moved to the marrow, yet found no means to repay such kindness. When he heard the merchants’ plight, he eagerly urged them to seek the old man’s help, and so they came to his shop to speak with him.

The old man exclaimed in surprise, "Your four ships’ cargo is of immense value—even if I liquidated my entire estate, it would not cover a tenth of it. If there should be any loss, how could I ever repay you?" The foreign merchants replied, "Simply take the goods. Come three years later to collect the payment—how would that be?" Though the old man possessed considerable wealth at the time, he had yet to establish a household. Hearing the three-year term, he frowned and said, "I have no family here. Though I would not, in good conscience, betray another’s trust, the uncertainties of life are beyond prediction. Should some unforeseen misfortune arise within these three years, where would the two of you seek recompense?" Seeing that he could not be persuaded, the merchants turned to their former lodging-house keeper, who managed the old man’s accounts and was trusted by the foreigners, urging him to mediate. After appraising the goods, they valued them at over a million taels, of which only a hundred thousand were paid upfront, the remainder due in three years. A contract was drawn, and the transaction was concluded.

Within two months, war broke out in the West, and no foreign ships arrived, causing the price of imported goods to soar. The old man sold his stock, turning a threefold profit. He consulted with the lodging-house keeper to set aside the principal, using the rest for further trade. His wealth grew daily, and the great families of Guangdong vied to marry into his household. He took a wife, acquired property, and lived in a manner rivaling princes and marquises. Yet he remained ever more sincere, cautious, and generous, bestowing his bounty widely so that many benefited from his kindness.

Five years later, the foreign merchants finally returned. Overjoyed at their arrival, the old man prepared a lavish feast with music and performances, inviting all who had signed the contract and seating the merchants in the place of honor. After several rounds of wine, he raised his cup and declared, "Thanks to the goods from the two of you, I now possess millions. Without your generosity, I could never have attained this. My sales ledgers and trading records from these years are all here. Beyond the original capital, I ask that we divide the profits." The merchants laughed and said, "We had an agreement from the start—profit or loss rests with your fortune and fate, what concern is it of ours? Return our principal, and keep the rest for yourself; do not decline!" The old man insisted otherwise, and they exchanged deferential refusals. The mediator then said to the merchants, "Since Master Liu is so gracious, why not accept interest at one percent per annum on the original sum? It would be only right, and would not disappoint his goodwill." The merchants still demurred.

After several days of deliberation, they ultimately followed the mediator’s suggestion, fully repaying both principal and interest. At once, the foreign merchants all praised Old Man Liu for his sincerity and generosity.

Before long, the Guan trading house collapsed due to insolvency and was sued by the foreign merchants. The authorities sealed its premises and sought a successor to take over, but none could be found. The foreign merchants unanimously recommended Old Man Liu, though he declined, saying, “A foreign trading house requires vast capital—my funds are scattered, how could I reopen it?” The merchants replied, “No matter! Simply register your name with the officials, and we will provide the capital.” Reluctantly, he agreed.

After Old Man Liu took over the house, the foreign merchants flocked to him. In less than a decade, he became the wealthiest man in the province. He lived to over ninety, witnessing the birth of his great-grandchildren. Even now, his family remains one of great prominence.

The Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarked: “As the world’s customs decline daily, cunning deceit grows while honesty wanes. In dealings with others, not a single word comes from the heart—schemes and treachery shift in an instant, a thousandfold. Even ministers deceive their lords, children deceive their fathers, wives deceive their husbands, until the world becomes a realm of artifice. Those who practice such things believe themselves clever, unaware that they provoke Heaven’s wrath! Yet when one or two men of perfect sincerity appear, spirits and gods secretly aid them, smoothing perilous paths as if level ground, until they ultimately enjoy blessings unmatched in the human world. Men like Old Man Liu are truly worthy of emulation!”

**20. Washing the Inkstone in the Lotus Pond**

The intimacy with a maidservant by the lotus pond was already detestable—
how much worse to stubbornly deny it before one’s mother-in-law!
Too little time had passed for repentance, and the old ways returned;
in sickness, the demand for her life was entirely justified.

There was a certain scholar from Hangzhou in Zhejiang, a *zhusheng*. He studied the techniques of Shen Buhai and Han Fei under Jiang Yiting, possessing minor talent but given to wild and unrestrained behavior. In the year *bingwu*, Master Jiang took up a post in the yamen of Prefect Xianyun Ya in Shanghai. After the autumn examinations, the scholar visited his teacher at the circuit office and proudly displayed his examination essays to his peers.

At that time, a spirit-writing session was being held in the yamen, and the planchette descended, identifying itself as Master Xia Wanru. The scholar inquired about his official prospects, and the spirit wrote in large characters: *“Your future holds great promise, but alas, your sins of speech and lust have exhausted all merit! Reform swiftly, and you may yet prolong your years. Otherwise, vengeful spirits will soon arrive—do you still hope for scholarly success?”*

The scholar laughed and said, *“Does a celestial being now speak like some pedantic old scholar? If you speak of vengeful spirits, pray tell—what is the cause?”* The planchette wrote again: *“Do you truly wish for plain words? Do you recall the matter of washing your inkstone in the lotus pond ten years ago?”* The scholar’s countenance changed at once, and he kowtowed in silent prayer.

The planchette wrote once more: *“The Underworld has reported to the Osmanthus Palace to strike your name from the rolls and reduce your allotted years—thus do I know of it. If from this moment you earnestly repent your past wrongs, you may yet salvage a fraction of your fate. Mere prayers will be of no avail!”*

The onlookers saw the scholar’s face turn ashen. When the planchette fell still, someone asked him what the immortal had revealed. The scholar sighed and said, *“Such frivolous and reckless conduct—how shamefully I have wronged both human and spirit! I tell you this now in all earnestness: may those with aspirations take me as a warning!”*

Earlier, the scholar had once studied at his aunt’s home. His aunt had a beautiful maidservant, whom he had long wished to seduce but never found the opportunity. One summer day, he carried his inkstone to the lotus pond to wash it. Just then, the maidservant arrived to gather lotuses. Seeing no one around, he began to jest and flirt with her. She did not resist too strongly, and they entered a small pavilion by the pond, where they became intimate. Thereafter, whenever they found a moment, they would meet, and before long, the maidservant conceived.

At year’s end, the tutor dismissed the school, and the scholar returned home. When he went to pay his New Year’s respects, his aunt invited him to stay the night. After the household had grown quiet, the maidservant suddenly appeared and said to him, *“I am deeply grateful for your affection. For three months now, my monthly courses have ceased. If you remain devoted to me, allowing me to serve you always as your concubine, it would be your greatest kindness. But if you intend to abandon me, I will not dare to resent it—only, I beg you to quickly find some good medicine to prevent my disgrace from being exposed. I would be forever grateful.”*

The scholar consoled her, saying, *“I have already told my mother of our bond and will ask my aunt for you. I will never do anything so heartless—do not worry.”* The maidservant wept in gratitude, and that night, they once more shared intimacy, unaware that the scholar had no intention of marrying her. Upon returning home, he completely ignored her and never again visited his aunt’s house. The maidservant waited day and night, but all news of him ceased.

Not long after, her belly began to swell, and her mistress noticed. Unable to endure the beatings and torture, she confessed the truth. The mistress, who had always favored the scholar, immediately sent for him and intended to give the maidservant to him in marriage. The scholar steadfastly denied it, saying, *“This wanton maid must have slept with someone else—how dare she defile my name!”* With that, he brushed off his sleeves and left. Believing his words, the mistress subjected the maidservant to even harsher restraints. With no way to prove her innocence, the maidservant hanged herself that very night. The scholar paid it no mind, never imagining that the immortal would uncover his deceit.

When he later recounted this to those who inquired, he devised a way to repent. Many urged him to humble himself and do good, and to invite eminent monks to perform rites for the maidservant’s salvation. The scholar nodded in agreement. From then on, his arrogance gradually diminished.

Yet before a month had passed, his old ways resurfaced. He spoke recklessly, distorting truth at will, and once more indulged in the pleasures of flowers and willows, casting the immortal’s warning entirely from his mind. The following year, he coughed up frenzied blood and died. As he lay dying, those tending to him all saw a woman with unbound hair standing at his bedside—surely the maidservant had come to claim his life.

The Master of the Flower Seat sighed: *“Alas! To wallow in the lotus pond of desire is to be struck from the register of the cassia palace. To forfeit a boundless future for the sins of lust and deceit—how utterly reckless! Having once been captivated by her beauty and led her astray, how could there have been no lingering affection in those entangled exchanges? Yet he sought rare remedies in the quiet of night, swearing oaths of betrayal. Cutting off all tender words in the empty valley, he became the vilest of scoundrels. Worse still, when the truth came to light and she endured the lash without end, the girl confessed all. Though her mistress bent to spare her, he twisted the tale to clear himself of blame and heap guilt upon her. This was not merely abandonment—it was as if he had slit her throat with his own hand! Ah, the pity of it!”*

His heart had perished long before—what need was there for ghosts and spirits to punish him!”

---

**21. Bao Xunquan: A Life Spared, a Verdict Reversed—How Could Host and Guest Share the Same Dream?**

The example of Heaven’s selection of talent shows that scholarly pursuits need not be pursued with such bitter toil.

My kinsman by marriage, Uncle Bao Xunquan, was in his youth enrolled among the scholars but later abandoned his studies. Mastering the techniques of Shen Buhai and Han Fei, he wandered Jiangxi for over a decade. Though the second month brought apricot blossoms and the eighth osmanthus—signs of the metropolitan and provincial examinations—he had long since cast them beyond consideration.

In the *xinhai* year of Daoguang’s reign, he served as a guest administrator in the Gan County yamen. There, several thieves had already been sentenced to death by the previous magistrate. After reviewing their case, Bao grew suspicious and urged the magistrate to re-examine the matter. It was revealed that they were innocent commoners, framed by the constables. Though he sought to pardon them, the military officers schemed for their release, and the magistrate’s orders were obstructed, nearly swayed by their machinations. Bao, however, argued forcefully on principle and ultimately secured their exoneration.

The following year, in the early days of the *wuzǐ* year, he suddenly dreamed of gazing upon a celestial roster bearing his name. Dismissing it as a springtime fancy, he thought no more of it. Two months later, he dreamed again of the same roster, and this time, someone beside him said, *“The heavenly list is fixed—you must return at once.”* He awoke and again paid it no mind. The very next day, the magistrate arrived to urge him to sit for the examinations.

Bao exclaimed in surprise, *“You and Xunquan have been friends for years—you must be well aware that he abandoned the examination essays long ago. Why do you suddenly speak thus?”* The magistrate smiled and replied, *“I am not unaware that the master has long embraced lofty ideals. Yet on the night before last, I dreamed I was burning incense at the Wenchang Temple when an attendant led me beneath the corridor. There, I saw a long roster hanging on the wall, filled with countless names, each grouped by province and prefecture. The attendant told me, ‘This is the autumn examination list for this year.’ I looked closely at where I stood and saw, inscribed above, ‘Ma of Zhejiang Province, first place,’ while the master’s name appeared among the thirty-some. I thought to myself, ‘The master has never sat for the examinations—how could he be listed?’ The attendant said, ‘This is because last year he resolved the case of the framed thieves; Heaven has specially rewarded him with a place in this examination.’”* Startled, he awoke.

The master, having heard these words and the exact numbers, found them to match his own dream, and though his heart could not remain unmoved, he still hesitated in doubt. As he had long been skilled in the art of *liuren* divination, he performed another casting and received an auspicious omen, which declared, “This augury foretells certain success. As for the travel expenses for your journey there and back, as well as half a year’s tuition fees, I shall bear them all myself—what say you?” Unable to refuse, Bao at last prepared his luggage and set off.

Yet having neglected his studies for so long, he could no longer compose the eight-legged essays. In the examination hall, all three of his compositions were written in *sanxing* style, and the examiners had already discarded them without recommendation. Then, by chance, the chief examiner, finding none of the recommended essays to be in *sanxing* form, insisted on selecting one to meet the quota. Searching through the discarded papers, he came upon Bao’s and, struck by its archaic cadence, ultimately chose it for success. The rank and number matched the dream exactly, though the top scholar was one Ma Yu, while Bao later achieved first rank in the *dati* selection and now serves as a magistrate in a certain county of Min.

Amidst the yamen’s shadows, bribes bought a shared fate—
then came the ghost, blood-drenched, from the bath of death.
Ill-gotten wealth exacts its due without fail;
the corrupt official’s line cut short—how piteous his plight.

Cao Zhiying of Huizhou served as magistrate in a certain Sichuan county. Among his cases was one where a nephew had murdered his uncle. The nephew, wealthy beyond measure, widely distributed bribes. He presented Cao with eight hundred taels of silver, and all confessions and documents were entrusted to the inner staff of the yamen for revision. As they edited the statements, a ghostly voice was heard outside the window, sighing in distress, yet the staff—having also received bribes—remained unmoved. The nephew ultimately escaped unscathed.

Years later, one of the yamen staff died first. At the moment of his death, he saw a ghost, drenched in blood, approaching.

The ghost declared, "You and Magistrate Cao took bribes, allowing the wicked nephew to slip through the net. Moreover, to secure his exoneration, you fabricated charges against me, leaving my grievance unredressed. Now I have obtained the Emperor of the Underworld’s leave!" With that, it raised its hands and throttled its own throat to death. Cao soon followed in death. Afterward, his family’s fortunes declined, leaving only a single son. Upon entering the Confucian school, the son suddenly died, and Cao’s line was thus extinguished.

---

**23. The Robber of Xuancheng**

Where could the jade flee—his schemes so deftly laid?
Wealth and fame both won, his calculations made.
Who could foresee the debt would come knocking at the door,
A bandit’s vengeance clasped tight to his core?

In Xuancheng, a notorious robber had long plundered with impunity, his gold vast and his gang numerous; none in the garrison dared challenge him. A certain prefect, stern and capable, upon assuming office for less than ten days, devised a plan to capture him. The robber offered a massive bribe, which the prefect discussed with his confidant. The confidant urged him not to accept, but the prefect smiled and said, "If I kill him, where could the jade go?" He took the bribe yet still executed the robber as planned. On the night the robber was put to death, the prefect’s gatekeeper saw the robber enter the residence, unchecked by shouts; though pursued, he vanished into the inner chambers. At dawn, the prefect’s concubine bore a son. When the boy grew up, he ruined the family’s fortune—all knew he had come to collect the robber’s debt.

---

**24. The Ghost Seizes the Remarried Woman**

Her husband’s murder—how lamentable the deed,
With stolen wealth, she fled to strum the pipa’s lead.
Not for remarriage should her life be spared,
Yet good and evil before the magistrate are hard to guard.

In front of the official residence at Caojing, there lived a woman from a local family. In her youth, she had an affair with a man named Jia, unbeknownst to her parents. A man named Yi, a native of Jinling who had drifted to Caojing for petty trade, married her and took her home. Jia, posing as a relative, frequently visited their household.

Not long after, Yi fell ill. Coveting his death, the woman abandoned him without care—medicines, food, tea, and water, fearing she could not provide them, he languished and died. She then took his wealth and remarried Jia.

A year later, she suddenly fell prey to malaria. With each bout, her former husband appeared to demand his life. Terrified, she secretly fled to her mother’s home, where the ghost never came, and her malaria soon abated. Several years passed, and she remained at her mother’s, too afraid to return. On the Qingming Festival, she went to the City God’s Temple to watch the procession. Upon her return, the malaria flared violently. Her speech became a murmuring babble, indistinguishable. Suspecting delirium from illness, her mother listened closely—her accent resembled that of a Jinling native. Struck by this, she asked, “Who are you?” The sick woman replied, “I am Yi!”
