"I suffered humiliation in life at her hands, and in death she took all my wealth to share with her adulterous lover in revelry. Two years ago, I sought to claim her life, but she fled in secret. After years of pursuit, I have finally found her—now I shall not spare her!" With these words, she clawed at her own flesh, leaving her skin torn and blood scattered in disarray.

Neighbors who had once advised her came forth, saying, "Even if you take her life, it will not restore yours. Moreover, this was no premeditated murder—better to invite eminent monks to perform rites for your deliverance." The ghost refused. They then offered to ensure unbroken sacrifices at every festival and season, but again, the ghost would not relent.

At the time, the woman’s son was already several years old. Her mother said, "What if we give this child to you as your own?" The ghost cried out harshly, "What use have I for this mongrel?" From then on, she would tear at the woman’s hair, slap her cheeks, or pinch her flesh. When medicine was brought, she would seize her throat to prevent her from swallowing. At other times, she would strip the woman bare and force her to kneel upon the ground.

Suddenly she would sing, then laugh, then rage and curse with howling shrieks. The torment was beyond all description, and the woman begged only for a swift death. Those who pitied her, when the ghost possessed her body, would ask, "You came to claim a life—why not let her die quickly?" The ghost replied, "When she once wore me down, she made me long for death yet could not have it. So now I make her endure this living torment. When the time comes, I will take her before the Great Magistrate for judgment!" This went on for half a month.

One afternoon, she was unusually calm, and it seemed the ghost had left her. But as dusk fell, her mother, inside the room, suddenly saw the ghost enter from outside, holding iron chains. In the blink of an eye, it vanished—only to possess the woman’s body once more. The ghost cried loudly, "Today the term is up! I’ll chain you and take you before the Great Magistrate!" With her own hands, she seized her throat and died.

Zuohua Master said: "Though a widow’s remarriage may incur a ghost’s resentment, it does not warrant vengeance—vengeance is reserved for those who first harbored murderous intent. During her illness, their bond was already in tatters, devoid of conjugal affection; after death, her hasty remarriage was as reckless as a quail’s flight, leaving behind the unmistakable traces of estrangement and solitude. The wound to the heart, the piercing of the bones—this was far crueler than if he had personally plunged a blade into her belly.

Though I did not kill Boren, Boren died because of me."

"A single phrase pierces the heart—thus is the verdict sealed for a thousand ages!"

---

**25. Silver Hidden in a Plum Tree**

A humble hut transformed in an instant to vermilion gates—
Yet rare is the tailor’s ancient virtue, steadfast and true.
Inscribed upon this painting, the cry of dismay resounds:
Who could have foreseen that a lowly script would mirror Wen Sun’s hand?

Han Shushan of Yunjian, ¹ a man of wealth who cherished propriety, acted without hesitation when righteousness demanded, earning the esteem of his village as a man of virtue. The tale of how he amassed his fortune offers a lesson worth heeding.

Shushan’s ancestors were poor. His father, Old Man Han, worked as a tailor, setting up shop west of Xiuye Bridge. Though his craft was humble, his kindness knew no bounds. One year, as the year’s end approached, a heavy snowfall blanketed the earth. ² At midnight, having finished his work, he was about to retire when suddenly the door knocker rattled violently, as if someone leaned against it. A sigh was heard. Holding up a candle, he opened the door to find a man seated there, clutching a bundle. Upon inquiry, he learned the stranger was a clerk from a Shanghai firm, returning from collecting debts in Zhapu. The hour was late, and he had missed both the last boat and any available lodging, intending to spend the night under the eaves until dawn.

Old Man Han exclaimed in alarm, "Since you’ve just collected debts, your purse cannot be empty—how can you sleep exposed to the elements? Even if nothing befalls you, how will you endure such bitter cold? Though my home is cramped and lowly, it can at least shield you from wind and snow." With that, he invited the man inside.

---

¹ *Shu* (漱), pronounced *shù*. Yunjian refers to Huating County.
² *Yu* (雨), pronounced *yù*, distinct from the word for "rain" in "wind and rain." According to the *Jiyun*, it denotes falling from above.

Seeing the man’s clothes and shoes soaked through, Old Man Han took out his own new garments from the past New Year and had him change into them. He then prepared wine and food, saying to his guest, “This is what I had readied for my hired hands today—just to ward off the cold. Do not think it too meager.” By then, the guest was racked by cold and hunger, barely able to endure; now, welcomed inside with such earnest courtesy, he was deeply moved and could not stop expressing his gratitude.

After the meal, Old Man Han arranged a bed and laid out bedding, and the guest at last settled in for the night. By dawn, the wind and snow had grown fiercer, and no boats could depart. The old man kept his guest, waiting for the skies to clear, providing meals and wine without the slightest sign of reluctance. That evening, the guest said to him, “I am deeply grateful for your noble kindness and have nothing with which to repay you. I’ve heard that grain is very cheap in Yunjian, and shipping it to Shanghai yields a handsome profit. I have surplus funds from my debt collection—allow me to lend you three hundred taels for trade.” The old man firmly declined with solemn dignity, and the guest nodded in acquiescence.

The next day, the wind died down, and Old Man Han arranged a boat for his guest, personally seeing him aboard. Just as the mooring rope was cast off, the guest called out to him again, “The three hundred taels I mentioned yesterday are under the bed. Return home and take them. I will await you at my firm during next year’s Lantern Festival.” The old man, startled, tried to return the money, but the boat had already set sail. With no other choice, he went home to find the gold indeed beneath the guest’s bed. He took it for the time being, and when the New Year arrived, he used it all to purchase grain and transport it to Shanghai.

Upon inquiring at the firm, Old Man Han encountered the guest from before emerging from within. The guest clapped his hands upon seeing him and exclaimed, “You are truly a man of your word!” The old man informed him that he had purchased a certain quantity of grain and transported it there. The guest then accompanied him inside to meet the firm’s proprietor, announcing, “This is the very Mr. Han of Yun Jian whom I met last year—he has brought the grain as promised!” The proprietor expressed his gratitude, saying, “My associate carried a large sum of money and lodged in the open, and but for your noble virtue, he might have met with misfortune. Now you have arrived on time, refusing to take advantage of the opportunity for gain—you are truly a man of antiquity in this modern age!” The old man demurred in modest refusal. The proprietor ordered the main hall to be opened and invited the old man inside, where a lavish feast was prepared as if for an honored guest. After the meal, the former guest was instructed to accompany him on an outing, and upon their return, the old man’s luggage had already been brought to his quarters, where he was invited to stay.

The next morning, the old man presented the grain purchase ledger to the guest, instructing him to unload the rice ashore, as he intended to return to Yun Jian. The guest smiled and said, “The matter of the grain has already been arranged. Stay a few more days—do not rush to leave.” Each day, the guest accompanied him on outings, sparing him from loneliness. After several days, the proprietor once again hosted a banquet, seating the old man in the place of honor. He addressed him, saying, “The grain you brought has already been sold at a considerable profit. I now wish to entrust you with an even greater sum to transport goods on my behalf, and you may take half of the profits earned.” With that, he placed a large ingot of gold before the old man, saying, “This is your rightful share of the profits.” The old man declined at first but ultimately accepted. He then said to the proprietor, “Since you have entrusted me with this task, I shall do my utmost. However, I have a humble request—whether it may be granted, I do not know.” The proprietor replied, “I would be glad to hear it.” The old man said, “I have heard that those who do good will prosper. I wish to set aside two-tenths of the profits to relieve the poor and, wherever there is a worthy cause, to contribute to it. Yet, as I am but a poor man’s son, and the gold comes from you, I dare not act without your permission.” The proprietor agreed and immediately advanced two thousand taels of silver to the old man.

From then on, the old man redoubled his efforts in doing good, and the goods he transported always yielded substantial profits. Within a few years, his wealth grew considerably. He purchased a new residence near Xiu Nan Bridge and sold off its furnishings. As his family prepared to move in, the old man entered the house first to sweep and clean. He noticed a desk drawer filled with scraps of paper and, fearing they might be mistreated, placed a brazier on the floor. As he sorted through them, burning each in turn, he came upon an old notebook resembling a diary, on which was written: *“If you need silver, it lies beneath the plum tree.”* Finding this strange, he looked around but saw no plum tree in the house. Suspecting it to be a jest, he set the notebook aside without burning it.

Behind the house stood several rooms on an upper floor, their staircase rotted and dilapidated. As he removed the old steps to replace them with new ones, the old man—accustomed to frugality and labor—personally took up a broom to sweep beneath the stairs. Suddenly, he noticed a painting of a plum tree on the wall, its branches slanting gracefully, its blossoms and hues seeming to sway. The words in the notebook came to mind, and he realized there must be a hidden cache below. That evening, he gathered his family and, holding lanterns, went to investigate.

Beneath the plum tree, he saw that the ground was paved with flat stone slabs, which shifted easily when shaken. Exerting all his strength, he lifted them away. Below lay four massive jars, filled to the brim with gold and silver, and thus he became immensely wealthy. From that time onward, the old man redoubled his virtuous deeds, and his descendant Shushan upheld the family teachings, engaging in every form of goodness. Shushan’s son, Luoqing, has since passed the provincial examination, while his other sons and grandsons have gained renown in the academies. The rewards they have reaped are boundless.

The Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarked: *“During my brief travels in Songjiang, I heard the story of Old Man Han in great detail. Though he was not deeply learned in books, his conduct was of the highest integrity. His words always came from the heart, and his actions were never feigned. His love of goodness and joy in giving were entirely sincere, without the slightest trace of reluctance—truly embodying the spirit of one who sees the drowning and starving as if they were his own.”*

Thus, though his arts were humble, Heaven suddenly bestowed upon him boundless wealth. People saw only how easily he obtained it, unaware that what brought him to this was no mere accident.

---

**26. Uprightness as Divinity**
*A spotless official becomes a spirit—his governance unerring,*
*Sweet melons and hemp flourish where he treads.*
*He scoffs at the Daoist’s idle ease,*
*Yet in dreams, he startles awake to meet Magistrate Xu.*

Xu Yunián was my maternal grandaunt’s brother. A man of vast learning, skilled in poetry and painting, his nature was forthright and unguarded, rushing to aid others in distress as if he could never do enough. He cherished talent above all, and upon witnessing even a single virtue in a person, he would praise them without cease.

Having passed the provincial examination in the *xinsi* year of the Daoguang era, he was appointed magistrate of Huan County in Gansu, later transferred to Dunhuang and promoted to prefect of Anxi. All the places he governed lay at the farthest frontiers. Their people were honest and simple, free from the cunning and litigious habits of the interior. Master Xu governed them with quiet dignity. Lawsuits were judged and settled the same day, with no cases left unresolved. He maintained the strictest personal integrity, and in his spare time, he would summon the most promising scholars to instruct them in composition and refine their literary skills.

Observing the abundance of mulberry trees in the region, he specially hired silkworm-rearing women from his hometown to teach the locals the methods of raising silkworms and reeling silk. Wherever he went, voices of praise arose, and when he departed, the people enshrined and worshipped him as a deity.

He had barely reached fifty when he died of illness in the official residence of Anxi. Earlier, during his tenure as magistrate of Dunhuang, there was a Daoist in the City God Temple who frequently broke the law. Master Xu expelled him from the county. After Xu left office, the Daoist, through underhanded connections, regained his position as temple abbot and resumed his misconduct as before.

One morning, the Daoist awoke, suddenly rolled up his belongings, and attempted to flee in a state of great panic.

Someone inquired about the matter, to which the Daoist replied: "Last night after falling asleep, I dreamed I heard the shouts of attendants and the blare of ceremonial music. Stepping out to look, I saw the new City God arriving for his post, his retinue in perfect order. As I stood peering from the side, I suddenly heard a summons from the hall: 'Seize Daoist So-and-so at once!' Two underlings bound me and dragged me before the City God. Looking up, I saw it was none other than the former magistrate, Master Xu. He thundered in rebuke: 'I once expelled you from this jurisdiction—having observed my departure and sneaked back, you should have conducted yourself properly. Instead, you persisted in your wickedness without repentance. Today I ought to hasten your death, but out of consideration for my recent assumption of office, I shall mete out a light punishment!' He immediately issued a writ and had me flogged. Then he commanded: 'Leave this temple today—do not linger here to court death!' Whereupon the bailiffs drove me out."

As I stumbled down the steps and awoke, the pain in both thighs was unbearable—I dare not stay here any longer!" Thereupon he gathered his belongings and staggered away in haste.

At that time, the people of Dunhuang still did not know of the master’s passing. Later, upon inquiry, it was learned that the day the Daoist saw the master assume office was the very day he passed away in Anxi, his mat exchanged for the final one. Truly, when one is upright, they become a spirit—how could this not be so?

The master’s eldest son, Yanzhi, was married to my paternal cousin and served as a magistrate in eastern Guangdong. His second son, Yuanzhong, currently holds office in Taizhou, Jiangsu, and has earned a reputation for good governance.

The master’s third son, Runquan, and fifth son, Yejin, both passed the provincial examinations in succession and served as officials in the Ministry bureaus. This shows how enduring the master’s legacy has been.

The Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarked: "During the eight years the master served as magistrate, every post he held was in the barren and impoverished borderlands. Yet he disciplined himself strictly, living without extravagance—his meals never included more than one dish, and he kept no concubines. From his modest salary, he still managed to provide for his relatives. Each year, without fail, he would remit funds from afar before the New Year, sending anywhere from several taels to dozens, adjusting the amount according to the closeness of kinship. Dozens of households among his kin relied on these gifts to endure the winter and celebrate the New Year. His integrity and self-restraint, along with his reputation for good governance, are still recounted by the people of Ganzhou and Liangzhou to this day. That he became a spirit after death, and that his descendants have risen to prominence—is this not only fitting?"

**27. Two Cases of Disaster Assessment**

*Diligence and caution still fail to ensure real relief—*
*How much worse when tasks are left to clerks and underlings?*
*Both lives cut short, yet spare no pity—*
*The official’s warrant was no summons of death.*

County Assistant Magistrate So-and-so awaited his post in Jiangsu. Assigned to act as Assistant Magistrate of Fengxian County, he was placed in charge of the Fourth Tuan Township. At the time, the previous official had died of illness, and his coffin still remained in the yamen. The assistant magistrate, bringing his family, temporarily lodged in the City God’s Temple.

That year, the county suffered flooding, and both tax exemptions and disaster relief were implemented. The prefectural government issued an order for him to survey the disaster-stricken households in his township. Coincidentally, the Provincial Administration Commissioner had also sent an official to the area. The two men, being old acquaintances, soon arranged to live together. Day after day, they indulged in drinking and singing, entrusting the task entirely to clerks, village heads, and local constables. These underlings colluded in fraud, engaging in embezzlement and false claims, so that the impoverished people received no real benefit.

Before long, the assistant magistrate and his wife died suddenly without illness, one after the other. The commissioner’s delegate returned to the provincial capital to report on his mission and died within a month. The assistant magistrate was not yet forty, the age of vigorous service, and had no serious faults—yet he met such a grim retribution. People suspected that his family’s residence in the temple had profaned the deity, provoking divine wrath. But observing the cause of his death, one sees that the netherworld’s punishment lay here, not there!

Again, in the *gengyin* year of the Daoguang reign (1830), the region north of the Yangtze suffered a great famine.

The local authorities petitioned for relief and aid, but the number of registered households seemed suspiciously high, raising doubts in the governor’s mind. He therefore ordered the Jiangsu provincial treasurer to select ten capable and upright officials from among the assistant magistrates and clerks of the prefectures and counties, who would join the local officials in conducting a reexamination. Those chosen for this task were considered the finest of their generation. Yet all followed the governor’s intent, insisting on strict scrutiny. The relief did not reach all in need, and the savings from the disaster fund amounted to tens of thousands of taels.

Only Magistrate Zheng Zujing and one other conducted their reexamination with leniency, thereby offending the governor and forfeiting promotion. Seven of the ten, however, were commended for their meticulous work and received imperial rewards. The following year, all seven died suddenly without illness. Zheng, in recognition of his earlier service in maritime transport, was promoted from vice-magistrate of Nanhui to magistrate of Jiangdu. One of his sons entered the Imperial Secretariat as a *xiaolian* scholar, and the colleague who had joined him in the reexamination remained unharmed.

Another Case of Disaster Verification
During the *gengyin* year of the Daoguang reign, the region north of the Yangtze suffered a severe drought. The local authorities memorialized for relief and aid.

In the twenty-eighth case, a single thunderclap struck three men—
Beating their mother and stealing silver only heightened their crimes.
The vast net of Heaven is ever inescapable;
Sure enough, all three met retribution as if sharing a tripod’s fate.
Before the Confucian Temple’s gates lay the marketplace’s bustle.

In Suzhou, there was a certain Jia who habitually disobeyed and abused his mother, subjecting her to scorn, curses, and beatings. There was also a widow who had saved over a hundred taels of silver, intending to deposit it with a shop to earn interest and sustain her daily life. This was noticed by a certain Yi and a certain Bing, who stole the silver and divided it between themselves. The widow, having lost her livelihood, died in grief. Though all knew Yi and Bing to be the culprits, none dared speak out for fear of their lawlessness. Meanwhile, Jia’s mother was also tormented to death by her son.

All three men served as attendants to the provincial governor. In the summer of the *renyin* year, the barbarian threat loomed ominous. The imperial troops marched from Zhejiang to Suzhou, and the local authorities established a military supply bureau at Canglang Pavilion, adjacent to the prefectural Confucian Temple. Governor Li, arriving on official business, allowed his attendants and servants to rest beneath the great trees before the temple gates.

At that moment, a single crimson sun hung in the sky, vast and cloudless for ten thousand *li*. Suddenly, wind and clouds churned in fury, thunder and lightning surged forth—then, with a single deafening crack of thunder, Jia, Yi, and Bing were struck dead simultaneously.

**Nineteen: The Theft of Silver and Heaven’s Retribution**
*Diligent needlework, heartbroken toil—*
*Years of labor amassed silver in the scores.*
*How could you steal and collude with another?*
*Does Heaven betray the earnest and the poor?*

There was also an elderly widow, a certain Madam Shi, who lived alone, supporting her orphaned child through needlework. When her son grew older, he took up accounting, and his wages were enough to provide for his mother. Yet Madam Shi continued her needlework as before, and so she gradually saved a considerable sum—several tens of taels of silver—which she intended to use for her son’s marriage. Their humble home was cramped, separated from the neighbor’s house by only a single wooden partition. Whenever Madam Shi went out, fearing the loss of her silver, she would bind it to her waist.

One day, she went to Yuanmiao Temple to burn incense. Hearing that the temple was rife with pickpockets, she untied the bundle from her waist and entrusted it to a certain Jia, an acquaintance who ran a rice shop, for safekeeping. When she returned from her prayers to retrieve the silver, Jia’s face changed color. “Who took your silver?” he demanded. The widow was stunned; wailing and weeping, she argued her case. Jia swore to Heaven, calling down curses to prove his innocence. As the two quarreled, a crowd gathered around them, but none could settle the dispute.

When Madam Shi had handed over the silver, a certain Bing, a neighbor of the shop, had witnessed the exchange and was still present in the shop at the time. She pointed to him as a witness, but Bing sneered, “If that’s true, then you’ve truly seen a ghost! I’ve just arrived from Chang Gate—I haven’t even seen your face before. How would I know whether you two are telling the truth?” Upon hearing Bing’s words, the crowd erupted in uproar, all siding against Madam Shi.

The widow, unable to clear her name, returned home in despair and hanged herself. When her son came home and prepared her for burial, he knew nothing of the cause of her death, and in his grief fell gravely ill. As he lay bedridden, he dreamed his mother appeared to him, saying, “Tomorrow at the market square, two men will be struck by lightning—my wrongs will be redressed. The original silver will be returned to you. Why not rise, despite your illness, and go to witness it?” The next day, the son indeed rose, weak as he was, and went.

The sun had just reached its zenith when the sky was suddenly overcast, the wind howled, and thunder crashed violently. Jia and Bing, each clutching a bundle of silver, knelt facing each other and were struck dead. Bing soon revived and told the crowd, “That day, after the old woman entrusted her silver to Jia, he schemed to embezzle it and offered me a thirty-seventy split. We never expected to incur Heaven’s wrath together. The underworld authorities, deeming Jia the instigator, spared me from the death penalty and ordered me to confess before all. They also commanded that the original silver be returned to the widow’s son, but I do not know his name—what am I to do?” At that moment, someone familiar with the matter pointed to the son and said, “Is this not the rightful owner?” The son then claimed the silver before the crowd and returned home. He placed it before his mother’s spirit tablet and wept bitterly, after which his illness vanished as if lifted.

Bing lay bedridden for half a year before he could rise, one arm and one leg broken, crippled for life.

30. Sealing Silver with Red Lacquer
A humble trade in fish he plied,
Yet ever quick to aid the needy side.

Amidst auspicious omens, true treasure found—
Seizing the moment, he sealed silver with red lacquer.

The Yu family of Jiaxing was a great household of the prefecture, owning many properties. A certain Zhu, who made his living selling fish, rented one of their houses to live in. The house stood beside the prefectural yamen, part of the dowry of Madam Yu the Elder. Each year’s end, the old lady would send an elderly woman or a maidservant to collect the rent—not entrusting the task to servants or stewards, so there was no harassment or clamor. Zhu found this convenient and lived in peace, and so it had been for years.

Though Zhu was but a small peddler, his nature was generous and open-hearted, ever ready to aid those in distress. Once in the marketplace, he came upon a man and woman with a child of about ten, weeping together so piteously that passersby were moved. Zhu asked what was wrong, and they replied that they were from Huai’an. Their home had been ruined in a flood, and they had drifted here. Hearing that this year’s harvest was abundant, they wished to return but lacked the means. They had considered selling the wife, but her resolve could not be shaken; selling the child, yet their hearts could not bear parting. At a loss, with hunger gnawing at them, they could only cling to one another and weep. Zhu asked how much they needed to bring wife and child home. “Just two thousand coins would suffice,” they said. Zhu immediately took them home and gave them the full amount. People all mocked him, but such acts of kindness were common for him.

At year’s end, the custom in Jiaxing was to make offerings to the gods. People would often purchase paper silver ingots and place them in baskets. Two men would carry them inside, feigning great effort as if barely able to bear the weight, and say, “Today we’ve unearthed a hidden treasure!” This was taken as an omen of wealth and abundance in the coming year. Yet only the common folk of the streets and alleys practiced this; scholar-official families disdained it and were unaware of it.

That year, as Zhu and his wife had just finished their offerings, Lady Yu’s rent-collecting maidservant arrived. The couple invited her to drink, saying, “The rites are now complete, and the God of Wealth has just come—next year we shall surely prosper. Please drink this *sanfu* wine, and take these silver ingots with you. When you return, present them truthfully to the Lady, and she will not reproach you.”

The maidservant laughed and said, “I have already come twice! The first time was just when you, good sir and madam, were ‘unearthing the treasure’—I dared not disturb you. When I returned and told the Lady, she remarked that at seventy years of age, she had seen gold, jewels, and jade in great abundance, yet never a buried silver ingot. She wished to beg one to refresh her eyes and ears. That is why I have come again, and I shall return it promptly—pray do not begrudge it.”

Zhu smiled and said, “This is but a rustic New Year’s custom, not a true discovery of buried treasure. Where would we find silver to present to the Lady?”

The maidservant’s face darkened with anger. “How truly petty and mean! Would the Lady deceive you over a single silver ingot? You make excuses to refuse me!”

Zhu and his wife vehemently denied having any silver, but the maidservant grew furious. “This house belongs to the Lady, and the buried silver came from it—how dare you claim it as your own? I shall report this to the Lady, and she will send her steward to question you!” With that, she brushed her sleeves and strode off.

Zhu and his wife stared at each other in astonishment. After a short while, the maidservant returned with a message, carrying two silver ingots. She offered them to Zhu, saying, “The Lady knows that those who unearth buried treasure must not use it hastily, for hasty use leads to swift depletion. She now offers two ingots in exchange for one, as an omen that your future business ventures will yield double the profit. Pray do not refuse again.”

Zhu was about to speak further, but his wife signaled him with her eyes and said to the maidservant, “Since the Lady has so graciously instructed us, how dare we continue to conceal it? But first, please drink a cup of wine, and we shall fetch it for you.” She then beckoned her husband outside and handed him a silver ingot, saying, “Quickly melt some sealing wax and mix it with mud to coat it.”

Following her instructions, the ingot turned a deep, dull black, its surface mottled with earthen hues, so that at a glance it appeared to have come straight from an underground vault.

The maidservant was handed the silver, and she said, “We dare not accept the Lady’s principal, but since custom forbids refusal, we shall temporarily receive it, pending your further instructions.” Delighted, the maidservant took it away. Soon she returned, saying, “The Lady has kept the silver to show her descendants! She commands that these two treasures and this year’s rent be given as gifts instead.” The Zhu couple were overjoyed beyond their wildest hopes. Having unexpectedly gained fifty taels of silver, they abandoned the fish trade and prepared to open a small sundries shop for their livelihood. Taking the treasures bestowed by Madam Yu, they drew up a list and went to the silver merchant to appraise them. The shopkeeper, a clansman of Madam Yu’s, saw them and smiled, saying, “With such a propitious star over you, how can you still be doing such petty business?” He returned their silver, gave them ten times the goods in exchange, and would not hear their refusal. Considering the calculation most favorable, they then leased a separate storefront, chose an auspicious day, and opened for business on a grand scale. From the opening day, depositors, investors, and partners arrived in an unbroken stream, overwhelming them with demands. Without spending a single coin, everything fell into place. The goods they stocked yielded profits several times over. Within a few years, their wealth equaled that of the Yu family.

The Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarks: “This Zhu, a mere peddler, was yet able to aid others in distress so generously. His breadth of mind—how could petty, narrow men ever glimpse his shadow? Heaven specially lent the hand of the Yu family to enrich him, causing the young maid’s eyes to blur and mistake paper money for silver. It further led the venerable matriarch to forget herself, regarding her family treasures as mere windfalls. What began as an auspicious omen swiftly became true wealth. The man was extraordinary, and his fortune even more so. Compared to Old Man Han’s plum tree concealing silver, does this not surpass all expectation?”

---

**31. Seven Cases of Reaping Retribution for Capturing Bandits**

To be a notorious bandit is to deserve the blade;
To scheme beyond one’s bounds is to forfeit life.
A warning to all ambitious men of rank:
Beware the day you cross paths with Nine-Catty Wang.

Many who capture bandits rise in office,
Yet wrongs go unredressed, grievances unheard.
Who could have known that, once his triumph faded,
His son would lose his wits, his line be severed?

Father and son, eager to display their prowess, seized a mighty brigand,
Greed for merit led them to record the bandit’s name.
Who could have foreseen the brave constable would vanish into a fish’s belly?
The verdict sealed, they met a sudden, violent end.

Magistrate’s Clerk Zhao Wen, a man of Li County, was bold and capable but restless and impatient. Considering his post a dead end with no path to promotion, he believed that capturing bandits from neighboring jurisdictions was his surest route to advancement. He spared no expense, hiring informants and pursuing fugitives, netting several arrests—though none were ringleaders.

Now, there was a certain Nine-Catty Wang, a notorious bandit of Zhejiang Province. When Li learned of his whereabouts, he secretly reported to his superiors, obtained a warrant, and crossed into another jurisdiction to apprehend him. Wang confessed at once upon interrogation and was executed according to the law. Li, having captured a major bandit from a neighboring province, was summoned to the capital for an audience. On his way, he stopped in the provincial capital to await his credentials. While lodging at an inn, a fellow official awaiting appointment approached him for a loan. Li entered his room, opened his trunk to retrieve silver, but found the lock jammed. Pressed by the borrower, he wrenched the lock with such force that the contents inside shifted violently. When the trunk finally sprang open, a knife clattered to the floor. Enraged, Li stamped his foot, his shoe flying off, and stomped barefoot upon the ground, shrieking wildly before collapsing.

At that very moment, Subprefect Wang Juru, who was also awaiting appointment in the provincial capital and was Li’s closest friend, happened to be staying at the same inn.

---

Wang hastened to his side and saw that the knife which had leapt out had pierced straight through the sole of Li’s foot and emerged from the instep. He swiftly pulled it free, and blood gushed forth like a spring. Supporting Li onto the bed, he summoned a wound physician, who applied medicine to staunch the bleeding and ease the pain. Though Li could not walk, he ate, drank, spoke, and laughed as usual. Wang stayed with him until evening before returning to his own lodgings.

The next morning, Wang went to Li’s residence, where a servant greeted him and said, “After my master fell asleep, his spirit seemed unsettled. All night, he muttered incessantly—we could not make out what he was saying.” Wang hurried into the room to see Li propped up on his pillow. Upon Wang’s arrival, Li did not stir, and his speech was disjointed. Suspecting illness, Wang urged him to return to his post to recuperate, saying, “Why not go back to your office and rest for a few days before returning to Suzhou?”

Li glared at him and said, “I am a man of Zhejiang—they should arrest me in Zhejiang, not in Jiangsu! If you want me to go, send me back to Zhejiang!”

Li was from Shandong, yet his speech now resembled the dialect of the Jiaxing-Huzhou region—Wang thus knew he was possessed by the spirit of Nine-Pounds Wang. Wang then prepared a boat and sent him back to Zhaowen. Even as he departed, Li repeated to Wang, “If you want me to go back, send me to Zhejiang.” Wang gave a vague reply, then had Li’s family help him into the sedan chair. Only after seeing the boat set sail did Wang return home. Within three days, news of Li’s death arrived.

In Kuaiji, a certain Shi had initially served as a patrol inspector in Guangdong. After repeatedly capturing foreign pirates, he was promoted out of turn to the rank of magistrate and assigned to several demanding posts. Later, he was dismissed from office during the triennial evaluation. His youngest son had always been remarkably bright, earning the epithet “a colt of a thousand *li*.” After Shi returned home, his young son suddenly fell into a state of spirit-induced madness.

Day after day, he knelt and bowed toward the wall, repeating, “This case has nothing to do with me. I had no part in the public affairs of Guangdong.” These two phrases were all he would mutter. When his father questioned him, he would glare and say, “It’s all your fault!” In the end, he died in his madness. Soon after, both Shi and his wife passed away, and the family scattered like stars.

Prefectural Adjutant Wang Boyang—*Adjutant being the title of a vice-prefect*—was serving as acting magistrate of Shanghai. He hired local militiamen to pursue pirates at sea. Among them were a father and son named Zhou, both fierce and skilled in combat. Every time they ventured out, they returned with captives. One day, they reported the capture of twelve major pirates, along with their boat and crew. The Adjutant was overjoyed and immediately interrogated them. The prisoners confessed to being salt smugglers from Fujian and Guangdong. The boat contained several thousand taels of silver, all profits from salt trafficking, with no other stolen goods. Enraged by their refusal to admit to piracy, Wang ordered them subjected to severe torture.

The three wooden restraints were applied in combination—*three restraints being instruments of torture, as in the *Records of the Grand Historian*: "He was clad in a red prison garment and bound with three restraints," referring to shackles for the neck, hands, and feet, equivalent to modern cangues and fetters.* All confessed to being pirates. Yet whether their confessions were true or not could never be known!

The day after the capture, Zhou’s son, seeking to retrieve an item he had left aboard the naval vessel, set out to reclaim it. The warship was too large to enter the harbor, so smaller boats were used to ferry men to and fro. Zhou’s son piloted a lighter to the warship’s side, and in a display of bravado, leapt aboard. After retrieving his belongings, he leapt back down—only for the lighter to be swept away by a sudden crosswind. He lost his footing and fell into the sea. Though rescue was attempted, the tide was ebbing, and he was carried away with the current; neither he nor his body was ever recovered.

The Adjutant, still oblivious, proceeded to close the case as one of major piracy. The prisoners were sent to the provincial capital and executed in pairs. Meanwhile, the Adjutant was promoted for his "exemplary efficiency in apprehending criminals," and the Zhou militiamen were posthumously awarded the rank of garrison commander.

Yet scarcely three days after the imperial edict arrived, the Adjutant died suddenly without illness, his final moments marked by what seemed to be a vision. Though all knew the case to be a miscarriage of justice, none dared speak out. Still, the Dao’s retribution was not amiss!

Between the years of *Yisi* and *Bingwu* in the Daoguang reign, a major case of banditry and official murder occurred in Jiangnan. The culprits remained at large for some time, prompting the higher authorities to offer a substantial reward for their capture. Soon after, reports surfaced that the bandits were hiding in the Li River region north of the Yangtze. The provincial governor and judicial commissioner dispatched an Adjutant and a magistrate to coordinate with local officials in a rigorous manhunt.

Within two months, over twenty suspects were reported captured. Subjected to severe torture, all bowed their heads and confessed. They were executed in pairs in the marketplace. Yet public opinion did not deem the verdict just.

Yet the Adjutant and the magistrate were ultimately rewarded by higher authorities for their success in capturing the bandits. Within two years, the magistrate was assigned to temporarily govern a county in Changzhou. Having assumed office for only seven days, he suddenly died without prior illness, appearing at the moment of death as though he had seen something. The following year, the Adjutant was promoted to prefect of Xuzhou and received his commission to take up the post. Upon reaching Yuanpu, he too died suddenly without illness, also seeming to see something at the time of his death. Both men were in the prime of their careers—barely forty years of age—yet they perished in quick succession, each at the moment of assuming his new post in high spirits. The public found this extraordinary and attributed it to the unredressed injustices of the earlier case.

A certain Military Advisor Jiang, eager to secure a magistracy, befriended a magistrate of Huating who had captured several bandits. The Huating magistrate handed over the prisoners to Jiang and petitioned for his reward. However, the higher authorities found the number of prisoners insufficient to meet the regulations for promotion. Jiang then spent his own money to purchase two more bandits to complete his tally. He was subsequently issued credentials and sent to the capital for an imperial audience, after which he received an edict appointing him as a magistrate. No sooner had he arrived at his lodgings than he suddenly collapsed to the ground. When helped to his feet, his speech was wildly incoherent, as though quarreling and cursing with someone unseen. He died before midnight.

A certain Chief Clerk of Yanghu, a man of avaricious and cruel disposition, first served as an assistant magistrate in Lou County, where he earned the epithet "Blood-Spattered Nun."

Later, when transferred to Yanghu, the escaped convict Zhuang Wuke had long evaded capture. The Chief Clerk secretly learned that Zhuang was hiding in Huizhou and promptly reported this to the Changzhou Prefecture. Under orders, he set out to apprehend him and succeeded in arresting him in the countryside of Jing County. The magistrate of Jing County, eager to claim the merit for himself, offered two hundred taels of silver as a bribe, and so the Chief Clerk relinquished the prisoner. Zhuang was sent to Suzhou and swiftly executed. Not long after, one morning as the Chief Clerk was about to attend the yamen assembly, he suddenly glared into the empty air and rebuked, "Zhuang Wuke is here!" Then he cried, "He has entered!" He rushed into his bedchamber, turning to his family and shouting, "Block the door quickly—do not let him in!" Moments later, he stamped his feet and wailed, "He is inside the room! What is to be done?" He then fled beneath the bed, clutching the bed-curtain with a death grip, shrieking for help. After some time, his breath gave out and his voice grew hoarse. Blood streamed from his seven orifices, and he died.

Another magistrate, from Wujin, had served in Nanhui County during the years *jiyou* and *gengzi*. At the time, the opium ban was strictly enforced, and those caught smoking it were put to death. Local officials who apprehended fifteen cases in a month were immediately promoted. Yuqian, then Governor of Jiangsu, oversaw the campaign with severity.

The magistrate, currying favor with his superiors, reported over a hundred cases within two months. Yu was greatly pleased and petitioned to have him granted the rank of *tongzhi*. The campaign was set for a six-month limit, during which no executions were permitted by law. Yet with so many arrests, the majority perished in prison.

Several years later, the magistrate was transferred from Wujin to Yuanhe and was deemed *zhuoyi*—his governance outstanding. He traveled to the capital for an imperial audience. There, he rode in a five-horse carriage, its crossbar emblazoned with twin bears—a sign of rising authority.

As the boat sailed to Qingjiangpu, the magistrate’s servants in the forward cabin heard him shout loudly, “If you have something to say, speak properly—do not lay hands on me!” At the time, they were aboard a Changzhou pleasure boat, and the courtesans on board had long been intimate with the magistrate, so they assumed he was merely jesting. Soon after, they heard shouts of rebuke, followed by his voice: “Even we do not deserve to die—how could you bear to drive us to our deaths?” Only then did the servants find this strange and rush in together to see. They found the magistrate’s face ashen with fear, his hands and feet braced as if fending off an attack. Once the others entered, he finally calmed, saying, “Fortunate that you came—otherwise, I would have been in grave danger!” When asked what had happened, he fell silent and would not speak. Someone suggested he request sick leave and turn back to Suzhou, but he refused. The next day, they crossed the Yellow River and reached Wangjiaying. Before they had traveled two stages, his illness flared violently. He fell into a stupor, insensible to all around him, and whatever he muttered was filled with rage and contention. His son, who had accompanied him, then resolved to escort him southward, traveling day and night with all haste.

Upon reaching Benniu in Changzhou, his illness had already reached a critical state. Suddenly, he clawed at his own tongue with both hands and died with a great cry.

The Master of the Flower Seat remarked: “Bandits who plunder others’ wealth are not tolerated by the king’s law. To capture and execute them seems, on the surface, without fault. Yet if one truly acts with a heart for the state, rooting out the wicked and pacifying the good, who would say it is not fitting? Or perhaps when local officials charged with apprehending bandits pursue a case and make arrests, they fulfill their duty in office, while the bandits face their just deserts. If bandits die by the law, how dare they bear grudges against the enforcers of justice?

Yet as parents to the people, if officials cannot instruct and provide for their populace, driving them to destitution and banditry, only to then execute them en masse—‘mourn them with compassion, do not rejoice’—even the humane would feel regret. But if the territory is not one’s own jurisdiction, the office not one’s responsibility, the matter unrelated to official duty, and one acts merely out of covetous desire for promotion, crossing borders to hunt and seize—even if the stolen goods are real and the bandits confirmed, a single selfish thought means the bandits, once caught, will bear hatred. How much more so when many cases are false or incomplete, or when names are misattributed?”

Or where the peach withers in place of the plum—its branches collapsing in substitution—where the innocent are framed as bandits; or where petty thieves, mere pilferers and prowlers, commit no capital offense, yet charges are fabricated to ensnare them. Thus the wronged and aggrieved bow their heads to the executioner’s blade. If the dead retain awareness, against whom should they bear their vengeance if not those who seized them as bandits to secure promotion? In the past thirty years, those I have seen rise through such means—while well-wishers still fill their halls, the curses of the dead already linger at their doors. Here I record several of the most egregious cases, setting them down as a warning to those in power.

**Case 32: The Guangping Student**

A steadfast integrity, how could it ever yield to slackness?
Yet for the sake of clover’s trifling gain, he bent his principles to follow.
Perhaps he bore no strange affliction from the start—
but the jailer’s fatal word, once whispered, sealed his doom.

The Guangping student—Guangping, a county name—was an old acquaintance of my father (as the *Record of Rites* says: “When meeting a friend of one’s father, one does not presume to advance without invitation”—here, *father’s acquaintance* refers to a friend of one’s father who shares his aspirations). I dare not speak his name directly. A man of broad learning and literary skill, he was also a master of the Eight Principles of calligraphy (that is, the art of writing. As the *Garden of Calligraphy* states: “The dot is *cè*, the horizontal stroke *lè*, the vertical *dū*, the rising stroke *qū*, the left-falling stroke *cè*, the right-falling stroke *lüè*, the left-upward stroke *zhuó*, and the right-falling stroke *zhé*.” The ancients derived their brush techniques from the character *yǒng*, encompassing the dynamics of the Eight Principles to master all possible strokes). He received government stipends as a student (饩, pronounced *xì*; *to receive stipends* refers to being granted a granary allowance) and repeatedly sat for provincial examinations without success (that is, he failed the provincial exams multiple times).

Impoverished, he took up work as a private secretary, and local officials vied to employ him.

Thereafter he arrived in Piling (Pí, pronounced *pí*; Kunling, that is, Changzhou), where the prefect engaged him to oversee correspondence, and host and guest were greatly pleased with one another. Within a few years, he obtained a teaching position through purchased rank. No sooner had he completed the formalities than he contracted a strange illness. At first it resembled beriberi, then his hands became so cramped he could not move them (挛, pronounced *luán*; as the *Zhengyun* states: “cramping of hands and feet”). Later his head stiffened and his neck bent so he could not raise it at all, as though his body were bound by the Three Wooden Restraints (for the Three Wooden Restraints, see the third entry in the previous “Apprehending Thieves” section), his head encased in a cangue like a criminal in stocks (桎梏, pronounced *zhì gǔ*; as the *Book of Later Han*’s “Biography of Fan Pang” records: “all were bound by the Three Wooden Restraints, their heads encased, and exposed in the courtyard.” By this is meant the appearance of a convict wearing a cangue and chains). The prefect sent him home, and before a full month had passed, he died (匝, pronounced *zā*, meaning “a full cycle”).

In life he had always been earnest and cautious, without major transgressions, yet he suddenly suffered this cruel retribution. People universally declared him ill-fated, unworthy of office. Though he possessed but a single strip of blue felt (as the *Book of Jin*’s “Biography of Wang Xianzhi” relates: “A thief entered his chamber and stole everything. Xianzhi calmly said: ‘The blue felt is an old family possession—you may leave it.’ The thieves fled in astonishment.” From this derives the saying that a teacher’s position is “blue felt”), he lacked even the fortune to enjoy it, instead meeting his end through this strange illness. Moreover, the stipend for his clerical post was meager, and he had family dependents—how could he suddenly amass a thousand taels to purchase a teaching position? People also found this suspicious.

Only years after his death, when the matter was mentioned in conversation with Cao Zhulou, was it revealed that his death was likely the result of karmic retribution. During his time in Piling, he had grown close to a wealthy family of the prefecture. A ne’er-do-well clansman of theirs repeatedly extorted the family with fabricated grievances, leaving them unable to find a day’s peace. When they reported him to the authorities, he was lightly punished, but upon release, his disturbances only worsened, leaving them at their wits’ end. They consulted the scholar, who promised to eliminate the nuisance in exchange for a gift of a thousand taels as a reward (as Ma Zu chang’s poem states: “A thousand taels as a gift for a man’s longevity.” By this is meant the euphemism for presenting someone with wealth).

The scholar firmly refused at first, though he subtly hinted at his desire to purchase a teaching post, lamenting his lack of funds. The wealthy family promptly replied, "If you can rid me of this scourge, I shall cover the expenses for your post." Only then did the scholar consent. He reported the ne’er-do-well’s unlawful conduct to the prefect, who despised local ruffians and immediately conducted a personal investigation, ordering the county to arrest the man and imprison him. The scholar further instructed the county constable to ensure his death in custody. Once the ne’er-do-well was dead, the wealthy family indeed paid the scholar’s expenses, securing him the position of county instructor. Yet within half a year, illness struck him down. Cao and a close relative of the scholar had heard of these events, and they sighed in dread.

The Master of Sitting-Among-Flowers remarked: "Though the scholar was refined and learned, a moment’s error led to his grim demise, and all who heard of it mourned him. Yet the Dao of Heaven is luminous and just—no man may kill with impunity." (These two lines are from the *Book of Han*, in the biography of Yan Yannian, spoken by his mother. As Yan Shigu notes: "He who takes many lives shall himself meet death.")

Such men as the ne’er-do-well, though neither treacherous nor thieving, brought only suffering and burden upon their clansmen. Yet even those clansmen did not truly stand in irreconcilable opposition. One was rich, the other poor; pressed by hunger and cold, the latter merely hoped, by virtue of kinship, to partake in the river’s bounty. (As the *Zuozhuan* states: "In the Zhou’s ancestral covenants, those of different surnames came after." The *Book of Han*’s "Eulogy on the Empresses" says: "When one’s person attains the height of glory, one’s clan gradually receives the river’s moisture." Note: "Clan" is here called "ancestral covenant." To "partake in the river’s bounty" means to seek relief.)

Now, such matters may arise from human sentiment, and the man himself was not one whom royal law necessarily condemned. Yet to wield the dagger against one’s own kin (Zheng Kangcheng and He Xiu debated fiercely, composing the *Mo Shou* on the *Gongyang Commentary*, calling it "wielding the dagger within the same chamber." Note: The phrase "wielding the dagger within the same chamber" to describe intra-clan strife originates here), inflicting the pain of flayed skin (The *Yijing*’s *Bo* hexagram states in its image: "Flaying the bed to the skin portends imminent disaster." Note: The phrase "the pain of flayed skin" to describe personal calamity derives from this. These two lines refer to the wealthy family’s elimination of a threat to their kin), to cast a net across the heavens, and ultimately to cause another’s hands to be stained with ill-gotten gain (脏, pronounced *zāng*. The *Guangyun* defines *zāng* as "accepting bribes." Note: These two lines refer to the scholar engineering the ne’er-do-well’s death).

At last, when that man had been imprisoned and his grievance buried deep, the scholar too fell gravely ill within his tent, his hands contorted and neck broken, his appearance like that of a captive prisoner (俘, pronounced *fú*. 俘囚: a criminal). Was this not what is meant by "using that man’s own Way to return and govern his own body"?

---

**33. The Delicate Maiden of Wu Gate**

Resentful yet not wrathful—pity her plight,
Two new poems dashed off in idle strokes.
Beware the ruts of old—do not tread them again,
For Gao of Jiaxing is the cart before you.

In the autumn examination of the *renzi* year, Gao of Jiaxing, after completing the three essays of the first session, suddenly inscribed two quatrains in large script:

*"Do you recall the time we stood beneath the blossoms in the moonlight?
Deep in the night, I secretly composed love poems.
This time, as you take the autumn exam, the cold wind bites—
The frost has seeped through my silk slippers, though you know it not."* (First poem)

*"Beneath the deep yellow earth, jade bones lie sleeping,
A desolate gaze back—all fades like mist.
No need to weave another* Record of Passing the Examinations *(The* New唐书·艺文志 *records:* Cui’s *Record of Passing the Examinations in Xianqing* [era], five juan; Li Yi’s *Record of Passing the Examinations*, two juan),
For when the mandarin ducks are embroidered, one becomes an immortal."* (Second poem)

Afterward, he inscribed *"By the Delicate Maiden of Wu Gate"* (吴门: Suzhou) on the sealed examination hall (锁院: the examination compound) in Zhejiang. Upon leaving the hall, he hired a boat and set off for home by starlight, but died before reaching his household. This must have been a case of seduction and abandonment, leading to death with lingering resentment. Yet the verses are "resentful yet not wrathful" (These four characters derive from the *Analects* commentary on "poems that may express resentment"), capturing well the essence of the *feng* poet (风人: poet). Still, to have given herself to an unworthy man and thus become an ill-fated pair (The *Zuozhuan* states: "An ill-fated pair is called an enemy")—how lamentable!

---

**34. A Certain Chaste Widow**

A Certain Chaste Widow

*"Dare to offer illicit union for mockery—*
*In the years of Yǐ and Bǐng, the crime began not lightly.*
*To grasp the braid was deemed the chaste widow’s chastity,*
*Yet three men under one law—this alone was just."*

This chaste widow was a native of Chen Family Alley in Shanghai. She entered a household as a child bride. Diligent and prudent in her duties, she won the favor of her parents-in-law. In the autumn of the *renzi* year of Xianfeng (1852), she was at last wed. At the time of the cotton harvest, she followed her mother-in-law to the fields to pick cotton. As dusk approached, her mother-in-law, ravenous with hunger, returned home first. The widow, having not yet finished her task, remained alone. A rogue named Jia, accompanied by his followers Yi and Bing, passed by. Seeing the widow’s youthful beauty, Yi and Bing jested with Jia, saying, *"If you can couple with her in the wild, we shall pool our silver and feast you in celebration."*

Jia laughed and said, *"What difficulty is there in this?"* He rushed forward and seized the widow, whispering lewd words. Caught off guard, the widow was terrified and screamed wildly. Seeing no one responded, Jia dragged her to the ground, tearing her skirt. The widow clutched at Jia with her hands, glaring fiercely as she cursed him, her voice growing ever more vehement. Enraged, Jia stuffed a clod of earth into her mouth and tried to remove her undergarments, but the widow resisted with all her strength. After a while, she lay motionless—when he touched her, she was already stiff.

Terrified, Yi and Bing fled, and Jia attempted to escape as well, stumbling repeatedly as he tried to rise, unaware that his queue was still grasped in the widow’s hand. A villager passing by witnessed the scene and alerted her family. Her parents-in-law and husband arrived together, and onlookers soon gathered in a throng.

They seized a rope to bind Jia, only to find his queue still clutched in the widow’s hand, her grip so tight they could not pry it open. Her parents-in-law and husband wept as they prayed: *"Jia, your wickedness has brought this woman to an untimely death. Now that we are all here, you cannot escape. Release her hold on your queue, and we shall report this to the authorities to avenge your wrong and petition for your commemoration as a woman of chastity and resolve."* No sooner had they finished their prayer than her fingers loosened of their own accord. The onlookers all declared that the spirit of the steadfast widow, though dead, had not faltered.

They bound Jia and turned him over to the authorities, then apprehended Yi and Bing as well. All three were tried and sentenced according to the law.

The Master of the Flowery Studio remarked: *"Alas! Amid the wild fields and creeping grasses, violence struck without warning, and she found no path to call for aid. In such moments, to live is to yield, and to die is to resist—had she not held fast to her resolve, how could she have remained unwavering unto death? I have often observed that many learned gentlemen of past and present, when seated in ease, can discourse eloquently on loyalty, filial piety, and the emulation of sages, dismissing the integrity of humble thatched roofs as though it were of little consequence. Yet when self-interest presses upon them, their moral conduct crumbles, and they weep, saying: ‘I would do as they do, but my strength fails me.’ How then do they compare to this widow, who in life remained unyielding through every trial, and in death became a vengeful spirit to slay her assailant? The gulf between worthiness and unworthiness—how vast it is!"*

35. *Elder Fei of the Feng Clan*

The Intendant Zhao’s Clerk Gains the Favor of Governor Fan

*"To model governance on the ways of past dynasties is to ensure titles of nobility for ten thousand generations;
Not for vainglory or favor, but that even a young grandson may one day become a governor."*

During a famine in Jiangsu Province in the Qianlong era, the magistrate of Jiangyin, lacking the art of pacification, drove the people to rebellion—they raised their poles in revolt. The magistrate then reported the uprising as an act of treason. Governor Fan, upon hearing of the disturbance, traveled personally to the scene. Passing through Changzhou, the grandfather of Governor Fei Heting—then serving as a clerk in the Intendant Zhao’s office—accompanied the prefect to welcome the governor at his moored vessel. The clerk was instructed to wait outside. After some time, the governor escorted the prefect out and, catching sight of the clerk, was struck by his appearance. Turning to the prefect, he asked, “Who is this?” The prefect replied that he was a clerk in the Intendant’s office.

By then, the ringleaders and dozens of their followers had all been bound. Yet outside, rumors spread that the charge of treason required the slaughter of the entire city, and the people trembled in terror.

The governor then summoned Fei inside and said, “As a clerk in the Intendant’s office, you must be well-versed in legal precedents. The starving commoners of Jiang County have risen in revolt—by statute, none, young or old, should escape mass execution. What measure would ensure that punishment aligns with both circumstance and guilt?”

Fei replied, “Do you intend to enforce the law strictly, or do you intend to act as a lord for ten thousand generations?”

The governor said, “Explain the distinction between the two, and I shall choose.”

Fei answered, “If the entire city had indeed rebelled, then mass execution would be warranted. Yet in Jiang County, the unrest arose suddenly, driven by impoverished people seeking food—hoping only for a few measures of grain to fill their mouths. They never intended rebellion. To condemn them all to slaughter would contravene the sagely heavens’ care for the common folk. Now that the ringleaders have been apprehended, in my humble opinion, it would be better to apply the statute for bandits who assemble to commit robbery: sentence the leaders to decapitation and public display, and mete out military exile or penal servitude to the rest. This would strike a just balance between circumstance and guilt—an act worthy of a lord for ten thousand generations.”

The governor found his words profoundly convincing and ordered Fei to draft the proposal. One was beheaded, and over a dozen were exiled. Later observers remarked that Fei’s single utterance had saved ten thousand lives, and that his descendants would surely prosper. His son, whose name is not recorded here, rose from the rank of tribute student to serve as Intendant of the Tong-Guang Circuit in Shaanxi. His grandson, Kai-shou, became Governor Fei Heting. Their ancestor was posthumously honored as Grand Master for Glorious Happiness and General Who Quells Might, and to this day, their family remains distinguished. III.6 The Virtuous Merchant Fei
True evidence seized—what defense remains?
Yet the thief’s tale reveals a good man’s intent.
Ten cakes of silver, four strings of cash—
The old neighbor’s gain brought only laughter at his folly.

The grandfather of Intendant Fei Gengting ran a general store dealing in northern and southern goods. Generous and principled, he was known throughout the village as a virtuous man. The poor among his neighbors often came to his home for food. One year, as the New Year approached, an elderly neighbor lingered in the shop after eating, sitting by the counter for a long time without leaving.

The shop assistant opened the cashbox to find ten silver dollars missing. After searching everywhere in vain, all suspected the elderly neighbor. They seized and searched him, and indeed found the money. At that moment, voices rose in a chorus of abuse, leaving the old man with nowhere to turn.

Just then, the virtuous man emerged. Upon inquiry, he said, “You are all greatly mistaken. I took the money to give to him because of his poverty, but I forgot to tell you. How could you insult him so hastily?” Turning to the elderly neighbor, he apologized, “They did not know. Please do not take offense. But you were too slow-witted—why not simply say the money was a gift from me instead of enduring such humiliation?” He added, “You mentioned ten dollars earlier, but that is still not enough. Take another four thousand cash as provisions for the New Year.” The old man then took the money and silver, bowed in gratitude, and departed.

Such was the nature of his conduct. Later, his grandson, Intendant Fei Gengting, passed the imperial examination as *huìyuán* in the *jǐmǎo* year and rose to the position of Grain Intendant of Fujian. The virtuous man was repeatedly honored with imperial commendations, for Heaven’s reward for good deeds is never amiss.

The Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarked: “The two Fei patriarchs were both registered in Changzhou. Their deeds remain the subject of admiring praise to this day.”

Those who discuss these matters say that Elder He Ting’s single utterance saved ten thousand lives, and what he preserved was of great magnitude—something Elder Gengting could not compare to. Thus, the karmic reward they received also differed in scale. Yet I alone do not hold this view.

Why? Elder He Ting’s words were such that anyone with a sense of priorities could have perceived them. What was truly remarkable was merely his few phrases before the governor—neither provocative nor obsequious, yet tactful and persuasive. As for Elder Gengting’s actions, the thieves had already been captured with the stolen goods in hand; reprimanding them would not have been excessive, while releasing them would have sufficed to demonstrate mercy. Yet he went to such lengths to protect and conceal, even fearing others might still suspect them, and added another four thousand cash beyond the ten dollars. Such a heart—so vast in compassion, so meticulous in care—truly knows no bounds. As Su Ziyou once said to Fan Zhongxuan, “You are a being of the Buddha’s realm!” So too might I say of this virtuous man.

**37. Zhang Mingde**

His manners were but slightly wanton, his levies ever so light—
Yet how his "Bright Virtue" shamed the name he bore!
Demanding payment for the tea he proffered, his heart was far from kind;
Yet Heaven’s Dao has ever balanced good with just retribution.

Zhang Mingde, a household scribe of Huating, was cunning and adept at manipulating documents. Through scheming connections, he became a granary clerk. Once in power, he grew ever more unbridled, acting without restraint. The honest and meek among the villagers were all ruthlessly devoured by him. If anyone bore even the slightest grudge against him, he would immediately ensnare them in perilous legal traps. Thus, his peers all regarded him with wary caution, none daring to oppose him.

There was a runner named Chen Dazhong, a man of upright and forthright character, who alone refused to submit to him. Mingde harbored deep resentment and sought an opportunity to harm him, but for a long time found no opening.

In the spring of the *renyin* year, as the grain transport affairs neared completion, Mingde entrusted Dazhong with several hundred *dan* of grain receipts, instructing him to go to the countryside to collect the commuted payments. At the time, each *dan* was commuted to five dollars and sixty cents in silver. After Dazhong had departed, Mingde reported to the officials and increased the price to six dollars and thirty cents. When Dazhong returned and submitted the payments as previously agreed, Mingde suddenly declared, “This still falls short by over three hundred dollars—could you have embezzled the difference?” Dazhong, enraged, retorted, “When I left, the rate was only five dollars and sixty cents. The sudden price hike in the city—how could I have known?” They parted in bitter dispute.

Mingde ultimately reported to the officials that Dazhong had embezzled funds. The officials detained Dazhong and ordered him to make restitution. Dazhong refused to admit guilt, whereupon he was imprisoned, convicted of misappropriating official silver, and sentenced to military exile in Henan. Dazhong owned several rooms of housing and over ten *mu* of land, all of which he sold to provide for his family and cover travel expenses, having already signed the deeds. When Mingde learned of this, he went to the buyer and said, “Dazhong has embezzled and depleted public funds, so his property should be liquidated to repay the authorities. If you purchase the assets of a criminal, you will share in his guilt!” The buyer, frightened, offered Mingde a generous bribe and sought his counsel. Mingde feigned hesitation and said, “Has the payment already been made?” “Not yet,” the buyer replied. “If not,” Mingde said, “there may still be a way. Quickly bring the deed and the purchase money, and I will submit them to the officials on your behalf. When Dazhong comes to demand payment, direct him to the treasury to collect it—then you will have nothing to fear.” The buyer followed his advice and handed over the entire sum to Mingde. By the time Dazhong was exiled, his family’s property had already been reported as confiscated. Upon hearing this, he could only seethe with resentment and dared not claim the money.

That winter, Dazhong set out for his exile, leaving his wife and children with his wife’s family. As he left the city, his bitter wailing moved all who passed by. At that moment, Dazhong bore an extraordinary injustice, exiled a thousand *li* away, his family scattered like stars. He asked himself when he might ever return home, doomed to carry his grievance to the end of his days. Meanwhile, Mingde, nursing only a slight grudge over Dazhong’s manners, had first secured his conviction and then seized all his assets. Arrogant and overbearing, he believed himself as unshakable as Mount Tai upon bedrock, certain that no one would ever challenge him.

Later, when the Yellow River breached its banks in Henan and the roads were blocked, those sentenced to exile were ordered by decree to return to their home counties to await imprisonment until the waters receded before proceeding.

Dazhong returned to Huating on the twelfth day of the second month in the *guimao* year. He had scarcely spent ten days in the county jail when Mingde’s calamity struck. Previously, in Huating, the silver payment for corvée labor had been burdensome, and the people’s commutation fees were slow to arrive. The grain transport commissioner had first advanced funds to cover the shortfall, and if the amount remained insufficient, the boats would depart while the laborers were detained to await further payment. This practice had been followed for years without error. That year, however, Magistrate Liu had only recently assumed office. Mingde, seeking to coerce him, informed the authorities in advance that the new grain transport could not proceed until the corvée fees were fully paid. When the magistrate refused to comply, Mingde daily incited the transport laborers and boatmen to storm the yamen and clamor for payment. Enraged, the magistrate reprimanded Mingde, who then consumed raw opium in the gatehouse, intending to feign suicide and extort compensation—certain that the magistrate would revive him and seek alternative funds to appease the laborers. The grain transport boats then set sail.

The officials' funds could then be embezzled at will! Mingde then spoke with the gatekeeper. Seeing smoke rising from his beard, the gatekeeper was greatly alarmed and reported it to the magistrate. Enraged, the magistrate immediately ordered Mingde imprisoned. Before he even entered the prison gate, he fell into a stupor, unable to speak. Those who consume raw opium are revived by cold water but must avoid hot tea, which kills instantly. Dazhong, already in prison, heard that Mingde was about to arrive and rejoiced. He had prepared hot tea in advance. When Mingde entered, Dazhong greeted him, saying, "Mingde, have you come here as well?" He then offered the tea. In his dazed state, Mingde drank it at once. As soon as he finished, he collapsed to the ground and died shortly after. After his death, Mingde’s wife and children attempted to carry his body out over the prison wall. Dazhong and the other prisoners refused, saying, "You must first repay the proceeds from the sale of my property and give each of us in this prison a thousand taels of silver." During Mingde’s life, he relied on his cunning and deceit to bully his peers. They all despised him, and Dazhong bore particular resentment toward him.

When he died, none failed to call it a delight, and in the end, no one stepped forward to intercede for him. His son spent twelve hundred taels of silver before the body was allowed to be carried out. For from Dazhong’s counterclaim to Mingde’s death, only ten days had passed. Two months after his death, Dazhong was once again sent into exile. On the eve of his departure, he entrusted someone to record the sequence of events by which he and Mingde had successively fallen into crime and imprisonment, and had the account carved onto printing blocks, which were distributed far and wide to demonstrate the subtlety of karmic retribution.

The Master of Sitting-Among-Flowers remarked: “The subtlety and swiftness of karmic retribution have never surpassed this case. Did Heaven act solely to redress the grievance of one Chen Dazhong? It is only that in Dazhong’s case, the peril and malice were especially conspicuous! Long-concealed schemes eventually ensnare their own architect—like the ‘gentleman in the vat’ or the ‘man with spear and shield.’ From ancient times to the present, the pattern has been one and the same.”

"What benefit lies in treachery?"

38. The Potent Virtue of the Pan Clan
Wealth cannot buy worthy descendants—
Who has cultivated such pious karma?
Fortunate that the eldest son could carry on the legacy,
Riches and honor should endure in unbroken succession.

The Pan clan of Wu Gate was renowned for its virtue through the generations, and Heaven thus greatly prospered its descendants, gathering and nurturing their brilliance. Among them was the Grand Tutor, Duke Wengong, whose rank reached the highest of the Three Dukes and whose person embodied the Five Blessings. The fullness of his good fortune was unmatched not only in recent times but also rare among the ancients.

The origins of his flourishing fortune have been recorded in many other texts, and there is no need for redundant repetition. The Grand Tutor’s eldest son, Gongfu, a cultivated talent, delighted in goodness and was generous in bestowing alms, yet sought no official advancement. Though the Grand Tutor held power in the court for over a decade, Gongfu would visit the capital only every other year to pay his respects, returning home soon after. While residing at home, he refused private audiences with those in power; if they came to see him, he did not turn them away. He was well-versed in discussing local advantages and grievances, yet never sought personal gain from such matters, nor did he return visits in kind.

After the age of forty, he devoted himself earnestly to the cultivation of Buddhist merit. Regardless of status, he treated all with equal courtesy; when those in distress sought his aid, he never refused. He owned several thousand *mu* of land, and while he accepted rent from tenants who paid, he made no demands upon those who could not. Whenever a joyous occasion arose in his family or a year yielded poor harvests, he would waive their rent entirely. His other acts of goodness are too numerous to recount in full.

In my travels to and from Wu Gate, I came to know well of his conduct and principles, and believed that if the Dao of Heaven truly exists, his descendants would surely prosper. In the first year of the Xianfeng era, the Grand Tutor retired from office in his old age. The current Son of Heaven treated the elder statesman with great honor, and Gongfu’s nephew inherited his uncle’s official rank. His eldest grandson was graciously bestowed the title of *juren* by imperial favor. The following year, in the *renzi* year, he passed the metropolitan examination and became a *jinshi*, ranking third in the first class. Thus, grandfather and grandson achieved the highest honors in the imperial examinations, becoming a celebrated tale in the literary world. This was undoubtedly due to the depth of his ancestors’ virtue, as well as Gongfu’s own efforts to perpetuate their goodness through generations. The rise of the Pan family shows no sign of waning.

**Three-Nine Antidote for Arsenic Poisoning**

**Three-Nine Antidote for Arsenic Poisoning**

The essence of practicing medicine lies in saving lives with a compassionate heart;
Extorting wealth at will is never justified.
Nine lives wronged by your greed shall be repaid by yours—
Nine generations fated to die by arsenic, the judgment is clear.

A man of She County, Jiang Zigang, possessed a secret formula to instantly neutralize arsenic poisoning. Yet those who sought his remedy were forced to pay exorbitant sums; if his demands were not met, he would sit idly by and watch them perish. One day, while traveling to a neighboring county to practice medicine, he died suddenly in the night. He appeared in a dream to his host, saying: *"For my obsession with profit, I caused the deaths of nine people. The deceased brought their grievances before the underworld court, where it was decreed that I must suffer arsenic poisoning for nine lifetimes. Now, I am to enter the cycle of rebirth. I bribed the ghostly attendants to pass on this antidote to you. If you save one life with it, I shall be spared one lifetime of karmic retribution. Should you spread it widely to aid the world, your merit will be boundless."*

With that, he wept and departed, lamenting, *"My remorse comes too late!"* The formula consisted solely of one *liang* of *fangfeng* ground into powder and taken with water—no other ingredients were needed. *Strange Tales Worthy of Belief* also records that arsenic poisoning can be miraculously cured by mixing gypsum with cold water.

**Forty: The Magistrate of Yunjian**

A burden borne on the shoulder, set down by the roadside—
To flog him for blocking the path was already perverse.
The dead was but a hired laborer in life;
To demand a life in repayment, the magistrate himself should have died.

A certain prefect, formerly a ministry official, was appointed magistrate of Yunjian. His nature was avaricious and cruel. Whenever he traveled, his retinue would trample all in their path...

From the *Book of Jin: Treatises on Carriages and Attire*: "The outriders and retinue of the *Zouqi* lead the procession."
