Those who traveled the roads and failed to yield swiftly enough were flogged without mercy. One day, the magistrate was returning from the western outskirts of the city when he encountered a paper merchant carrying his wares on a shoulder pole, standing by the roadside. Enraged that the man had not set down his burden, the magistrate ordered his underlings to drag him before his palanquin and berated him. The man, simple and stubborn, grew indignant and said, "I never blocked the road—what crime is there in not setting down my load?" The magistrate flew into a rage: "What lowly wretch dares defy me so!" He ordered his men to beat the man severely. After the flogging, he had him dragged forward again and demanded, "Do you admit your guilt?" The man replied, "What crime have I committed? I truly do not know!" The magistrate, cruel and obstinate, could not bear to be humiliated by a commoner in the streets. Shame and fury rising, he ordered the man beaten hundreds more times. Blood and flesh flew in all directions, yet still he did not relent. He commanded his men to escort the man to Huating to stand trial for obstructing the road. The clerks and runners extorted tens of thousands of coins from the shopkeeper, but fortunately, the magistrate of Huating, seeing the man’s grievous wounds, did not press further charges. After several days in custody, he was released and sent home. Upon his return, the shopkeeper, resentful of the trouble he had caused, drove him away. The man, already subjected to brutal punishment and now cast out by his employer, hanged himself.

Within ten days, the magistrate’s back erupted in five carbuncles, the pain unbearable. The physicians called this condition "A Hundred Birds Paying Homage to the Phoenix" and said that if the sores did not fester, there might still be hope. One night, he dreamed of the paper merchant lifting his hand to tear at the carbuncles. The agony was so intense that he screamed and awoke, calling for his maidservant to bring a light. The carbuncles had all burst, pus and blood soaking his bedding. Every physician threw up their hands in despair. From then on, whenever he closed his eyes, he saw the paper merchant standing before him. No prayers or exorcisms brought relief. He could no longer lie flat but had to prop himself up, resting his head on the bedframe. The slightest movement sent pain searing through his heart and lungs. Those who saw him said it was a vision of hell itself. He shrieked in torment for weeks before dying. At the moment of his death, the entire yamen saw the figure of the paper merchant in the air.

The Master of the Flower Seat remarked: "When scholar-officials wield power, they often act recklessly, heedless of others’ suffering. Alas! Are not all men of the same kind? How can one indulge such cruelty and call it gratification?"

When blood and flesh flew in all directions, he could do nothing to me. Yet when sores festered and pus overflowed, I too could do nothing to him. I urge all scholar-officials of this world: take heed never to reach the point where you can do nothing to another, only to regret it later."

41. **Heaven’s Thunder Strikes Down a Murderous Plot**

A cunning scheme long harbored in the heart,
To kill for wealth—such deeds are deeply wrong.
The wife, caught in the fields, met heaven’s wrath;
Her husband’s fate must see his crimes made known.

Within the northern gate of Danyang, a family of commoners lived in considerable affluence. They ran a grain shop in front of their home. The four brothers had only one child between them—born to the second brother—a boy of just a few years, raised jointly by all four households and cherished like a pearl in the palm. His bracelets and necklaces, all of gold and adorned with pearls and jade, were worth over a hundred taels. One day, the wet nurse carried him into the shop to play. Suddenly, a woman appeared, bringing cakes and pastries for the child. She followed the wet nurse into the house, claiming to be from Zhoushang in the northern countryside. Her appearance was unremarkable, but her words were quick and sharp.

The household grew quite fond of her, and after sitting briefly, she would leave. From then on, she came frequently, each time taking the child in her arms—sometimes pulling sweets from her sleeve to feed him, other times carrying him through the streets to buy him treats. This went on for months, and the family grew accustomed to it. One day, she took the child away again and did not return for a long time. Everyone assumed she had taken him to visit relatives in the city and thought nothing of it. But when night fell and all was still, the child was nowhere to be found. The entire household erupted in chaos. The boy’s aunt went to the northern countryside, searching village by village, but no one had seen such a woman. Days later, someone reported that ten *li* outside the city, at the foot of a mountain in a deep ravine, a baby lay face-down on the ground. They rushed to the spot and found it was indeed the child—already stiff. Sand and mud filled his seven orifices, and his gold ornaments had been stripped away. Only then did they realize the woman had coveted the jewelry, visiting often to hold the child as part of a scheme to murder him for his wealth. They reported the crime to the authorities, but the woman remained at large. In truth, she was the wife of a farmer near the Seven *Li* Temple. Widowed, she had taken up with a man from a neighboring village. The man was poor, and she provided for him, but when her means fell short, she resorted to theft and abduction to make ends meet. She committed many wicked deeds, but never in her own village, so no one suspected her. After murdering the child, she gave all the gold and jewels to her lover, who sold them for capital and set off to trade elsewhere. The woman stayed behind, and one day, while bringing food to several village women working in the fields, she sat waiting for them to finish eating. Suddenly, dark clouds gathered, and thunder and lightning surged, swirling around the women’s heads. The others trembled in fear, but the woman remained composed, saying, “Lightning strikes those with guilty hearts. If any of you have done wrong, confess now, and you may escape heaven’s punishment.” Before she could finish speaking, a thunderclap split the air, and she fell to her knees—dead. Meanwhile, her lover was trading far from home, still some dozen *li* away. That same day, wind and thunder seized him and dragged him to the spot where the child had died. He knelt on the ground, unable to rise. A thunderbolt struck his head, and a purple mist coiled around him. Though his mind was dazed, his mouth could still speak, and he confessed everything—his affair with the woman, their thefts, abductions, and the murder of the baby. The next day, he too perished.

The family’s line of descent thus came to an end.

Hearing this, the Master of Sitting-Among-Flowers sighed and said, “The might of thunder is so divine and swift! Alas! Even with such a warning to the people, there are still those who harbor dark schemes and poisonous plots, acting wantonly without fear or restraint—how utterly benighted and insensate they are!”

---

**42. The Ox-Headed Man**
*Karmic retribution from past lives pursues one in the present;*
*The Mirror of Karma reveals the seeds of disaster.*
*Fortunate to meet a monk midway,*
*One may repent anew and turn the wheel of rebirth.*

In the eastern region of Zhe, a scholar named Wang was strolling through the countryside one spring day when he encountered an old cow in the fields. As it lowered its horns to charge, Wang hurried past, but the beast wheeled around, intent on pursuit, only to be restrained by its herder. Even so, it strained against the rope, stamping its hooves and fixing him with a wide-eyed glare.

That night, Wang dreamed he was seized by a black-clad bailiff and taken to an official bureau, where he stood trial alongside many others.

(Continuing the account:) The term "confronting the record" refers to being called to answer for one’s deeds. Among the plaintiffs—some male, some female—all bore ox-heads upon human bodies, and each accused Scholar Wang of having harmed them. Seated above was an official crowned with coral, his court robes draped with strands of pearls, his attire resembling that of a censor. He demanded of Wang how he had killed so many oxen.

Wang declared that for generations his family had upheld Confucian learning, abstaining from beef and dog meat for three lifetimes—how could he have slain cattle? The presiding judge ordered a green-robed clerk to bring forth a mirror and bid Wang gaze into it. In that moment, he suddenly recalled his past life as a butcher, wielding sharp blades with consummate skill, often slaughtering over ten oxen in a single day. By middle age, he had deeply repented his trade, fearing karmic retribution, and so shaved his head to become a monk on Mount Tiantai. His discipline was rigorous, and at over eighty years of age, he passed away seated in meditation. Yet due to his past sins, he could not ascend to the Trāyastriṃśa Heaven.

Once Wang grasped the cause of his former existence, he bowed his head and confessed.

The presiding judge then addressed him, saying, "Since you have transmigrated from the cycle of rebirth as a śramaṇa, do you still retain knowledge of the inner scriptures in this life?" Wang replied, "At the age of twelve, I copied seven scrolls of the *Vajracchedikā Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra* for my parents to dedicate merit to my grandparents in the afterlife. Thereafter, on every new and full moon, I recited the sūtra thrice without fail, even while traveling for examinations or by boat." The presiding judge’s countenance softened as he declared, "If this is true, then the matter is easily resolved!"

Turning to the assembly of ox-headed figures, he said, "Wang has already laid down the butcher’s knife and sincerely cultivated the Buddhist fruit. Though his karmic sins of killing were not yet exhausted, causing him to fall back into the cycle of rebirth, I propose that he now perform repentance rites for you all, allowing him to return to the human realm. What say you?" The assembly assented in agreement.

Wang then vowed to adopt a vegetarian diet for forty-nine days with his entire household, commission five hundred copies of the *Vajracchedikā Sūtra*, and dedicate them to pray for the plaintiffs’ rebirth in the Pure Land. The presiding judge explained this to the assembly, and all were moved with gratitude, joyfully agreeing to release their grievances. The judge then ordered a black-robed attendant to escort Wang home.

Upon reaching his door, he awoke and fulfilled his vow, reciting the sūtra as promised. He also had ten scrolls of the sūtra transcribed in gold ink and distributed them to various temples for veneration. After completing these acts, he dreamed once more of the men and women returning to express their thanks: "By the power of your sūtras, we have all escaped the animal realm and been reborn into wealthy and noble families!" With joy and reverence, they bowed and departed.

**Contents of the Lower Scroll:**

**1. Enduring Slander Without Protest**

A man of humble means yet content in poverty,
His pulse-touched heart repays silver to ease a patient’s woe.
With boundless magnanimity, he stores unseen virtue—
Where else could such a noble elder be found?

The late father of Prefect Wei of Changzhou was a man of generosity and benevolence, well-versed in the healing arts of Qi and Huang. Whether treating the rich or the poor, he devoted himself wholeheartedly, never demanding payment. For those too destitute to afford medicine, he would even donate funds to provide remedies. When villagers traveled to the city for treatment, he would first offer them congee or cakes. Only after they had eaten would he take their pulse, explaining: “Long journeys and hunger disrupt the blood and qi. I give you food to rest briefly so that your pulse may settle. Do I do this out of mere kindness? No—I do it to refine my art.”

Such were his pretexts for doing good. Once, he was summoned to treat a patient who had lost ten taels of silver from beside his pillow. The patient’s son, swayed by slander, suspected the elder of theft but dared not confront him. Someone advised him to kneel at the elder’s gate with incense in hand. Seeing this, the elder asked in surprise, “What are you doing?” The young man replied, “I have a matter to ask but fear your reproach, so I dare not speak.” The elder said, “Speak freely; I will not blame you.” When the truth was revealed, the elder led him into a private chamber and said, “The matter is true. I temporarily used the silver for an urgent need and intended to return it discreetly during my next visit, like ‘restoring the jade to Zhao.’ Since you’ve asked, take it now—but tell no one.” He then counted out the silver and gave it to him.

When the son first arrived with incense, people assumed the elder, known for his integrity, would never commit such a base act. But when the silver was returned, they sighed, “How unfathomable the human heart can be!” Slander spread rapidly, yet the elder remained unperturbed, dismissing it with indifference.

Soon after, the patient recovered and, while tidying his bedding, found the lost silver beneath his mattress. Stricken with remorse, he exclaimed, “The silver was here all along—how could I have wronged such a virtuous man? I must go at once to clear his name before the crowd, lest he bear this unjust stain any longer.” Father and son hastened to the elder’s gate, kneeling once more with incense. Seeing them, the elder smiled and asked, “What brings you here now?” The son, shamefaced, replied, “The lost silver was found. I wrongly suspected you, and for that, I deserve death. Here is the silver you returned to me. Punish me as you see fit.” The elder laughed, helped him up, and said, “It was nothing. Pay it no mind.”

The son then asked calmly, “When I slandered you before, why did you endure the defamation without protest, leaving me so deeply ashamed? Now that you’ve forgiven me, may I ask the reason?”

The elder laughed and said, “Your father and I are neighbors from the same lane, and I have long known his diligence and frugality in managing wealth. When he was ill and heard of the loss of ten taels of silver, his condition would surely have worsened—perhaps even to the point of no recovery. Thus, I chose to bear the stain of false accusation, so that when your father learned the lost item had been found, his sorrow would turn to joy, and his illness would naturally heal.”

The ailing father and son knelt again, kowtowing deeply, and said, “We are deeply moved by your boundless virtue, that you would endure defilement to save my life. We vow to repay this great kindness in our next lives, even as dogs or horses.”

The elder then invited them inside, set out wine, and entertained them warmly until they parted in high spirits. That day, the onlookers stood shoulder to shoulder like a wall, all saying, “The elder’s actions are beyond the comprehension of ordinary people!” From then on, the name of the virtuous man Wei resounded far and wide.

The former censor, rising from the rank of *jinshi*, later became the judicial commissioner of a certain province. Elder Wei reached the venerable age of eighty, repeatedly receiving imperial patents of nobility. Many of his grandsons also gained official positions. Those with discernment said, “Heaven’s dispensation of reward to the virtuous is never in error!”

*Master of the Flowery Seat* remarks: “In the Western Han, Nie Buyi was once mistakenly accused of stealing gold from a fellow official. Without protest, he compensated the loss with his own gold. When the gold was later found, the true owner was deeply ashamed and apologized, yet Nie did not reproach him. Observing Elder Wei’s story, how can one say that the people of ancient and modern times do not measure up to one another?”

---

**II. The Serpent’s Cry in the Belly**

The viper and serpent take form, crying within the womb—
Drowning daughters has always been a sin most grave.
I wish officials and gentry would heed this plea:
Raise infants, protect the young, and spread their care.

In Huzhou, poor families often practiced the custom of drowning infant girls. My mother once spoke of a relative’s maidservant who, after marriage, gave birth to daughters and drowned each one. Over ten years, she bore seven girls, not one spared. Later, she conceived again, but when the time came for delivery, no child was born—only the sound of a serpent hissing in her belly, heard for a day and a night, until she shrieked and died.

As the *Book of Songs* says: *“The viper and the serpent—these are the omens of women.”* Could it be that the serpent’s cry in her belly was the transformation of the drowned girls’ vengeful resentment?

---

**III. Saving a Life to Extend One’s Own**

A shared boat reveals deep compassion—
One sees the elder was no sinful man.
The underworld’s messenger, punished for leaking secrets,
Laughs at the burden he bore, no light matter.

At year’s end, Elder Wang of Xiuning was returning home after collecting debts, sailing in a small boat through wind and snow. A man on the shore begged to board, but the boatman refused. The elder alone took pity and allowed him. Once aboard, the man was nearly frozen and starved to stiffness. The elder warmed him with a tattered fur coat and gave him fiery liquor. After some time, the man could speak and kowtowed in thanks. When asked about his circumstances, he said, “I am no mortal—I am the City God’s messenger, sent to collect souls!”

Asked whose souls he was to collect, the messenger produced a register listing thirty-three names, with the elder’s at the very top. Terrified, the elder knelt before him and pleaded for mercy.

The messenger said, “This is by decree of the Eastern Peak; even the City God’s office cannot intervene. How dare I bend the law?” The elder pleaded all the more earnestly. The messenger replied, “Very well—out of gratitude for your kindness in sharing this boat, I shall place your name at the end of the register. You may gain a few days’ reprieve. Return home at once to settle your affairs, and on the third day of the new year, await me at the left of the temple gate.” The elder thanked him profusely.

Soon they reached a village, where the messenger took his leave and disembarked. The elder moored the boat to watch him and saw him enter a household. After a short while, he emerged, and the house erupted in wailing. Recalling the messenger’s words as truth, the elder hastily plied his oars and hurried home. No sooner had he stepped ashore than he saw a man and woman clinging to each other in tears. Upon inquiring, he learned that at year’s end, they had sought to sell the wife to repay their debts, but finding no buyer, they had resolved to die together. The elder thought, *I have but days left—what need have I for silver?* He gave them all the silver he had collected from his debts.

By the time he reached home, it was New Year’s Eve. The following day, on the first day of the new year, he summoned his kinsmen, neighbors, and all who owed him money. Those unable to repay their debts had their bonds burned. Those who had sold their fields but lacked the full price were permitted to redeem them; if they could not, he increased the sum owed.

He said, “Let me not burden those who come after.” Again he called his family together and entrusted them with his final affairs. When this was done, he addressed the assembly: “Today I take eternal leave of you all—let each of us make merry to the full.” Thereupon he set out wine and drank joyously, at ease throughout the day, and only at dusk did the gathering disperse.

On the third day of the new year, he personally went to the City God’s temple to report his arrival. There, at the left of the gate, he indeed encountered the former messenger, whose countenance was grim and sorrowful. The messenger said to him, “You have brought trouble upon me! I, moved by a moment’s compassion, altered the order of your fate. Yesterday the City God, citing your saving of the couple’s lives and the burning of the debt records, memorialized the Eastern Peak. The Eastern Peak replied, ‘This man was already dead—how could he perform such virtuous deeds?’ He commanded the City God to conduct an investigation, and for my leaking the celestial decree and trifling with the law, I have been exiled to Yunnan. As for you, your lifespan has been extended by twelve years, and your descendants’ names have been entered into the register of official emoluments. You must redouble your efforts in doing good. Twelve years hence, I shall meet you here again.” With that, he vanished.

The elder returned home and suffered no further ills. Before long, both his sons passed the district examinations in succession, and his household grew ever more prosperous. Twelve years later, on New Year’s Day, he dreamed once more of the former messenger, who came to announce: “The Lord on High, seeing that since your lifespan was extended you have abstained from killing, released living creatures, and strenuously performed good deeds, has appointed you City God of such-and-such a county. Now that your allotted years in the world of light have come to an end, you shall assume your post. Three days hence, I will come with an escort to receive you.” Upon waking, he summoned his children and grandchildren to his bedside and calmly entrusted them with his final affairs. When the appointed day arrived, he bathed, dressed in his finest robes, and sat upright in wait. Suddenly, faint strains of drums and music reached his ears. The elder said, “The escort has arrived!” Then, with a smile, he passed away in perfect serenity.

The Master of the Sitting Flowers remarked: “This is a tale many in my hometown and among my kinsmen can recount. That a man granted but a few days should, through his goodness, have his life extended—how generous is Heaven’s recompense! And that he should escape the realm of hungry ghosts to receive an office in the netherworld, awaiting his summons in full regalia while music heralds his departure—compared to the howling wind and snow of twelve years before, when he nearly perished in his solitary boat, the contrast could not be more vast. If this be so, what should deter men from doing good?”

Four: Jealousy and Villainy Lead to Wrongful Killing

How could a young maiden bear to lose her way at night?
A moment’s misjudgment—her head was severed outright.
For one person’s sake, four lives were cruelly undone;
The character for "knife" within "jealousy" is no false pun.

There were two clerks, Chen and Zhou, born in the same village, living in the same hamlet, both serving as scribes in the Yuzhou Judicial Commission. Whenever their shifts changed and they traveled to and from work, they always agreed to journey together. That year, as they were returning from their shift, Chen delayed for some business and urged Zhou to go ahead, so Zhou set off alone. He rode a mare, reins in hand, proceeding at an unhurried pace. At a fork in the road, he encountered a young woman riding a stallion, clip-clopping toward him. The stallion, seeing the mare, followed her, and the woman did not notice. As dusk fell, the woman asked Zhou, “How much farther to such-and-such a village?” Zhou, surprised, said, “You’ve taken the wrong road! That village lies to the west, yet this place is fifty *li* away. With night falling and no companion, I fear you’ll never reach it!” Hearing Zhou’s words, the woman was startled and began to weep. “What am I to do?” she cried. Zhou said, “I have a small farmstead about two *li* from here. Come with me for now, and stay the night at my tenant’s house. Tomorrow, you can follow the road to your destination—would that be acceptable?” The woman had no choice but to agree. Zhou then said, “You are a young woman, and I am a lone traveler. My tenant might grow suspicious. Why not pretend to be my relative?” The woman reluctantly consented.

They then hurried together to the farmstead. Zhou said to the tenant, “On my way home from duty, I met my cousin who was heading to such-and-such a village but lost her way. Fortunately, she ran into me, so I’ve brought her here to stay the night. She’ll leave at the fifth watch tomorrow.” The tenant replied, “This rustic dwelling is cramped, with few rooms—I fear we’ll be poor hosts!” He then led the woman inside to a bedroom. Outside the room stood another chamber, where he set up a bed by the door and placed Zhou’s luggage upon it.

At the fifth watch, the tenant rose and called for Zhou, but received no answer. Searching the main hall, he found it utterly still and empty. Carrying a lamp, he entered the bedroom to look—there, the two lay side by side on the kang, dead at some unknown hour. Blood soaked the pillows and mat, their throats slit yet not fully severed. Terrified, the tenant thought to himself, “These two arrived under cover of night, with no witnesses. Better to bury them and erase all trace—then I’ll avoid entanglement.” In haste, he carried the corpses out and dug a pit to bury them.

The next day, he led the mules to the market to sell them. By chance, Chen was returning home and, passing through the market, spotted the mules. Assuming Zhou had already returned, he went to inquire at his house. “He hasn’t come back,” they said. Chen replied, “But his mules are at the market—how can you say he hasn’t returned?” Together, they went to the market and saw the mules were indeed there. Asking where they had come from, they traced the trail to the tenant’s home. After repeated questioning, the tenant could no longer conceal the truth and confessed everything as it had happened.

The magistrate was informed and went to inspect the site, where two corpses were indeed unearthed—one was Zhou, and the other a monk in a narrow-sleeved robe with wide cuffs, his head completely shaven. The crowd erupted in shock and clamor. The magistrate ordered the monk’s corpse displayed in the public square and offered a reward for anyone who could identify him. Someone recognized him as a disciple of a monk from a certain temple, and a warrant was swiftly issued to arrest his master. When brought before the court, the master’s face paled, and his words faltered. Threatened with torture, he confessed the truth: “Last night, my disciple and I were at a certain household performing repentance rituals. On our way back, we passed this spot and heard groans from beneath the ground. We dug it open and found a man and woman, the woman still alive. I intended to conceal her for immoral purposes, but my disciple objected, so I killed him and buried them both.” When asked where the woman was, he replied, “She is hiding in the temple.” Officers were sent to search and found her.

Though the monk’s case was resolved, the killer of Zhou remained unidentified. The magistrate questioned the tenant about who had originally lived in the house. “It was my younger sister,” he replied. She was summoned—about twenty years old and quite attractive. Ordered to be confined in a secure chamber, she was examined at night by a midwife, who confirmed she was no longer a virgin. Pressed to reveal her lover, the woman could not conceal the truth and admitted it was a certain man from the western neighbor’s household. He was arrested that very night, and upon interrogation, he confessed at once. It turned out that he had entered the woman’s room at night and, seeing two people sleeping head to head, suspected her of infidelity. Consumed by jealousy, he killed them both, never imagining his mistake.

The case was adjudicated: the neighbor and the monk were both sentenced to death for causing fatalities through illicit affairs, while the tenant was charged with the crime of moving corpses. The chronicler of these events remarked, “As the ancients said, ‘Lust leads to murder.’ How true!”

When Master Zhou chanced upon that beauty, inviting her to spend the night at the tenant’s home—was this not the most extraordinary encounter of his life? Yet their joy lasted but a fleeting moment before lives were lost in an instant. The man who entered her chamber turned their bed into a battleground, while the one who heard the sounds abandoned mercy for cruelty. Desire flared, and all drowned together in the sea of karmic retribution. A single woman’s straying led to four deaths—how could one not tremble? How could one not take warning?

Five: A Son’s Death and Rebirth
Nine Demons Left by Ancestral Wickedness—
To Ruin a House, Waste is the Sure Path;
Better to Exhaust Wealth in Alms and Relief—
Five Sons Reborn, Their Blessings Overflow

At Lotus Lake, Duke Wang Wenjian’s forebear, a certain elder, once held wealth that dominated the countryside. After reaching his twentieth year, he fathered nine sons in succession, yet all proved dull and slow-witted. In a certain year of the Qianlong reign, famine struck severely, and many bore the gaunt look of starvation. The elder spent his entire fortune to relieve his fellow villagers, saving countless lives, though his own household thereby declined into reduced circumstances.

Before long, his eldest son fell ill and died. Within a year, all nine sons perished one after another, leaving not a single survivor. The couple grieved in bitterness, their minds dazed as if deranged. Having long venerated the Great Compassionate Bodhisattva Guanyin, they composed a memorial, burning it before her image—its words laden with sorrow and resentment.

That night, the couple dreamed that the Great Bodhisattva admonished them, saying: "The nine sons you bore in the past were nine demons—all prodigal scoundrels. Due to a hidden transgression committed by your ancestor, they were sent to ruin your household. Yet because you exhausted your family’s wealth to relieve others, your hidden virtue was vast, and the Celestial Emperor specially commanded the responsible authorities to reclaim those demonic spirits and instead bestow upon you literary men to revive your posterity. If you further cultivate good karma, in twenty years, the Star of Literature shall be born into your family. Do not resent this."

The couple’s dreams were identical. From then on, they redoubled their diligence in doing good, ever cautious and reverent. They aided the imperiled and rescued the distressed, tirelessly striving as if fearing they might fall short. Before long, several of their wives and concubines conceived in succession. Within a few years, they bore five more sons, all of whom studied the classics and composed fine essays, earning renown in the academies. In the next generation, Lord Wenjian achieved the highest honors in the imperial examinations and rose to the rank of Minister. His younger brother likewise...

...both achieved the rank of *huìyuán* that very year, and their descendants have since maintained an unbroken line of official eminence.

**VI. The Palace Examination Scroll Written in Pale Ink**
How perilous—seizing the moment to topple another!
Hard-won imperial words, sharp with rebuke.
Yet pale ink alone sealed the favor of connections,
While master and disciples, brothers in deed, shared equal renown.

When Lord Wenjian Wang and his younger brother Yiwu were students, they had already earned distinction in the academies. The education commissioner, Master Donggao Dou, held them in high regard, and they were known together as the "Two Wangs." After passing the provincial examinations, they sat for the *yǐmǎo* metropolitan examination. Dou, now chief examiner, set the first question on the passage: *"What the people love, the ruler must love."* During his tenure as commissioner, he had visited Huzhou Prefecture to inspect the schools and lecture on the classics, arriving at a unique interpretation of this passage. Now, by coincidence, it became the examination topic. Among all the candidates, only the Two Wangs’ answers aligned with his own understanding. When the results were announced, Yiwu placed first as *huìyuán*, while Wenjian ranked second. Those who failed the examination soon spread groundless rumors.

At the time, Prime Minister Xiang was in power. Disliking Dou’s unyielding integrity and refusal to curry favor, he sought a way to undermine him. Hearing the rumors, he rejoiced and promptly reported them to the emperor.

The Pure Ancestor was swayed and demoted Dou from office, while Yiwu was banished to return to his native place to study. On the day of the palace examination, Wenjian, mindful that his teacher had incurred official censure and been exiled in low spirits, wrote his paper in pale ink, finishing hastily with no further ambition. Coincidentally, Prime Minister Xiang had a former tutor who also passed the metropolitan examination that year. Xiang instructed him, “If you write your palace examination paper in pale ink, you may secure the top rank.” When the papers were reviewed, Wenjian’s was assumed to be the tutor’s and was placed among the first rank. On the day the results were announced, the Pure Ancestor, seeing the pale ink, expressed doubt. Xiang immediately praised it from the side, saying, “For one to write so solemnly and elegantly in pale ink—twice as difficult as using dark ink—he must be a scholar of profound learning.” The Pure Ancestor agreed, and Wenjian was thus selected first. When his name was called as Wang Yixian, the emperor turned to Xiang and demanded sternly, “Was this also the work of Dou Guangnai?” Xiang fell silent, not daring to utter a word. Thereupon Dou’s grievance was fully vindicated, and the groundless rumors ceased.

How extreme it is! The deeds of petty men invariably bring fortune to the noble-minded. Yet petty men only wrong themselves in vain!

**7. Respect for Teachers Rewarded**
*Long attendance at the lecture mat—past karma’s thread,*
*Decades of heart’s blood bequeathed, essays handed down instead.*
*To all you champions who seized the topmost crown,*
*Know this: “respect your teacher” holds the true key’s sound.*

A certain provincial graduate from Zhejiang had, from childhood, been tutored by a renowned teacher engaged by his father. The teaching was rigorous, and the father treated the teacher with utmost reverence. From his first lessons to his earliest compositions, the graduate never changed teachers. When he came of age and became a student of the Imperial Academy, the teacher entrusted him with all the examination essays he had written in his life. He further selected several dozen of his finest pieces, instructing the student to memorize them thoroughly, saying, “All my life’s essence is contained herein. If you encounter these in the examination hall, they will secure you the top rank.” The graduate received them and soon committed them all to memory. Before long, the teacher fell gravely ill. The graduate and his father tended to his medical care with utmost sincerity and care. Upon his death, they prepared his coffin and burial with meticulous attention. They also generously supported his widow and children, providing for them as generously as when the teacher was alive, continuing for years without wearying.

In the year he passed the provincial examination, the elder gentleman dreamed of his teacher coming to express gratitude, saying, “Previously, I entrusted several dozen of my essays to your son. I fear he may forget them over time, so you should urge him to study them diligently—this is how he may repay me.” Upon waking, he asked the graduate, who confirmed it was true. He then recounted the dream and pressed him to continue reading. By the seventh month, the graduate had mastered only six or seven of them. Planning to set them aside for the time being, that very night the elder gentleman dreamed again of his teacher’s arrival, who said, “Yesterday, I saw your son reading my essays late into the night, yet he has not fully memorized them. The examination draws near—you must supervise him closely and ensure not a single piece is neglected.” Upon waking, he reproached the graduate, who, left with no choice, committed them all to memory.

When the examination came, two of the three questions in the first session matched essays from his teacher’s collection. He transcribed them exactly, but one question on Mencius remained. Exerting all his effort for a full day, he composed an answer to rival his teacher’s work, their skill and depth perfectly matched. When the results were announced, he indeed placed first.

The Master of the Sitting-among-Flowers remarked: “As the world’s customs decline, wealthy families indulge in feasting, revelry, and sensual pleasures, spending thousands of taels without hesitation. Yet when it comes to hiring teachers to instruct their sons, they haggle over every penny. Compared to the elder gentleman’s treatment of his teacher, can this not be called loyal and reverent? That he ultimately reaped his just reward was only fitting!”

Eight. The Intendant’s Clerk

A certain man served as a clerk in the provincial intendance, his life a litany of unchecked villainy. Wielding power and twisting law to his ends, he struck fear into both officials and commoners alike. In the provincial capital stood the Hall of Pure Integrity, a refuge for widows with nowhere else to turn. Through backstairs influence, he secured the directorship of the hall. Within a few years, he had siphoned off most of its funds into his own purse, leaving the women within barely enough to survive. His eye then fell upon a young and wealthy nun in a certain convent. He seduced and debauched her, then took her as his second wife. Whenever an official committed even the slightest misstep, he would extort them relentlessly; if his demands were not met, he would entrap them with draconian charges, often driving them to their deaths. His wickedness knew no bounds, its full extent too vast to recount.

He had two grown sons who, witnessing their father’s misdeeds, frequently admonished him. Enraged, he cast them both out of the house to live apart.

In the spring of the *guiyou* year of the Jiaqing reign, the household was struck by *Huilu* (the fire deity, hence "to suffer a conflagration"). The husband, his wife, and their daughter, along with several maidservants and retainers, were all consumed in the flames. Earlier, there had been an elderly servant woman in the household, over seventy years of age, who recited the Buddha’s name and inclined toward goodness. She often disapproved of her master’s conduct. One evening, in the main hall, she saw a man with a crimson face and clad in a scarlet robe—just like the fire deity depicted in statues—and in great alarm, she fled to avoid him. The next day, she told her master, adding, “You should guard against fire.” He rebuked her as delusional and drove her away. Another woman was hired in her place, but three days after entering service, she too perished in the fire.

Every night, the household’s inner and outer doors were locked. When the fire broke out, the family begged to have the doors opened to seek help. Fearing that looters would take advantage of the chaos, he stubbornly refused. By the time officials arrived and ordered soldiers to break down the door, the maidservants, his wife, and daughter had all burned to death. Only he remained alive. The soldiers dragged him out, but suddenly he remembered that important case documents were stored in the upper chamber of the inner building. If they were lost, he would be exiled for the offense. At that moment, the inner building still stood unharmed, so he rushed back inside to retrieve them. No sooner had he ascended the stairs than the wind whipped the flames straight into the inner chamber, burning away the staircase. The officials shouted for help, but those gathered around could find no way to intervene. Soon after, the building collapsed, and he fell into the fire and died. The next day, when his remains were examined, his head, face, and limbs were all charred, leaving only his torso intact—like a scorched log. The retribution was indeed grim!

This fire consumed only the household of this man; the neighboring homes on either side were untouched. His two sons, having moved out earlier, escaped the disaster. Some said, “His ancestors had accumulated hidden virtue, so the retribution fell upon him alone.” The Master of *Zuohua* sighed, “Alas!”

The benevolence of Heaven’s heart may indeed be called perfect! Take this man, for instance—his power relied upon the city walls and the altar of the soil, like the foxes that burrow beneath the ramparts and the rats that nest within the sacred shrine, shielded by the sanctity of their lairs. His crimes had amassed like mountains, and his retribution was nearly more terrible than the burning of the forest, his body reduced to naught but a charred log. Yet his household still had worthy sons to carry on the family line, inheriting the hidden virtue of their ancestors. The homes on either side, though sharing walls, were spared the raging winds and spreading flames. As for the old woman who chanted the Buddha’s name and practiced goodness, she was allowed to witness the calamity from afar, her person untouched by the disaster. When misfortune or error strikes, even the law may grant pardon; how then could misfortune reach the fish in the pond?

"Those who share in evil must be punished—guilt is as indistinguishable as raccoon-dogs from the same mound. Whether spared or not, all depends on what each person has brought upon themselves; how could Heaven be said to take sides?"

---

**Nine. The Venerable Wu of the Seal**

A governor’s father, a worthy grandson ascends to the lofty halls of the imperial archives—
Yet his trade was but salt and meat, a humble livelihood.
Who could have matched Wu Litao of Suzhou?

Wu Litao of Wu Gate (蠡, pronounced *li*), father of a governor, made his living selling salt and meat. Though he mingled with market traders, he delighted in goodness and gave generously, tireless in his efforts. Whatever remained after providing for his own clothing and food, he bestowed upon the poor, and the villagers all called him a virtuous man. He had two sons, both of whom passed the highest imperial examinations. The elder, named Jun, became a governor; the younger, Shuxuan, served as a departmental official before being appointed chief examiner of the Shaanxi region, later rising to the rank of salt commissioner.

Sun Cihe entered the Hanlin Academy and rose to the rank of expositor-in-waiting. The venerable father repeatedly received imperial favors, a reward for his abundant virtue, it is said.

---

**X. The Incense Shop**

Cast out by dog, kept by man—
A petty gain sought, wrath from the gods began.
Had the husband heeded not his wife’s plea,
No cause would explain her death and his ruin’s decree.

In Wuxing, a certain couple ran an incense shop. One day, as they dried incense in an open space outside their door, a dog urinated upon it. A neighbor informed the husband, who wished to discard the tainted goods. The wife, however, mindful of their capital, insisted they keep it. Persuaded by her words, the husband mixed the soiled incense with the good and sold it all within days. Before two months had passed, their shop was consumed by fire. It was the height of summer, and the wife was bathing in an upper chamber when the flames took her. After the fire subsided, only a single foot of her remains could be found; the rest had turned to ash. Those who understood such matters deemed it retribution for defiling the sacred.

---

**XI. Three Accounts of Usurious Exploitation**

Wealth amassed through usury seems secure,
Yet in twenty years, how many fields endure?
A son born to squander gold like dust—
Can greed’s riches ever long outlast trust?

In times of flood and drought, when famine reigns,
A single peck of grain buys boundless plains.
Nearing seventy, a profligate heir appears—
Who then can say man’s schemes outlast heaven’s years?

Jiang Yuanlong, a native of Zhangyan in Jinshan, grew wealthy through diligent tillage. Much of his land was acquired through shrewd calculation. He also lent at exorbitant interest, watching for those with fine fields or estates who had fallen into hardship, then offering them loans. The interest was steep; those who defaulted faced compounding debts, and when repayment became impossible, he seized their property. Thus, through accumulation, he acquired thousands of *mu* of land within twenty years.

Later, Jiang bore a son named Dezhang, who took no interest in the family’s livelihood or estate. Scarcely had he reached twenty when he made gambling and dissipation his sole pursuits. Each time he ventured out, he carried several deeds of his fields as stakes, often wagering a single deed for ten taels of silver. When his silver ran out at the gaming table, he would return the next day to sign the contract—only for his opponent to deceive him, saying, “Did you not borrow fifty taels from me yesterday? How could you forget so soon?” Without protest, Dezhang would draw up a contract for fifty taels and hand it over. Seeing how easily he was duped, others swarmed to cheat him. Within a decade, he squandered his entire inheritance and died.

At Huangyan Bridge in Danyang, there lived a man named Zhou Shengzhang, whose family had modest means. In a certain year of the Qianlong reign, the wheat harvest was abundant, and the price of wheat fell to two hundred *wen* per *shi*. Shengzhang owned a hundred *mu* of land, yielding twice the usual harvest. In Danyang and Jintan counties, people relied on wheat as their staple. That year, Shengzhang encountered several windfalls and hoarded nearly four thousand *shi* of wheat. The following year brought famine; not a grain was reaped in spring or autumn, and both rice and wheat soared in price. Shengzhang kept his granaries sealed, refusing to sell. That winter, the Grand Canal ran shallow, halting merchant traffic, and wheat seed nearly vanished—save for Shengzhang’s hoard. The villagers of the surrounding hamlets came begging for loans. At first, he refused, but after repeated pleas, he agreed to exchange one *shi* of wheat—mixed with chaff and husks—for one *mu* of land. When his wheat was exhausted, the contracts filled his chests, and he had acquired five thousand *mu*. By nature frugal and skilled in accumulation, within a few years his fields exceeded ten thousand *mu*, and his gold and silver piled like mountains. Yet he had no heir. After countless prayers, in his old age he finally fathered a son. Born when he was sixty-eight, the child was named Liu Ba. Before Liu Ba turned ten, Shengzhang died. As he grew older, he treated gold and silver as dirt. Each outing required heavy purses, which he emptied before returning home. If on some days he had nothing to spend, he would fling the coins into the field ridges.

At the time, the community granary system was in effect, and the wealthiest man in each village was appointed as its steward. Liu Ba was thus entrusted with the role. The villagers, taking advantage of his youth and folly, borrowed grain from the granary, then colluded to default on repayment each autumn, leaving him to cover endless losses. Fond of gambling, he would wager a thousand taels in a single throw. As his family’s fortunes dwindled, he sold off his estates—so hastily that contracts were not even drafted, and deeds were carved onto wooden blocks for sale. By the time he died, he had not a single rafter of a house or a *mu* of land to his name.

When my father served as magistrate’s clerk in Danyang, Liu Ba’s son had no means of survival and was reduced to serving as a gatekeeper to eke out a living. To this day, when the villagers speak of prodigal sons, they curse them as “Liu Ba.”

Zhao Yankui was a native of Caojing in Huating. He amassed his fortune through the illicit salt trade and tyrannized his village. A man of the Yu clan, quite wealthy, resided in Zhangjiasha while maintaining over ten bays of houses in Caojing. Yankui coveted them. Yu had an orphaned grandson, young and dissolute. Yankui instructed his son, Song, to entice the youth into debauchery and gambling. When the grandson had no funds, Yankui lent him tens of thousands, demanding the house deeds as collateral. Within a year, the houses became his. He then invested heavily in renovations, completing them in the fourth month of the *gengxu* year. The mansion, resplendent and grand, stood as a veritable manor. That autumn, the authorities, branding Yankui a ringleader of illicit salt traffickers, pursued him relentlessly. Some proposed leniency through surrender. Yankui thus turned himself in and was enlisted as a patrol officer.

Having thus overzealously pursued the suppression of illicit salt, his former henchmen—once his claws and fangs—all turned into bitter enemies. They gathered in force and tore down his house. The fruits of years of extortion vanished in an instant, leaving only a heap of rubble.

Alas! What profit was there in it?

---

XII. White Cloud Hermitage

In drunken revelry, he stumbled before the god,
Foolishly seeking an auspicious lot for imperial exams.
Cursing the deity, he provoked divine wrath—
Through all three sessions, he toiled like a silkworm in spring.

By the lake in my Hangzhou stands White Cloud Hermitage, where the Old Man Under the Moon is enshrined. Its oracle slips draw from classics, histories, and even fine lines of poetry and song. For those seeking success in examinations or marriage, its responses are as swift as an echo. Thus, during the provincial examinations, scholars flocked to pray for divination slips.

A certain Qian, a student of the imperial academy, was arrogant in his talent and unrestrained in conduct. Before the *xinmao* examinations, he toured the lake with friends. After heavy drinking, emboldened by wine, he visited the Old Man’s shrine to draw a slip—only to receive an unfavorable response. He laughed, “How could I not pass?” and drew again, only to receive the same. Enraged, he cried, “Your answers are irrelevant! How can you claim to be efficacious?” He then hurled vulgar insults at the shrine. Finally, he declared, “I’ll draw one more. If it still doesn’t suit me, I’ll smash your statue!” The slip he drew read:

*Enough, enough, enough—
Like a spring silkworm spinning its cocoon,
Till death, it draws out its thread.*

His companions, aghast, knew he had provoked divine wrath and urged him to leave. That autumn, during the third session of the examinations, Qian entered the latrine. Suddenly, as if seized by something, he shrieked and staggered out. Returning to his cell, he sat in silence. When the proctor heard no sound, he lifted the curtain—only to find Qian dead. Though the cause remains unknown, his audacity in insulting the deity revealed his lifelong recklessness. The Old Man Under the Moon’s numinous power is truly fearsome!

---

XIII. Madame Yan

She dared to open the granary to relieve famine,
Relying on all to distribute aid justly.
When guilt came, she bore it alone—
Willing to ruin her estate to replenish the public store.

Governor Yan Jingfu, upon first assuming office as magistrate of Pingdu Prefecture in Shandong, was upright, enlightened, compassionate, and benevolent—possessing the virtues of an ancient model official. His mother, Madame Yan, resided with him in the yamen, constantly instructing her son in benevolence and kindness.

In the fifth month of a certain year during the Qianlong reign, the governor traveled to the provincial capital on official business. Suddenly, a great flood struck the prefecture, sweeping away countless homes. Tens of thousands of villagers fled into the city, but the waters continued to rise until only three planks of the city walls remained above the surface. With no food to be had, the refugees’ wails shook heaven and earth. The officials stood helpless, at a loss for what to do.

Madame Yan, upon hearing this, immediately ordered the release of grain from the Ever-Normal Granary to relieve the starving. Her staff advisors objected, saying, “This requires an official petition and awaits approval before action can be taken. Moreover, the magistrate is not present in the yamen—who would dare act without authorization?” Madame Yan, incensed upon hearing this, declared, “The Ever-Normal Granary was established precisely for emergencies. Now, tens of thousands wail in hunger, waiting to be fed. If we must wait for approval, will they not all become corpses of the starved? Our family is quite wealthy—should the authorities hold us accountable for acting without authorization, selling our entire estate would suffice to repay the loss. If you fear my son may object, let this old woman bear the responsibility alone; it has nothing to do with any of you.” She then commanded the assistant officials to be summoned and addressed them in person. The officials, tongues tied in astonishment, dared not speak. Madame Yan, her anger unabated, said, “Have no fear of being implicated. If there is any consequence, I shall ensure my son bears it alone. Your duty is merely to assist this old woman in overseeing the distribution.” The crowd, left with no choice, complied with her orders.

At once, cheers erupted like thunder, and all rejoiced in their rebirth. The gentry and wealthy families of the city, moved by Madame Yan’s virtue, also donated grain to supplement what the authorities could not provide. After seven days, the floodwaters receded, and the grain stores were entirely depleted.

The governor, upon receiving the report in the provincial capital, hastened home. Entering his residence, his advisors informed him of the grain distribution. The governor smiled and said, “What my mother did was entirely proper! Quickly draft a report for me, stating the facts as they are. I shall immediately send someone back to our hometown to sell our estate to make restitution—have no fear, gentlemen.”

When the report reached the governor-general and provincial treasurer, they were greatly alarmed and swiftly memorialized the throne, impeaching him for unauthorized use of the granary stores. The Pure Emperor read the memorial and commended the act, writing in vermilion: “As a high official of the frontier, you have such a worthy mother and capable subordinates—why would you impeach rather than commend them?” He then issued an edict permitting the distributed grain to be accounted as a regular expenditure, with no need for restitution.

The governor, deeply grateful for the imperial favor, redoubled his efforts in virtuous governance. When the emperor later made his eastern tour, the governor—by then transferred to Jinan Prefecture—was summoned for an audience. The emperor inquired in detail about the earlier events and specially bestowed a plaque upon Madame Yan to honor her. Later, the governor was repeatedly promoted, eventually rising to the post of governor of Guizhou.

The governor’s son, Jian, rose from a departmental clerk to the governorship of Zhili; his grandson, Bofu, advanced from the Hanlin Academy to the governorship of Fujian; and his great-nephew, Yiyou, was appointed as the Director-General of the Eastern River Conservancy after serving as a secretary in the Grand Secretariat. Many others among her descendants, whether serving in the capital as Hanlin academicians or departmental clerks, or holding provincial posts as surveillance commissioners or prefectural governors, all owed their success to Madame Yan’s lifelong accumulation of virtue.

**Fourteen. Following Precedent in Two Cases**
*An official kept his hands clean and never took bribes—*
*Why then was his line cut off after his death?*
*He submitted a memorial to the City God to inquire about karmic retribution,*
*Only to be told that following precedent was no excuse.*

Fei Gong of Gui’an began his career as a county magistrate and rose to the rank of judicial commissioner. By nature, he was incorruptible and refused private petitions. Once entrusted with the authority of the judiciary, he upheld the law in every case without bending the rules. In his old age, he remained childless. After retiring from office, he reflected on his decades of service and wondered: if he had maintained such unblemished integrity, why had he been repaid with the punishment of a sonless lineage? He then submitted a formal memorial to the City God’s temple to plead his case.

That night, he dreamed that an underling from the City God’s temple summoned him. Upon arrival, he saw the deity descend the steps to welcome him inside. Once seated, the god addressed him: “I read your memorial and sensed your indignation, so I specially invited you here to settle the matter. Your refusal to take bribes or show favoritism is a heart that could face Heaven itself. Yet you wielded judicial power for years—what did you rely upon to honor your lord and protect the people?”

Fei replied, “Nothing more than handling every matter according to precedent.”

The god smiled and said, “Your lack of descendants is precisely because of these three words: *following precedent*.”

Startled, Fei asked, “Then should precedent not be followed?”

The god replied, “That is not what I meant.”

Fei, a scholar, have you not heard that *laws establish great statutes, while rites accord with human sentiment*? The ignorant common folk, unwittingly ensnared in the web of law—if every matter is handled by precedent, how can the people endure it? Moreover, as you oversee the judicial authority, can you guarantee that local officials never wrongly condemn the innocent? And with your excessive self-assurance, when cases border on the ambiguous, you decide them by your own judgment—how many among them might be blameless yet executed? Measured against the sage’s principle of *when in doubt, punish lightly*, this seems not the way. *"When the waters are too clear, there are no fish"*—this is of your own making, so it is no wonder that Heaven’s Dao has misaligned your fate."

Fei fell silent, deeply regretting his ways.

The god then consoled him: "Throughout your life, you have been upright and impartial. In the future, you and I shall share the same official bond, honored with offerings in this region—what need have you of descendants?" With that, he ordered an underling to escort Fei home.

Upon waking, Fei’s desire for an heir finally ceased. He ultimately adopted his nephew as his successor. On his deathbed, he saw before his couch what appeared to be those seeking redress for grievances. When he rebuked them, they revealed themselves as six bandits from his tenure as judicial commissioner in a certain province—men whose crimes did not warrant death, yet whom he had condemned by the letter of the law. Knowing his end was near, Fei called for his robes and cap, donned them, and passed away. Later, it was said he became the City God of that prefecture.

Long ago, there was an official named Zhang, who served as judicial commissioner in Henan. In every case, he enforced the law with unyielding severity, showing no leniency—especially to the powerful or the sons of wealthy families. At the time, the prohibition against heterodox teachings was strictly enforced. A rich man was accused by his neighbor of practicing such teachings with seditious intent. Hearing of his wealth, Zhang arrested and harshly tortured him. The prefect and the provincial inspector, aware of the false accusation, pleaded his case to Zhang. Zhang merely scoffed, "Is that so?"

A mere commoner at that—yet able to make an *observateur* and a prefect exert themselves on his behalf—this was just like the household of Guo Jie in the time of Emperor Wu of Han, which was certainly not poor. The emperor had said: "Guo Jie is but a man of the streets, yet his influence extends to making a general speak for him—how can his household be called poor?" In the end, he was relocated. Here, too, the judicial commissioner fabricated evidence to secure the rich man’s conviction. The rich man died, his family was exiled to the frontier, and the injustice was widely lamented.

Later, one of his grandsons, serving as a *zhubu* awaiting appointment in our Zhejiang, fathered three sons. When the father died, the youngest son, fearing his two elder brothers would forbid his affair with a maidservant, poisoned them to death. The crime was exposed, and he was executed in the marketplace. Thus, their line came to an end.

Those who knew the judicial commissioner’s life all said this was the retribution for his purity carried to cruel excess.

---

**15. Yang the Judicial Commissioner**
A governor’s greed for a hundred thousand in gold
Brought the sentence of execution upon himself.

Who could have foreseen that on the following day, the yin spirits would arrive—
Striking the pillar, his skull split open, brains spilling across the hall.

In the late years of the Qianlong reign, bandits ran rampant across the Jiangsu-Zhejiang waters. Under imperial orders to capture them, Yang Tianxiang, vice commander of Chongming, made the arrest. Brigadier Chen Dayong submitted an urgent memorial to the throne, though in haste he omitted the governor-general’s title. The governor-general, old and avaricious, resented Chen’s solo memorial and sought to undermine him. Coincidentally, an edict arrived instructing the Jiangnan governor to review the case and execute the bandits. The bandits then bribed the governor-general with one hundred thousand taels of silver. He accepted the bribe and was determined to overturn the verdict. At that time, a certain prefect of Yangzhou, recently promoted from censor to an external post, paid his respects to the governor-general. The governor-general mentioned the case, hinting at doubts. The prefect hastily replied, “The Green Standard troops often falsely accuse the innocent to claim merit. Your Excellency should investigate thoroughly to avoid wronging the common folk.” The governor-general was delighted and immediately entrusted the case to him.

By then, the bandits had already received word from the governor-general. They fabricated charges against law-abiding coastal residents, fishermen by trade, framing them as the true culprits. Under torture, the fishermen falsely confessed. The prefect, swayed by the governor-general’s earlier remarks, believed the lie and ruled that the fishermen had been falsely accused as bandits. The governor-general promptly released the bandits from prison while impeaching the brigadier and vice commander, demanding their dismissal and punishment.

The governor-general then executed Tianxiang at the coastal port. The brigadier, accused of shielding his subordinate, was stripped of his rank and exiled to the military frontier. The day after Tianxiang’s death, the governor-general went to offer incense. As he was about to mount his sedan, he suddenly rebuked his attendants: “Master Yang has arrived—why did you not announce him?” He turned and walked away as if accompanying a guest. Upon reaching the flower hall, he first made bowing gestures, his lips murmuring as though arguing with someone. Then he began grappling with the air, striking himself across both cheeks until they swelled. After a long while, he cried out, “I was wrong to take the bandits’ gold and let you die! I deserve death! I shall repay your life!” He tore at his own hair, then said, “Stop pulling! I’m going! I’m going!” With that, he dashed his head against a pillar, brains spilling out as he died. At once, all knew it was Tianxiang exacting vengeance.

A year later, the bandit suddenly appeared at the Shandong governor’s yamen to surrender, confessing in detail how he had been captured in Jiangnan and bribed his way to freedom. The governor, unwilling to stir up a major case, executed the bandit while suppressing the matter. Only the Yangzhou prefect ultimately ended his career in honor. For the prefect had always been upright; in reviewing this case, he had not deliberately curried favor with the governor-general. Rather, having long served in the capital, he was all too familiar with the cowardice of provincial Green Standard troops and their habit of framing the innocent to claim merit. Thus, his stubborn prejudice led to this miscarriage of justice. His error was unintentional, so retribution did not fall upon him—yet his reputation never flourished. Moreover, in the year of Tianxiang’s death, a son was born to him, wild and unruly, nearly ruining the family.

The prefect shared years of friendship with my family. I often saw him recounting his chronicle, still claiming this case as a wrong righted. The Master of Sitting Among Flowers said: “From childhood, I knew well the injustice of this case. In my later years, while traveling briefly to Loudong, elders told me: ‘Tianxiang and the brigadier had long been beloved by the troops. When Tianxiang faced execution, soldiers from both the brigadier’s and vice commander’s battalions cried injustice and beat the drums, even offering to surrender their rations. For a time, weapons piled high as mountains, nearly sparking a mutiny. Fortunately, the brigadier knelt in supplication to calm them, and they finally returned to their ranks.’ Alas!”

The avaricious and senescent govern through bribes to succeed, while those who uphold integrity are sidelined by one-sided judgments. Thus, wrongs arise from the infamous "three characters." The brigadier was imprisoned, and turmoil nearly erupted as soldiers cast off their helmets. Vengeance was ultimately exacted by dashing against the pillar. Yet even the upright and unyielding prefect nearly met the same grim fate. How, then, can those who adjudicate cases fail to exercise utmost caution?

Sixteen. A Certain Bureau Director
When a matter concerns the public esteem that brings renown,
How could one privately bend the rules for kin?
A brother might seize the chance to gain an early foothold—
Yet no scheme escapes the knowledge of Heaven.

Under the established regulations, officials of the sixth rank and below—including assistant and miscellaneous functionaries—as well as those awaiting appointment in educational posts, were exempted from presenting themselves at the Ministry of Personnel to submit credentials. Instead, they were selected in turn through the standard examination process. Once assigned a vacancy, their documents were forwarded by the governor or governor-general of their native province, a measure designed to show consideration and spare minor officials the hardship of travel and the burden of waiting. The intent was most commendable.

Yet those awaiting appointment, unaware of the selection schedule, might serve as private secretaries in another province, relocate with their families, or even fall ill or die without filing a report. After a vacancy was filled, repeated inquiries might yield no trace of them, leaving many posts unoccupied.

When a certain gentleman from Fujian served as Director of Selection, his younger brother, having qualified under the same regulations, found his name placed too far down the list to secure a post. The Director then proposed a new rule: candidates for educational posts awaiting appointment must annually submit a sworn statement from their native prefecture and county, attested by the local authorities and forwarded through the governor or governor-general to the Ministry, confirming their presence in the province and absence of misconduct. Only then would they be eligible for selection. If a candidate ranked higher on the list failed to submit the required documents, the next eligible candidate—even if lower in rank—would be appointed out of turn.

Once the rule was enacted, the Director urged his brother to return home and file the statement. At the time, no other province was aware of the change, and his brother’s documents arrived first. Thus, he leapfrogged the list and secured the post.

Yet for those awaiting appointment, each sworn statement meant another round of exactions. The poor, though aware that the selection period had arrived, often found themselves utterly without resources and unable to comply. Many thus delayed their submission. Those residing in their hometowns or traveling afar were further hindered by their ignorance of the new requirement.

The Selection Bureau chief devised this scheme solely to favor his brother. Before a year had passed, he was exposed by the censors for partiality in handling official appointments, stripped of his post, and exiled to a distant garrison. His brother, having secured the position, was likewise dismissed shortly after taking office—what profit lay in such clandestine machinations?

**17. The Righteous Dog**

Thrice it scratched the gate to sound the alarm,
Yet in disaster’s wake, it seemed to grieve its debt unpaid.
Now human affairs are so topsy-turvy—
Only the dog upholds loyalty unswayed.

My maternal great-grandmother, Madam Chen, kept a yellow dog in her household. Her second elder sister, Madam Wang, doted on it exceedingly. The dog, in turn, displayed a rare understanding of human intent, unlike ordinary hounds.

At the time, a certain servant-woman—an old crone who had fallen out of her master’s favor—would often steal away to utter curses in secret. One evening, after the inner gates had been locked, the dog suddenly began scratching at the door, its howls mournful and urgent. Madam Wang found this strange and personally carried a lamp outside to investigate. No sooner had she unlatched the door than the dog seized her skirt in its jaws, as if tugging her forward. Even more perplexed, she summoned the still-awake servants and said to the dog, “What is the matter? Lead the way!” The dog immediately released her garment and set off ahead.

The party followed the dog to the firewood storage, where smoke was rising. Upon inspection, they found embers placed beneath the pile, and quickly removed them. Though Madam Wang suspected the old crone’s doing, she suppressed her anger and could not bring herself to dismiss her. Several days later, the dog howled again outside the gate. Hurrying to look, they saw the firewood nearly ablaze, but fortunately, the gathered servants extinguished it in time. Still, she did not send the crone away, only tightening inspections each night after the household had settled. Months passed, and vigilance slackened. One night, as everyone slept, Madam Wang suddenly heard the dog’s howls in her dream and awoke with a start. The cries were sharp and desperate. Hastily throwing on her clothes, she rushed out. As the door opened, thick smoke already filled the air. The firewood shed stood beside the main hall, and by the time she called for help, flames had reached the hall’s front gate—no path remained to enter. The dog returned, seizing her skirt and pulling her toward the rear gate, as if guiding her to flee. With no time to think, she and her younger sister each clutched a deed box for their farmland and led the household out through the back. The dog lingered by the gate, watching until all had escaped, then turned back inside. Called to no avail, it perished in the fire—perhaps reproaching itself for its delayed warning, having failed its master and betrayed the kindness of its keep.

---

**18. The Grateful Swine**

A strange tale from He Laibai:
*An official’s procession blocked—*
*How odd! In Changzhou, Magistrate Lü Youxin*
*Received deep kindness, repaid it with death—*
*One envies such swine, surpassing men.*

Magistrate Lü Youxin of Changzhou served as Assistant Prefect of Eastern Defense in our Hangzhou. One day, while traveling in his sedan, two swine suddenly prostrated themselves before his palanquin, lifting their heads in a plea for mercy. Whipped, they did not move; driven away, they would not leave. Greatly astonished, he halted the procession and ordered his attendants to investigate their origin. Soon, a butcher was brought forth, who explained that both swine had been purchased from a farming household days prior and were to be slaughtered that very day. They had escaped, he said, never expecting them to disrupt the official’s procession. At the sight of the butcher, the swine cowered even lower, trembling violently, their pitiful cries unceasing. Moved by compassion, Magistrate Lü turned to the butcher and said, “Give the swine to me, and I shall pay you their original price.” He immediately ordered the money fetched, paid the full sum, and took the swine back to the yamen, where they were kept in a pen.

The next morning, as the magistrate rose and entered the front courtyard, the two swine prostrated themselves before him, bowing their heads as if in obeisance. When he shouted at them to leave, they retreated a few paces but remained standing, watching as he entered the inner chambers before returning to their pen. From then on, every dawn they would wait in the front courtyard, bowing as before whenever he emerged. If he returned from outside, they would hear the distant sound of gongs and leap joyfully, as if in welcome.

After leaving office, he called to them, saying, “I am returning home now! I have kept you for years and cannot bear to see you slaughtered again. I shall send you to the Temple of Liberated Life.” The swine hesitated, uttering mournful cries, as if unwilling. Sensing their reluctance, he comforted them, “You do not wish to leave me? Then I shall take you home—how would that be?” The swine immediately bowed their heads, and he brought them back to Changzhou, where they were kept for over a decade. When the magistrate passed away, the two swine wailed day and night, refusing all food. Within days, they starved to death.

The two swine hesitated in distress, their mournful cries unceasing, as if unwilling to go. Magistrate Lü understood their meaning and comforted them, saying, “You do not wish to leave me? Then I shall still bring you back to my hometown—how would that be?” The swine immediately bowed their heads in response. Thus, he brought them back to his old home in Changzhou, where they were kept for over a decade. When Magistrate Lü passed away, the two swine wailed day and night, refusing all food offered to them. Within days, they starved to death.

The Master of Zuohua, reflecting on the cases of Chen and Lü, sighed and said, “Tales of loyal dogs abound in records. Yet among the six livestock, swine are deemed the most dull-witted—yet once granted the grace of life, they repay it with utmost devotion in life and even sacrifice themselves in death, behaving as if they were human. How many among us should feel ashamed in comparison? Today, people often revile others as having the conduct of ‘dogs and swine’—but if we consider the dog of the Chen family and the swine of the Lü family, I fear they would disdain even to rank among humankind!”

Amidst the common flaw of upright officials’ severity,
The noble man has ever prized restraint, not contention.
With virtue too meager to wield the brush of splendor,
Only then does one know how Heaven esteems examination glory.

Renxiang Zhou Yunxiu, having become a live-in son-in-law to the Yu family of Piling (Changzhou), settled there. Yunxiu was deeply mired in drink, and though enrolled as a scholar, he had long ceased to participate in the provincial examinations. In the year *jiyou*, his son also passed the *maocai* examination. Yunxiu then brought him back to Hangzhou, and father and son entered the examination hall together. No sooner had Yunxiu taken his seat in the cell than he began to feel disoriented, muttering incoherently. A candidate in the neighboring cell lifted the curtain to look, whereupon Yunxiu pounded the desk and cursed loudly. Unaware of the demonic possession at first, the neighbor argued back. The proctors and fellow candidates, hearing the commotion, gathered to see. Yunxiu glared wildly around, hurling abuse without cease. Suddenly, he rushed out, wielding his cell’s wooden board to strike at anyone in his path, sending the crowd scattering. Then, abandoning the board, he seized a proctor’s firewood cleaver and began hacking wildly, his manner like that of a madman. The others urgently summoned the officials. At the sight of one clad in an embroidered python robe, Yunxiu leapt for joy, exclaiming, “Good! Good! The proctor has come to arrest you all—how dare you strike and curse me?”

He then pointed in all directions, saying, “It was not I who wished to brawl—you all cursed me first, so I could not but retort. You struck me, so I could not but strike back.” Suddenly, he brandished his fists at the empty air, shouting, “Do you still dare to hit me?” The proctor, at a loss, went to report to the examiner. When the examiner arrived, Yunxiu remained as frenzied as before. With no other recourse, they ordered strong men to restrain him, then petitioned the chief examiner to have his hands and feet bound. He was taken under guard to the Hall of Impartiality, where he continued to hurl abuse, his words now utterly unintelligible.

On the tenth day, the gates opened, and attendants were sent to escort him out. By chance, his son, who had just submitted his own examination papers and was leaving the compound, encountered him. Together with other candidates, they supported Yunxiu back to their lodging. His clansman, Yunji, and two *xiaolian* scholars who admired Tao, having heard the news, hastened to see him. Yunxiu suddenly leapt up, seizing the hands of the two scholars, and cried, “You two have already been bound by the examiner—how have you escaped before the case is settled?” The two men stared in astonishment, while Yunxiu’s madness only worsened. His son, unable to attend the second session, hastily chartered a boat back to Changzhou.

As soon as they boarded, Yunxiu’s spirit cleared at once. When questioned about the events in the examination hall, he was utterly bewildered. All he recalled was a crowd of men and women surrounding him, cursing and striking him, to which he had responded in kind. After returning to Changzhou, he suffered no further ill effects. What karmic cause or consequence this may have been remains unknown.

Yunxiu was prudent, honest, and content with his lot, known only for his love of wine—nothing hidden or wicked lay within him. Some say his father had once served as prefect, upright yet severe, and may have wrongly implicated someone in a crime, thus bringing retribution upon his descendants, preventing them from completing the examinations and attaining official rank.

---

Twenty. **Vice Censor-in-Chief Wang**
*How strange—a scribe’s brush makes a Vice Censor-in-Chief,*
*Good and evil so clear, as if echo answers sound.*
*When matters touch the weight of moral teachings,*
*The stubborn and the timid alike rise at the call.*

Vice Censor-in-Chief Wang of Guangling (Guangling being Yangzhou) has lost his given name. Orphaned young and poor, he wielded the scribe’s brush to support his mother. At twenty, he became a government student, but though repeatedly recommended in the autumn examinations, he never passed. One New Year’s Eve, he dreamed two attendants in green summoned him away. Arriving at an official residence, grand and resplendent—its towering halls aglow—

Seated above was one whose crown and robes resembled a king’s. Beside him stood two crimson-robed clerks, holding a long register at the ready. Once the kingly figure had marked his judgment, the clerks called out for Wang to approach. Wang prostrated himself at the foot of the hall, stealing a glance at the king’s face—his expression was severe. He tossed down a ledger and ordered Wang to read it.
