There, beneath his own name, was recorded that he was destined to pass the examinations in a single sweep, enter the Hanlin Academy, rise to the rank of Governor-General, yet have it all erased by the baleful karma of his scribe’s brush. He had barely finished reading when the kingly figure struck the table and demanded, “Do you understand?” Wang kowtowed, begging for mercy. The kingly figure said, “In consideration of your filial devotion to your mother, I shall grant you a chance to reform. Change your ways at once, and your examination success may yet be restored. But if you persist in evil to the end, your life will be forfeit.” He then ordered the attendants in green to escort Wang out. Wang privately asked who the kingly figure was, and they replied, “It is the Wen Emperor. The register he signed is next autumn’s examination list—if you reform and do good, you may still appear on it. Do not betray the Emperor’s earnest and painstaking admonition.” With that, they pushed him away.

Startled awake, he found a single lamp still flickering dimly, while the neighbor’s rooster had yet to crow. The dream lingered vividly in his mind. Resolved to change his ways, he mused in silence: a poor man like himself had no means to perform good deeds—even if he abandoned his legal brush, how could he atone for past wrongs? Lost in thought through the night, he suddenly realized, “If the legal brush can kill, why can it not also save?” If he reversed its course, the gods might yet favor him. His mind made up, he rose, dressed, and sat waiting for dawn. At first light, he went to the Temple of Wenchang, burned incense, and prayed in silence.

From that day on, whenever litigants came to him, he would patiently mediate their disputes. If a party was in the wrong but sought litigation, he firmly rebuked them. Only when the cause was just and the party could not speak for themselves did he draft their pleas. For those without means, he went further, offering his own resources to aid them. After a full year of such conduct, the education commissioner awarded him first place in the prefectural examination. Encouraged, he redoubled his efforts to do good.

A neighboring household had a young widow who bore a posthumous child, and though their family was of modest means, they lived comfortably. Her husband’s clan, however, falsely accused her of impropriety and brought suit before the magistrate, claiming the child was not of true blood, fearing that an outsider’s lineage would disrupt the ancestral line, and petitioned the court to compel her to return permanently to her natal home. The widow’s own family, timid and weak-willed, dared not utter a word in her defense. Her grievance unvoiced, she wept day and night, vowing to die rather than endure the injustice.

An elderly neighbor, learning of her plight, told Wang. After investigating, Wang became convinced of the widow’s chastity. He visited her mother’s home and drafted a plea on her behalf, urging her mother to present it to the magistrate. The widow’s brothers hesitated, but Wang’s righteous indignation stirred them, and they were moved to act. Once the petition was submitted, Wang gathered his fellow scholars and the village elders, intending to address the matter publicly when the magistrate paid his monthly visit to the temple to expound the imperial edicts.

Some warned him, saying the affair did not concern him, but Wang replied, “Upholding chastity and protecting the orphaned are matters of moral principle—duties that all who study the classics should undertake. This is not like those who meddle in lawsuits for profit, which the statutes forbid. If there is any offense to be taken, I alone shall bear the responsibility.” The assembly praised his sense of justice and rallied eagerly to his cause. On the first day of the month, they gathered at the school. When the magistrate arrived and the edicts had been read, they presented their joint petition for review.

The magistrate, known for his discernment, examined the document and declared, “This concerns a matter of moral integrity. I shall personally investigate the truth of the matter. If the clan has indeed slandered her, they shall be punished according to the law. But you, gentlemen, must verify your findings thoroughly, lest you bring trouble upon yourselves.”

The scholar stepped forward and forcefully argued the case of false accusation, speaking with resolute conviction. The magistrate, seeing the righteousness of his words, commended and dismissed him. Within days, the hearing was convened. The clan members, their arguments exhausted, all confessed to the false accusation. The woman’s innocence was thus vindicated.

Once the case was closed, the woman instructed her maternal family to offer a hundred taels of silver to Wang in gratitude, but Wang refused. When they insisted, Wang grew angry and said, “Do you think I acted for the sake of a reward?” He rejected the gift sternly, his voice and expression severe, and the family departed in shame.

On New Year’s Eve that year, Wang dreamed again of the two green-robed attendants, who summoned him as before. They arrived at the same place, where the Celestial Judge, his countenance now serene, addressed him: “I commend your swift reform. Your scholarly honors have been restored, and you shall pass the next examination. For your act of preserving chastity and protecting the orphan—a virtuous deed that moved Heaven—you shall succeed this very year. Strive ever harder in your virtue, and your future will be boundless.” Wang bowed deeply in gratitude.

The Judge then ordered an attendant to escort him out. Along the way, he encountered two elders and a young man kneeling beside the path. Unsure of their identity, Wang returned their bow. The elders and the youth pressed their foreheads to the ground and said, “By your grace, you preserved our lineage and safeguarded our family estate. We, father and son, grandfather and grandson, are ashamed to have no means to repay your great kindness. We heard the Celestial Judge had summoned you, so we awaited you here.” Wang realized they were the woman’s uncle and husband, and he helped them rise.

The elder then pointed to the young man and said to Wang, “This unworthy son has received your boundless favor. Knowing you have no heir, we shall beseech the underworld authorities to let him become your son in repayment.” Wang demurred and took his leave. Eagerly searching for the green-robed attendants, he found them gone. Wandering at the crossroads, he felt lost and bewildered when suddenly a black-robed man addressed him: “You have not yet returned? Fortunately, you met me—otherwise, you would have been in peril.”

The black-robed man then took Wang by the hand and led him forward with great speed, so swift that in an instant they saw his family gate. Wang asked the man’s name and surname, to which he smiled and replied, “We have lived under the same roof for years—how could you not know me?” Pressed further, he revealed himself as the Kitchen God. Together they entered Wang’s chamber, where his mother and wife sat facing each other, their faces wreathed in joy. A man lay on the bed to one side, and as Wang stared in astonishment, the black-robed figure gave him a push from behind. In that moment, he felt his own body merge with the one on the bed, and he awoke with a loud cry. Indeed, his mother and wife were both in the room.

Wang then recounted all that had befallen him in his dream, rose at once, and burned incense to bow in gratitude to the Kitchen God. From that time on, he redoubled his efforts to do good. The following year, he passed the provincial examination and went on to achieve successive successes, entering the Hanlin Academy and rising to the rank of Grand Guardian. In the same year he passed the examination, he fathered a son. As the child was born, Wang seemed to see the young man from his dream enter the room, and this son, too, would later attain high office through the imperial examinations.

The Master of the Flowery Seat observed: “Though Wang’s use of legal petitions to save another was his own innovation, why should others not do the same to aid their fellow men? If they did, then of all the ways to benefit and assist the world, what could surpass the power of the written word? Truly, Wang’s abundant blessings and good fortune were well deserved!”

21. Magistrate Li Xiaolin

Why seek to flatter and display one’s talent?
For leniency and severity must ever be balanced in governance.
Behold the stern magistrate of Qingyang—
Even when he cried, “I was wrong!” it was already too late.

Magistrate Li Xiaolin, a *mingfu* (a term for county magistrates), had passed the provincial examination as a *jieyuan* (top scholar) before assuming office in Wanjing (present-day Anhui). His literary grace and charismatic bearing won the admiration of his peers. Yet his nature inclined toward severity. While awaiting his post in the provincial capital, he spent his days adjudicating cases at the governor’s yamen. At the time, both the governor-general and the provincial governor favored strict governance, and Xiaolin deliberately curried their favor, ensuring his rulings were harsh and unyielding. His eloquence was sharp, his judgments swift as flowing water. Once he had settled a case, even veteran clerks could find no grounds for objection. Thus, he won the full approval of the governor and governor-general, and in all matters, he leaned toward severity.

Those executed under his judgments were not necessarily all deserving of their sentences. Later, as he took up successive posts in troubled counties—where governance was most difficult—he invariably ruled with severity. Soon after, he was transferred from Xuancheng to assume the magistracy of Qingyang. One day, while traveling to the countryside to conduct an inquest, his sedan chair had reached the midway point when the bearers suddenly heard the magistrate’s stern rebuke. At first, they assumed he was angry with them, but then came a murmuring, as if he were arguing with someone. The words were mostly unintelligible, save for frantic cries: *“I was wrong! I was wrong! I should not have falsely implicated you in crimes that sent you to your deaths side by side!”* With that, all fell silent. When they arrived at the lodging for the night and halted the sedan, the magistrate did not emerge. They lifted the curtain to look—only to find him stiff and dead, his hands clutching his own throat.

The Master of the Sitting-among-Flowers remarked: *“Governance that favors severity already loses the spirit of compassion. How much worse when it stems from the private motive of currying favor through calculation and flattery? What recourse then remains for the people? The Way of Heaven is numinous and just; one cannot kill alone without consequence. Would that those who rule over the people might never wait until they encounter ghosts in their sedan chairs to cry, ‘I was wrong! I was wrong!’—then all would be well.”*

“22. *The Counterfeit Letter Preserving Chastity*
Hearing weeping stirs a kind thought—
Light of wealth, staunch in righteousness, the tale lives on.
Surely this must be a descendant of Lord Wen—
Would that I could buy yellow gold to cast his likeness!”

A young man of Jiaxing named Zhu had ancestors from Huizhou. As a child, he was bright and fond of reading, but after losing his parents in adolescence, he abandoned his studies. A fellow villager, having established a large shop in Jiaxing, took Zhu along to apprentice in trade. All apprentices regarded the shopkeeper as their master. Zhu was diligent, quick-witted, and sincere, and his master cherished him deeply. Once his training was complete, through frugality and hard work, he amassed a hundred taels of silver. He had long been betrothed and now planned to return home to marry. His master approved but, considering his meager savings, added another hundred taels, set a departure date, and saw him off.

Zhu returned within ten days, astonishing his master with his swift return. He explained that bandits had robbed him mid-journey, leaving him penniless. Realizing there was no point in returning empty-handed, he resolved to wait a year or two, save a little more, and then make another attempt. His master, hearing this, sighed deeply for some time, unaware that Zhu had not been robbed at all.

On his southward journey, Zhu disembarked at a certain place and traveled overland. That night, he lodged at an inn and heard bitter weeping from the next room. Listening closely, he discerned two women sobbing without cease until dawn. At daybreak, he asked the innkeeper about it.

The innkeeper replied, "It is a sorrowful tale. The ones weeping last night were a mother-in-law and daughter-in-law. The mother-in-law was widowed young and raised her only son to adulthood, for whom she arranged a marriage. The daughter-in-law was both beautiful and filial. A year after the wedding, driven by famine, the son left to seek work as a private secretary in Sichuan, sending home meager earnings to support his mother. When this proved insufficient, the daughter-in-law supplemented their income with needlework. Recently, due to military conflicts in Sichuan and Hubei, the roads have been blocked, and for three years now, no news or remittances have arrived. This year, famine struck, firewood as precious as cassia and rice as costly as pearls made survival nearly impossible. The mother-in-law, with no other choice, resolved to sell her daughter-in-law. Yesterday, a matchmaker came, saying a wealthy man’s son, enamored with the daughter-in-law’s beauty, offered three hundred taels to take her as his concubine—the agreement was already settled. The daughter-in-law, unwilling to leave her mother-in-law, and the mother-in-law, reluctant to part with her virtuous and filial daughter-in-law, wept bitterly all night."

Moved by their plight, Zhu asked in detail about their family name, clan, and the son’s name, age, and appearance, which the innkeeper recounted fully. Zhu then said, "I have some business to attend to and must delay my departure by half a day." The innkeeper agreed and left.

Returning to his room, Zhu took out all the silver from his purse and forged a letter from the son. He then went to their home. An old woman answered the door, her eyes brimming with tears. Startled, she asked, "Where have you come from, sir?" Zhu replied, "Is this the residence of such-and-such a family?" "Yes," she said. "Is there someone working as a private secretary in Sichuan?" "There is," she answered. When he asked for the name, the old woman described it exactly as the innkeeper had. Zhu requested to see the mistress of the house. The old woman said, "I am she." She then led him inside, asking where he had come from.

Zhu presented the two hundred taels of silver and the forged letter, saying, "I traded goods in Sichuan and became fast friends with your son. Now, returning south with my cargo, your son entrusted me to deliver this silver and letter—please accept them." Overjoyed, the old woman urged him to stay and pressed him for details of her son’s whereabouts. Zhu replied, "Your son is thriving in his travels, having amassed great wealth. He will return home any day now; the letter surely explains everything in full. I must hurry on my way and cannot stay to speak further." With that, he took his leave.

Taking a roundabout path back to the inn, he immediately sent a letter home, claiming to have been robbed and requesting a delay in the wedding. He then returned to the inn himself. His master, trusting in his sincerity and prudence, grew increasingly reliant on him. After another year, he saved enough to return home, once more lodging at the same inn. He asked the innkeeper about the mother and daughter-in-law of that family and what had become of them. The innkeeper exclaimed, "What a strange turn of events!"

"After I told the traveler what had happened, that very day a stranger from afar arrived at their home, bearing a letter from her son along with two hundred taels of silver. He entrusted these to them and left. Upon receiving the silver, the mother-in-law immediately informed the matchmaker to call off the marriage with the wealthy family. Before even a few days had passed, her son suddenly returned, bringing with him over a hundred thousand taels in wealth. Now they have become a prosperous household. Yet in Sichuan, there was no one entrusted to deliver the silver or letter—no one knows where the letter came from. They suspect that the gods, moved by the mother-in-law’s steadfast virtue and the daughter-in-law’s filial piety, secretly aided them."

Zhu nodded in agreement. The next day, he set off on his journey. A year after his marriage, he returned to the shop and once again lodged at the same inn. Rain prevented him from traveling, so he strolled outside the gate. By chance, he encountered the neighbor’s mother-in-law being carried past in a sedan chair. He quickly stepped aside to avoid her. Soon after, a young scholar in formal attire approached and invited him to his home. Zhu declined, saying, "We have never met—why this sudden honor?" The scholar smiled and replied, "It is nothing. I only heard from the innkeeper that you are skilled in calligraphy."

"Just now, I was about to send a letter to a distant place, but I suffer from an inability to wield the brush myself, and my secretary has gone elsewhere. Thus, I venture to beg your assistance." Zhu firmly declined, only relenting after much insistence. The young scholar then entered and retrieved the letter draft, saying, "Please transcribe this with a single flourish." Zhu wrote it out and handed it over. Shortly after, two servants emerged, placing a chair in the center of the hall and spreading a red *qúshū* upon the floor—an arrangement as if a junior were about to pay respects to an elder. Zhu paced about the hall, perplexed by the proceedings. Suddenly, the neighbor’s mother-in-law and her daughter-in-law appeared from within, both dressed in solemn and dignified attire.

The young scholar, flustered, attempted to flee, but the two servants restrained him, preventing his escape. The neighbor’s son then ordered several maids to assist the scholar into the chair, holding his hands on either side so he could not move. Thereupon, the mother knelt before him, her daughter-in-law following behind, and kowtowed in gratitude, saying, “Had it not been for your great kindness, my son and I—husband and wife—would not be here today.” The scholar firmly denied any involvement, protesting, “I am but a chance acquaintance, stranger to your affairs. Why do you play this cruel jest, placing me in such an impossible position?”

Once the crowd had finished their prostrations, the mother spoke: “You personally delivered the silver and letter to me. For three years, I have never forgotten your virtue, not even in my dreams. Though a true gentleman bestows kindness without expecting repayment, how could my son and I live with ourselves otherwise? Now, by heaven’s grace, we have met again, sparing us a lifetime of regret.” She then instructed her son to produce the letters from before and after as proof, and summoned the innkeeper to confirm. The innkeeper pondered for a long while before saying, “There is no doubt.” He recounted how, after hearing the weeping, the scholar had persistently inquired about their origins and had turned back mid-journey to assist.

Only then did the scholar reluctantly admit, “It was merely a moment of compassion—heaven took pity on your mother’s steadfast virtue and your wife’s filial piety, using me as its instrument. How could I dare claim credit for heaven’s work?” The neighbor’s son then urged him to stay, saying, “Though I can never fully repay your kindness, I beg you to remain for a few days, that I may show you the slightest measure of respect.” Seeing his sincerity, the scholar agreed to stay briefly, lodging in his study.

The neighbor’s son, learning he was a year older, asked to honor him as an elder brother, to which the scholar happily consented. Their bond grew so deep that even blood brothers could not surpass it. When he learned the scholar’s parents remained unburied, the neighbor said, “I own a pawnshop not far from your home. This year, I acquired a plot of land three or four *li* away, where the geomancer declared that after burial, scholarly success would never cease. My own family lives too far away and lacks the fortune to benefit from it, but you, brother, are modest and reverent, with deep-rooted blessings. I humbly offer it to you.” Regarding the burial expenses for his parents, he insisted, “Allow me to bear the cost entirely, that I may repay a fraction of your great kindness.”

After repeated refusals, the scholar finally accepted. They chose an auspicious day to return together, selecting the site and arranging the burial, with all signs favorable for the year and direction. The neighbor spared no expense, managing every detail, so the scholar spent not a single coin. After the burial, the neighbor urged him to oversee the pawnshop, adding, “Fortunately, the business provides enough to sustain us.”

In the morning it is called *yong*, and in the evening, *sun*. The neighbor urged, “With this modest livelihood to provide for our daily meals, why should you return to Jiaxing and wander far from your homeland?” The scholar refused, saying, “The shopkeeper has treated me with great kindness. To abandon him midway would be to betray his favor—an inauspicious act.” Thus, he bid farewell to the neighbor and departed.

Upon returning to the shop, he encountered a fellow townsman who had arrived earlier. This man recounted the scholar’s deeds in full to the shopkeeper. Astonished and delighted, the shopkeeper exclaimed, “I initially took Master Zhu for an honest and sincere man, but he not only acts with righteous courage—he also remains so unassuming, neither boasting nor seeking praise. Such a man is destined for great fortune.” From then on, the shopkeeper placed even greater trust in him.

When the shopkeeper later fell gravely ill, he had only one young son. He entrusted the entire shop to the scholar, stipulating a ten-year term, after which the principal would be returned to his son. After taking over, the scholar’s business flourished tenfold. When the term ended, he tallied the principal and interest, amassing tens of thousands of taels of silver. Mindful of the shopkeeper’s kindness and unwilling to betray it, he divided the entire sum equally, giving half to the shopkeeper’s son. Both families thus became immensely wealthy.

Later, the scholar fathered two sons. The elder, Jiaji, placed second in the *jiaxu* metropolitan examination; the younger, Kuiji, also ranked second in the *dingchou* examination. Both entered the Hanlin Academy, successively holding prestigious offices. Their wealth and honor continue unabated to this day.

**Two Placards of Hidden Merit:**
*A righteous wife and virtuous mother preserve purity like ice;*
*Mother and child reunite through great kindness.*
*Such hidden deeds earn heaven’s abundant reward—*
*Two examination triumphs, provincial and metropolitan, are bestowed upon you.*

Xu, a *shangshe* from Shanyin, managed a pawnshop in Suzhou, returning home every three years to visit his mother. One year, at forty and still childless, he considered taking a concubine. Coincidentally, a scholar in his village had died young, leaving behind a wife and two young children—a son and a daughter—with modest means. The scholar’s dissolute younger brother, Yi, had gambled away the family’s wealth. Conspiring with his wife, he plotted to drive out his sister-in-law and seize his brother’s property. Seizing an opportunity, he first lured away and sold the children. The widow, aware of Yi’s schemes, wept day and night, longing for death.

Just then, Yi’s wife overheard a neighbor’s matron mention the *shangshe*’s desire to take a concubine. She entrusted the matter to the matron, who then brokered the arrangement with the *shangshe*. Both he and his wife were pleased.

Just as the widow had gone out on the Qingming Festival to sweep her late husband’s grave, Yi instructed the matchmaker to arrange for the *shangshe* to meet her. Pleased with what he saw, the *shangshe* immediately offered a betrothal gift of a hundred taels of silver and selected an auspicious day for the wedding—all unbeknownst to the widow. When the appointed day arrived, Yi and his wife had the matchmaker falsely claim to have found the widow’s lost children, insisting she come in person to retrieve them, thus luring her out. A sedan chair carried her directly to the *shangshe*’s home, where upon entering the hall, she was greeted with the blare of suona horns.

The *shangshe*’s wife, née Zhang, personally lifted the curtain to welcome her. Seeing the widow still dressed in mourning white, she scolded the matchmaker, “Why have you not provided the new concubine with festive attire?” The widow, about to greet her hosts with propriety, was stunned by these words. Turning to the matchmaker, she demanded, “Where is my child? And who is this?” The matchmaker whispered urgently, “Have you truly not heard? Your young master has betrothed you to this household as a concubine. The master here is surnamed Xu, and this is his principal wife. Hurry and change into your wedding clothes to bow before heaven and earth. The master and mistress are both kind and virtuous—far better than enduring your young master’s daily humiliation at home. It is as different as heaven and earth!”

The widow stood frozen, dumbstruck, unable to utter a word. Then, tears welled in her eyes, streaming down her lashes as she choked on sobs. Suddenly, she lunged toward the steps, intent on hurling herself down. Madame Zhang quickly ordered the maids to restrain her and rebuked the matchmaker, “What manner of affair is this? How could you force such a thing without her knowledge?” At that moment, the *shangshe*, who had briefly stepped inside, heard the commotion and rushed out. Recognizing the widow’s chastity, he immediately told his wife, “Remove the incense and candles at once. Escort her to the inner chambers and devise a better plan. I would rather remain childless than force a widow into concubinage—such cruelty is unbearable.”

Slightly reassured, the widow followed Madame Zhang into her private quarters, where she was asked about her family and whether she had children. The widow recounted her ordeal in full, and Madame Zhang sighed deeply in sympathy. “Now that matters have come to this, what is to be done?” The widow, weeping, replied, “This is all the work of that beastly uncle, who has betrayed all goodness. My fate—honor or disgrace, life or death—now rests in your hands. If I may preserve my name and meet my late husband in the underworld, I will serve as your maidservant to repay this kindness.”

Moved by her plight, Madame Zhang informed the *shangshe*, who was equally compassionate. Upon learning the name of the widow’s late husband, he exclaimed in shock, “He was of official lineage!” He proposed sending her home, but Madame Zhang objected, “If you return her to that beast, you will only cast her back into the jaws of death.” The *shangshe* pondered in silence, at a loss, until he consulted his mother. She declared, “If my son can perform such a virtuous deed, how could he lack an heir?”

The new woman, unable to return home, might as well be taken as my adopted daughter and share my chamber. This would preserve her chastity, avoid suspicion, and—since you are often away—provide companionship for your wife and me in our solitude. One act, three benefits: what say you, my son?” The *shangshe* replied, “Mother’s words are most wise.” He urged Madame Zhang to inform the widow, who joyfully assented.

That very day, the bridal chamber’s floral candles and feast were repurposed into a *tangbing* banquet, whereupon she bowed to her new mother and formally met the *shangshe* and his wife as brother and sister-in-law. The *shangshe* likewise treated her as a younger sister. Gentle and obedient, she won her mother’s favor and even assisted her sister-in-law with household tasks. Yet she often thought of her children, so the mother instructed the *shangshe* to keep watch for them.

One day, the *shangshe* happened to pass a roadside earth-god shrine outside the city. An elderly monk, an old acquaintance, invited him to sit. A young novice of seven or eight brought tea. The *shangshe* glanced at him—his features refined and elegant, strikingly like his sister’s—and grew suspicious. He asked the monk, “Where did you find such a fine disciple?”

The monk sighed deeply. “It is a long tale. I knew both his grandfather and father. After the grandfather passed, the father soon followed. His uncle, lost to gambling, lured him away, intending to sell him into the *you* profession. Seeing this, I took pity and bought him for ten taels of silver. I heard his mother was still alive and planned to return him to her, but feared his uncle would only sell him again. So I delayed, waiting for another opportunity.”

The *shangshe* exclaimed in astonishment, “Then he is surely my nephew!”

After losing her son, not half a month passed before she lost her daughter as well. Grief-stricken, she fell ill, seeking only death each day. The elder said, “If you can show compassion and return my son, I will repay you double the price.” The monk replied, “This is my original vow. This child is exceptionally intelligent and perceptive; he is certain to become a person of great ability. I hope you will guide him well. But be sure his uncle never learns of this—do not let my small act of kindness come to nothing.” Thereupon, he sent the boy to return home with the *shangshe*.

The *shangshe* brought his nephew home and went straight to his mother’s chamber, calling his sister to him: “Come and see this young novice—whose son do you think he is?” His sister glanced at him and wept, saying, “Is this not my son? Where did you find him?” The nephew, gazing intently at his mother, rushed into her arms and sobbed; his mother wept as well. The *shangshe*’s mother was in the Zhang family’s chamber, and upon hearing the commotion, both mother-in-law and daughter-in-law came. The sister led her son to bow to them all, saying, “Were it not for my grandmother and my uncle and aunt, my son and I would never have hoped to meet again. My child, never forget this great kindness.” The *shangshe* smiled and said, “This is the virtue of the old monk at the village shrine. Otherwise, my nephew would have become a performer.”

The *shangshe* then recounted the monk’s words in full, and all present gnashed their teeth in hatred for his uncle. The next day, the *shangshe* took twenty taels of silver to thank the old monk, but the monk had vanished without a trace. The incense worker handed a sealed letter to the *shangshe*, saying, “The master, just before his departure, instructed me to leave this for the benefactor.” Opening it, they found the boy’s bill of sale. On the back of the contract were six lines written in large characters:

*"Zhen* is male, *Dui* is female,
In one qi they are born.
There is a tender child,
In that grove of the center.
By mountain’s edge and water’s brink,
Pines and cypresses stand dense and deep."

They read it and marveled.

The incense worker said, “The master instructed me to tell the benefactor: proceed two *li* southeast from here, and you may find news of the girl. Take these twenty taels of silver from my sleeve as ransom for her—the temple has no need of them.” The *shangshe* followed his words and set out. After roughly two *li*, he saw a small earthen hill blocking the path. Following the hill south, he came upon a vast, rushing stream, with rows of pines and cypresses lining its banks. Amidst them stood several tiled houses, their doors half ajar. A girl of six or seven years sat inside. The *shangshe* studied her closely—her features bore a striking resemblance to his sworn sister, yet he had no way to confirm the truth.

Suddenly, an old man leaning on a staff emerged. The *shangshe* bowed and inquired, “May I ask your honorable surname?” The man replied, “Lin. And what brings you here, traveler?” The *shangshe* said, “I have just witnessed something most strange and venture to seek your counsel.” The old man asked, “What matter?” The *shangshe* pointed to the young girl and said, “This child is my niece. How did she come to be here?” The old man stared in astonishment for a long moment before saying, “If she is your niece, why was she sold as a servant?” The *shangshe* explained how she had been abducted and sold, then offered to pay double the price to ransom her. The old man refused, saying, “This girl does not even know you as her uncle—how can you falsely claim her?” At a loss for words, the *shangshe* considered returning to consult his sister, but feared matters might change in the interim. As he racked his mind for a solution, a young man emerged from within—an old friend from Suzhou.

Upon seeing the *shangshe*, the youth exclaimed, “When did you return? And where have you come from just now?” The *shangshe* recounted the events. The young man pointed to the old man and said, “This is my father.” Then, turning to his father, he added, “This is the *shangshe* Xu, the kind man I told you about who aided Le’s family in their distress.” The old man was both startled and delighted. Laying down his staff, he bowed in greeting and invited the *shangshe* inside, where they spoke with great warmth and familiarity. The *shangshe* once more broached the subject of ransoming the girl, and both father and son agreed. The young man promptly went inside, returned with the girl, and handed the *shangshe* her bill of sale. The contract bore the name of his uncle, identical to the monk’s. The *shangshe* took out the silver from his sleeve and said, “Half is for ransoming my niece, and half is a gift to honor you, elder.” Both father and son firmly declined.

The *shangshe* would not hear of it, saying, “You have already shown great kindness by agreeing to the ransom. If I do not pay the price, how can my heart be at ease?” He placed the silver on the table, then swiftly took the girl home to return her to his sister, who was overjoyed beyond expectation. The *shangshe* further arranged for a tutor to instruct his niece, and she proved remarkably intelligent, reading dozens of lines each day.

At that time, the *shangshe*’s mother unexpectedly gained a companion for her daily life, and seeing how both her son and the girl were bright and elegant, able to delight the old woman, while Zhangshi was virtuous, gentle, and compliant, never displaying the slightest air of self-righteousness in her kindness, the *shangshe* felt deeply consoled. He then chose an auspicious day to travel once more to Suzhou.

Crossing the river to Hangzhou, he took the route through Jiaxing. When his boat docked at Xishui Post, he suddenly dreamed of two scholars, one old and one young, who came to his boat, bowed in gratitude, and said, “Thanks to you, my wife’s chastity was preserved, and my children’s futures secured. My father and I have petitioned the Heavenly Court, and you shall be granted high honors and noble sons. You must hasten back to Hangzhou to sit for the provincial examination. The celestial roster is already set—you are destined to place first. Do not proceed to Suzhou.”

The *shangshe* laughed and replied, “To pass the examination, one must compose *shiwen*. I have never in my life known what *shiwen* is—how could I possibly succeed?”

The old man said, “It is no difficulty. Tomorrow at noon, moor your boat here. A seller of old books will come—buy them all. Among them, you will find two volumes of examination drafts, both my own works from years past. They contain all the poetry and essay topics for this year’s examination. You need not fear submitting a blank paper.”

He awoke, astonished by the dream.

As dawn approached and he prepared to cast off the mooring line, a fierce wind suddenly arose, preventing the boat from advancing. Restless and idle, he gazed absently from the bow. At dusk, just as in his dream, a man arrived carrying over ten volumes of old books for sale. Recalling the previous night’s vision, he purchased them for a hundred coins and, indeed, found two hand-copied volumes among them. Upon inspection, they appeared to be the very *shiwen* he had been told of. The final volume contained only five-character regulated verse. He mused, “The dream has come true—I should copy these at once and return to Hangzhou instead of proceeding onward.” Then, suddenly chuckling to himself, he thought, “How could an unlearned man possibly pass the examination? I’d only invite ridicule. Besides, the second and third sessions of the provincial examination require classical texts, policy essays, and discourses—these two volumes may not contain them all. How could I complete the entire examination?” Resolved, he decided to wait for the wind to subside before continuing his journey.

No sooner had he settled his mind than weariness overtook him. Sitting down, he dozed off, and once again, the two scholars appeared in his dream. “Why so doubtful?” they asked. “If you fear you cannot compose the classical texts or policy essays, there are two bundles of books at the old shop west of the post station. The first volume on the eastern side contains the classical texts, while the third and fourth on the western side are the policy essays. All this year’s topics are included, even the supplementary essays—hurry and purchase them before it’s too late!” The younger one patted his shoulder and said, “Do not bring ruin upon yourself. Next year’s metropolitan examination topics are all here as well!”

He awoke, even more astonished. Following their instructions, he went to the shop west of the post station and, as foretold, found the volumes of classical texts and policy essays. He bought them and returned, though his heart remained filled with doubt. It was early in the seventh month, the heat still oppressive, and sleep eluded him. As he perused the purchased books, he came upon a volume titled *Records of Karmic Retribution in the Examination Halls*, which mentioned cases of candidates passing despite submitting blank papers. His heart stirred, and he thought, “If I follow the dream’s guidance, might I not be like them?” Yet he dared not fully believe it.

Exhausted at last, he fell asleep, and the two scholars reappeared, admonishing him, “If you still do not trust us, we shall summon your father.” Before they finished speaking, his father indeed arrived. The *shangshe* bowed in greeting, but his father glared at him in anger and said, “The underworld holds examination success in the highest regard. I regret not having taught you to read while I was alive. Now, thanks to the efforts of these two gentlemen, the Emperor has bestowed upon you this opportunity. Yet you would squander a ready-made *juren* and *jinshi* degree—what else do you intend to do, unfilial son?” Trembling, the *shangshe* replied, “I shall return to Hangzhou at once and think no more of it.” His father nodded. “That is the proper course.” Then, pointing to the old man, he said, “This is your sister’s father-in-law, and the younger one is her husband.” The *shangshe* finally understood. Before he could speak further, his father added, “Apply yourself well, so that your father may receive an imperial honor and bring glory to the grave.” With that, he pushed him awake.

At dawn, he instructed the boatman to change course and return to Hangzhou, where he sought lodging by the lake. He entrusted a *mendou* to draft his examination essays. Within days, the posthumous examination papers arrived, and he brought the assigned texts inside—all the topics were among them. He transcribed them neatly and submitted them, ultimately achieving high marks. After completing the three rounds of examinations, he remained at his lodging to await the announcement of results. Indeed, he passed, attended the celebratory feast, and returned home.

Upon arriving, his wife was arguing with the messenger, saying, “My husband is an official in Suzhou—how could he take the provincial examination in Hangzhou? What swindler are you, trying to extort money?” The messenger cried out in protest, “I saw your honored husband at the *Zhigong Tang*, wearing flowers and receiving the feast after passing. Now you deny it to avoid paying the reward?” Amid the clamor, the *shangshe* suddenly entered. The messenger, seeing him, exclaimed, “Is this not the newly honored gentleman?” Zhang-shi, seeing him, asked, “Why have you returned so suddenly?” He smiled and said, “First, pay the messenger, then I will explain.” He took out a generous sum of silver to reward the man.

He entered, bowed to his mother, and the family was reunited. He recounted in detail how he had taken the examination and passed. Then, turning to his sister, he described the appearances of the two scholars, young and old. His sister wept and said, “They were indeed my father-in-law and husband.” Zhang-shi then asked, “Do you know how swiftly Heaven’s justice has acted?” When he inquired further, his sister said, “After Uncle Shou spied on your departure, he used the ancestral rites as a pretext to demand our children, causing repeated disturbances. One day, he and his wife came together, but before they could enter the house, thunder and lightning struck. They were dragged to the market square, forced to kneel facing each other, and died, their bodies charred black as coal.” Xu sighed in astonishment and asked, “Then what became of the house?” They all replied, “We do not know.”

The next day, he went to pay his respects to the county magistrate, recounting in full his sister’s chastity and heroism, as well as how her uncle-in-law had attempted to sell her off, and requested that her family property be restored to her. The magistrate granted his request. At the time, several dozen *mu* of land and a dwelling house remained, all seized by clansmen, but these were all reclaimed and returned to her. The following year, Xu again journeyed to the capital to sit for the metropolitan examination, and this time passed with honors, appointed as a county magistrate. Meanwhile, Zhang Lianju’s husband had five sons—all of whom achieved success in the imperial examinations. Later, her nephew also came of age and passed the examinations in succession, becoming a *jinshi*. The two families continued to intermarry without cease, and to this day remain prominent clans in Shanyin.

The Master of Sitting-Among-Flowers remarked: “That one ignorant of literary arts should aspire to compete in the thorny examination halls and inscribe their name upon the flower-strewn roster is, in truth, something that reason declares impossible. Yet it has come to pass—could this not be because those who perform good deeds beyond all expectation are the very ones who receive rewards beyond all hope? As for the virtuous wife who harbored no jealousy and ultimately bore a unicorn-like son, and the chaste widow who preserved her integrity and was honored with the phoenix decree—while the husband and wife of beastly hearts and bestial conduct ultimately met the same fate, struck dead by lightning and perishing together in the thoroughfare, thus manifesting the reward of good and the punishment of evil—such is only as it should be. Yet the old monk who preserved the heir and refused the gold stands far above them all!”

**24. Tao Shun**

A servant’s duty is to serve his master’s will—
how could he stain his name with lust and theft?
Yet unrepentant, he falsely blamed a beggar still;
how could the courts of darkness suffer such deceit?

Tao Shun was a servant to my great-uncle, the late Minister Yao Wenxi. When Minister Wenxi held office in the capital, my great-grandfather remained at home and entrusted Tao Shun with guarding the gate. Shun carried on an affair with a maidservant and would steal his master’s belongings to sell for money to give her. Once, a single quilt went missing from the study where my four maternal uncles resided—it was Shun who had taken it. The study stood near the side gate, which he often passed through. Just then, a beggar arrived outside the gate seeking alms, and Shun falsely accused him of the theft. When the beggar protested, Shun hastily rallied the other servants to surround and beat him, releasing him only after his injuries grew severe. The beggar died within days.

Before long, Minister Wenxi was appointed educational commissioner of eastern Guangdong and summoned my great-grandfather to join him; Shun accompanied them. After several months there, he fell ill with measles and suddenly went mad. In the depths of winter, he danced naked in the courtyard, and only the strongest could restrain him and place him back in bed.

No sooner had they released him than he leapt up, knelt, and declared, “The city gods of An and Cheng counties have summoned me for trial. I should not have stolen my master’s belongings, nor falsely accused the beggar, nor beaten him to death.” With that, he ordered himself punished, slapping his own cheeks dozens of times. Then he commanded a beating for his legs, threw himself onto the bed, and struck them without count. Both buttocks turned black and blue, as if he had been caned. This continued for days until he shrieked and died.

As death approached, he asked his attendants for silver. They handed him the pouch from his pillow, but he waved it away, saying it was not what he wanted. When they produced paper spirit money from the market, his face lit up. They burned it before him, and Shun waved his hands toward the air, saying, “Take it, all of you—do not complain it is too meager.” This serves as proof that the custom of burning spirit money is not entirely baseless.

**25. The Thief Officer Struck by Lightning**
*Greed stirred in midstream, a thief’s heart arose—*
*Grief came when the great tiller crushed the boat below.*
*By dawn, his accomplices all met heavenly doom—*
*Never say there are no gods, for gods there are.*

When the Guangdong rebels crossed the river to occupy Guazhou and Zhenjiang, the commanding general assembled a naval force at Jiaoshan to block their escape from the river into the sea. Among the troops was one Li, a former helmsman of a small boat who had risen through the ranks to the rank of *bazong* through long service. Arrogant and unrestrained, he was often involved in nighttime raids on the southern embankment.

In the winter of *bingchen*, an elderly couple from Yangzhou was traveling south with their family aboard a fishing boat, sailing swiftly downstream. As they passed Li’s vessel, he hooked their boat and, under the pretext of an inspection, led his men aboard to search. They overturned baskets and emptied chests, seizing over two hundred taels of silver and a casket full of gold and silver jewelry. The couple protested and repeatedly demanded its return. Li threatened them, and the old man, frightened, first begged to keep the jewelry in exchange for the silver, then offered to surrender everything if only they would spare their travel funds—both requests were refused. Enraged, the old man cried, “Has the world truly turned upside down? Is there no justice left in heaven?” He turned the boat, untied the mooring rope, and set off.

Fearing the couple would report him, Li feigned kindness to deceive them, saying, “I was merely jesting with you! But carrying so much at dusk is inconvenient—let us wait until tomorrow morning, and I shall return everything intact.” He then moored the old man’s boat beneath the stern deck. That night, he lowered the massive rudder to crush it, sinking the vessel. The old man, his entire family, and his two sons drowned—eleven in all. Li was immensely pleased with himself.

At the time, there were over twenty men aboard Li’s boat; only five took no part in the scheme, while the rest shared in the spoils. One of the sailors, a certain water-brave named Jia, had long practiced the teachings of the Three Officials Dao. When not in battle, he would kneel night and day in the bow hold, reciting the *Scripture of the Three Officials* without cease. Upon hearing of this affair, he was deeply grieved.

Jia sighed, saying, “This will surely bring manifest retribution—yet what can we do, fellow passengers that we are?” Li’s cohorts all mocked him with scorn.

The next day, Li visited a friend on another boat. Suddenly stricken with a splitting headache, he hastily summoned a ferry to take him back. Midway across the river, the oars abruptly stilled. When Li demanded an explanation, the boatman pointed toward Li’s vessel and said, “Do you not see the Thunder God standing in fury at its bow?” Enraged, Li cursed this as demonic talk and raised the flat of his blade to strike the man. The boatman had no choice but to row on until they reached Li’s ship. The moment Li leapt aboard, a single thunderclap resounded—and he fell dead.

Those on the boat with him saw lightning strike the bow and all rushed to take shelter at the stern. The thunder crashed again, splitting the vessel in two. The rear half, with all aboard, sank at once beneath the waves. The front half remained afloat. At the time, Jia was still reciting scripture in the bow hold; hearing the repeated thunderclaps, he emerged to find Li dead, the ship halved, and not a single soul left among his fellow passengers. Terrified, he clutched his belongings and cried out for help. Someone sent a small boat to fetch him, but before it had traveled a full rod’s length, the thunder roared once more—and the remaining half of the ship instantly sank.

The five who had taken no part in the plot were all lifted by the thunder to the opposite riverbank, where they gradually regained consciousness. Questioned later, they said, “When the thunder struck Li, we too fled to hide. Then, as if in a dream or a daze, we saw a golden-armored deity seize us and carry us away—we had no idea how we came to be here.”

26. The Grave-Digging Soldier

The soldier ought to keep his person bound by law—
how could he bear to rob graves and desecrate tombs?
Breaking coffins, exposing bones, unseen by men—
yet lightning strikes him on the road, his guilt made plain.

In the summer of the bingchen year, beyond the north gate of Danyang, five soldiers were traveling beneath the scorching sun. Suddenly, a thunderclap erupted from the clear sky, killing three of them while the other two remained unharmed. When questioned about the three men’s usual conduct, the two replied with trepidation: “Only today have we learned the terror of thunder. These three, wherever the army was stationed, made grave-robbing their trade, never considering that their deeds might provoke Heaven’s wrath.”

The Master of Sitting Among Flowers laments: "Alas! Since the Red Turbans’ rebellion, crimson headbands have blanketed the land, and blades have scraped the heavens. They seize goods as easily as reaching into a sack—*rang* (pronounced *rǎng*, level tone, meaning ‘to plunder’; *tàn náng*, to thrust one’s hand into a bag to take something, as in Li Gu’s words from *The History of the Five Dynasties*: ‘If the Central Plains employ me as minister, reclaiming the Central Plains would be as effortless as taking something from a bag’)—and treat human lives as no more than grass to be mown—*yì* (pronunciation and meaning: ‘to cut’). How few are those like this lowly officer!

Yet when such men rely on the might of ‘Subduing Waves’ and ‘Crossing Seas’—*fúbō hénghǎi* (referring to generals, as in *The Book of Later Han*: ‘Ma Yuan was appointed General of Subduing Waves and sent south to strike Jiaozhi’; and *Records of the Grand Historian*: ‘General Han Yue was appointed General of Crossing Seas’)—and strike at the hour when the Dipper turns and Orion slants—*dòu zhuǎn shēn héng* (meaning ‘midnight,’ as in Qian Qi’s poem: ‘The Dipper turns, the moon has not yet set’; and Qin Shaoyou’s verse: ‘The moon sets, Orion slants, the painted horn sounds mournful’)—when the weak become meat for the strong to feast upon (a phrase from Han Yu’s *Preface to the Monk Wenchang’s Departure*), who then can punish them? Moreover, on a single boat, who can distinguish the good from the wicked? And yet, how clearly Heaven’s judgment is made manifest!"

At a single thunderclap, the Jing and Wei stand instantly apart—*Jīng Wèi* (the Jing River clear, the Wei River turbid; here used to signify the separation of good and evil)—where the wicked are all executed and the innocent left untouched—*wúgū wǎng jí* (the guiltless not implicated). Shall we call Heaven muddled—*mèngmèng* (as in *The Book of Songs*: ‘Beholding Heaven, so muddled’; *mèngmèng* meaning ‘obscure’)? Yet when has Heaven ever been muddled?

**27. Thunder’s Warning Against Filial Impiety**
The aged and widowed are pitiable souls,
Yet curses and abuse show too little kin.
A warning without execution has its cause—
For he who herds ducks is but a coarse man.

In Jiangyou, a certain Jia made his living herding ducks. His father was old and widowed—*mào* (aged) and *guān* (widower; as in *Mencius*: ‘Old and without a wife is called *guān*’)—and Jia, resenting his father’s idleness, would constantly revile and humiliate him. One day, as he rained down abuse, a sudden thunderclap struck, and Jia was lifted and thrown to his knees in the courtyard. The villagers rushed to see him—his beard, brows, clothes, and trousers all scorched by the lightning’s fire, his spirit and soul dazed, speechless and motionless. Among them was one who could decipher thunder-script, who urged them to quickly overturn the cauldron and look beneath. Following his advice, they found red seal-script characters inscribed there, which read: *Thunder’s Warning Against Filial Impiety*. The villagers pooled their funds to sponsor a *jiào* (a Daoist ritual for prayer and repentance) and carried Jia to kneel before his father. After three days of the ritual, he regained his speech. From then on, he reformed his ways, and the village called him a filial son.

**28. Buried Corpse Receives Reward**
A corpse wrapped in felt, buried by the roadside,
As if the Blue Sky’s Marshal had arranged it so.
Only then did he know official rank depends on hidden virtue—
His palace-examination essays were far from fine.

The father of Marshal Xiao Chunxi, the venerable Pin Sanfeng, served as Warden of the Yichang Prefecture Prison in Hubei. When the White Lotus rebels ravaged Hubei—*róulìn* (trampled, meaning ‘laid waste’)—Yichang was thrown into alarm. Vowing to die for his post, Pin Sanfeng ordered an old servant to escort the Marshal to the capital to seek refuge with his elder brother, a clerk in the Ministry. At the time, weapons blotted out the sky, and bones filled the fields.

Once, while traveling on the road, he came upon a corpse lying naked by the wayside. Moved with pity, Marshal Xiao ordered his servant to bury it by the road, and he even removed the red felt mat he had been sitting on to wrap the body.

In the *xinyou* year’s provincial examination, an examiner in his assigned hall received Marshal Xiao’s paper and was about to discard it. Suddenly, he saw something standing upright before his desk—its entire body was red, yet its face, hands, and feet were indistinguishable. Greatly alarmed, he suspected the discarded paper bore some hidden cause and retrieved it to review again. Finding it unremarkable, he resolved to discard it once more, only for the red figure to reappear before him. Three times he cast it aside, and three times it manifested. At last, he recommended the paper to the chief examiner, who sternly reprimanded him. The examiner remained silent, offering no explanation.

That night, the chief examiner also saw a red apparition moving about his desk. The next day, he recounted this to others. Someone familiar with the examiner’s rejection of the paper informed the chief examiner, who nodded in acknowledgment. That evening, the red figure again stood before him. Tentatively, he retrieved the paper and reviewed it—only for the apparition to vanish. When he placed it among the rejected papers, the figure reappeared as before. With no other recourse, he ranked it last among the successful candidates.

After the results were announced, the examiner questioned Marshal Xiao, who could offer no explanation. Upon returning home and recounting the matter to his servant, the servant replied, “Could this be the corpse you buried by the roadside that day?” Marshal Xiao asked, “Why was its entire body red?” The servant answered, “Did you not remove the red felt mat you were sitting on to wrap it? How could you have forgotten?” Marshal Xiao was struck with sudden realization.

Later, through the *datiao* selection, Marshal Xiao was appointed magistrate of a county in Jiangsu, where he earned the epithet “Blue Sky Xiao.” He rose to the rank of Vice Prefect of Suzhou. The *Master of the Flower Seat* remarks: “Marshal Xiao once governed Nanhui, where his administration was marked by benevolent policies. After his death, his family resided in this district. In the autumn of the *jiayin* year, bandits besieged the city, and it fell. Many of the rebels were local villagers, yet they took care not to violate the household of ‘Blue Sky Xiao.’”

The following day, he prepared boats, gathered all his family and possessions, and escorted them beyond the borders, where they knelt in farewell and departed. Even bandits follow the Dao—this alone shows how a principled official may yet prevail!

---

**29. The Shaanxi Scholar**

Was that fodder-cutter real or not?
His trembling heart still boasted of wrestling ghosts’ might.
A jest turned to no good end—
The wronged soul seized the chance to claim his life.

A certain scholar from Shaanxi studied the legalist arts of Shen Buhai and Han Fei. In his youth, he once misapplied a legal precedent, wrongly condemning a man to death. Ever after, he lived in dread, often recounting the tale to others, fearing that karmic retribution was inescapable.

Later, he accepted a teaching post in a county, where many of his colleagues delighted in telling ghost stories. The scholar, priding himself on his courage, declared that if evil ghosts truly existed, he would seize them with his bare hands. His companions sought a way to test his boldness.

As a banquet was soon to be held, they arrived early at a post station outside the city, where they found no one about. Only in the stables did they encounter a fodder-cutter, his face gaunt and sallow, his frame tall and dark, exuding an eerie presence. The group whispered among themselves, “This man will serve to test the scholar.” They approached him and said, “Tomorrow, we will have someone hide you beneath the bed. After the scholar falls asleep, you are to creep out and lift the bed-curtain to startle him. Can you do this?” The man replied, “I can.” “If you succeed, we will reward you handsomely.” His face brightened with evident delight.

The next day, after the feast concluded and the second watch had begun, the scholar returned to his room and lay down. His companions concealed themselves beneath the window to watch in secret. After a short while, the man emerged from under the bed. Instead of immediately lifting the curtain, he struck his chest and pounded his breast, his ghostly aura chilling the air. The onlookers were all filled with doubt.

Suddenly, they saw the man crouch upon the scholar’s desk, reaching into his robe to draw out a corner of an official document—its red and black ink vivid and gleaming. He leered at the sleeper within the bed-curtain and laughed. The onlookers grew even more suspicious.

The man sprang up, lunging straight for the bed. He flung back the curtain and cried out. From afar, they saw the scholar also lift the curtain and emerge, the two facing each other for a long while. Then the man rolled up his sleeves and strode forward. The scholar shrieked wildly and collapsed to the ground. The group, seized with terror, broke down the door and rushed in to save him, but his breath had already ceased. They searched in all four directions for the man, yet he had vanished without a trace. Inquiring throughout the post station, they found no such person had ever been there. Only then did they realize he was a vengeful ghost, seizing the moment to claim a life.

Master Zuohua recorded this and sighed, saying: “Those who assist others with the harsh methods of Shen Buhai and Han Fei—ought they not be cautious? Yet even in the case of this scholar, his error was merely the result of youth and insufficient mastery of his craft, a momentary misjudgment. Still, after so many years, he met with such a grim retribution. How much more perilous for those who wield the power of life and death, yet regard only bribes?”

When silver crosses the palm, black and white are swiftly confounded—thus, those released from judgment die with grievances unredressed, while the innocent, ensnared in false charges, suffer execution. For officials and their advisors who act thus—what manner of retribution awaits them?”

**30. A Certain Man of Keqiao**
*Betraying a brother’s trust, he reenacts the shame of Xintai—*
*Gnawing his leg in terror, he beholds a black hound’s approach.*
*Devoid of all shame, his life is forfeit—*
*Yet the ghost’s cry of “joy” is but lamentable sorrow.*

In Keqiao Town of Kuaiji, a certain Jia lay gravely ill. Entrusting his young son and daughter-in-law to his younger brother, Yi, he pleaded: “I am dying, and you remain unmarried. Our ancestral line depends solely on this child. I beg you, raise him well, that he may grow to manhood and continue our lineage. Then, though I perish, I shall not be forgotten.” With these words, he clasped his brother’s hand and passed away.

Yi adopted his nephew as his own son. At first, he cared for the boy with great devotion, earning praise from the village. Yet as time passed, the boy remained in tender youth, while the daughter-in-law had reached the age of twenty, her hair now bound with a hairpin. A poor household with few rooms, they dwelt together from dawn to dusk. Soon after, Yi forgot his vow upon the deathbed, and in a sudden lapse, brought upon himself the disgrace of Xintai. His infamy spread far and wide, and those who once commended him now turned to mockery. Several years passed...

After several years, the son had reached the age of twenty, yet Yi still compelled his daughter-in-law to share his bed each night, relegating the son to sleep beneath the bed. The son wept in silence through the long nights, too fearful to speak.

On the Winter Solstice, Yi recalled his brother’s coffin, left exposed in the fields—its thatch rotted away, offering no shelter from wind or rain. He wove a mat of reeds to replace the decayed covering. No sooner had he arrived at the burial site than a black dog suddenly lunged from behind the coffin, sinking its teeth into his leg. Yi shrieked and collapsed; those accompanying him helped him home. Once there, he began to speak in his brother’s voice, rebuking himself for betraying his kin and debauching his daughter-in-law. He struck his own cheeks until they swelled, then tore open his scrotum—flesh shredding and falling away in pieces. “Our ancestors kill this shameless wretch!” he cried, and with repeated shouts of “How satisfying!” he died.

---

**III. Three Accounts from the Eastern Marchmount Temple at An’gang**

The Eastern Marchmount of Mount Tai, domain of Qi and Qing,
Even a village god may wield divine might.
Hearing of An’gang in Dantu’s tale,
How many spring dreams startle awake?

In An’gang Town, Dantu County, stands a branch temple of the Eastern Marchmount, its numinous power resplendent, its responses as swift as an echo. A neighboring village harbored an unfilial son, against whom his mother lodged a complaint with the circuit intendant. The intendant detained the son, but the case remained unresolved. Each day, the man’s wife knelt weeping beside her mother-in-law, pleading for mercy on her husband’s behalf.

The mother, moved to pity, gave twenty silver taels to Zhao of An’gang Town, asking him to present them to the circuit intendant in hopes of securing her son’s release. Zhao concealed most of the sum, offering only five taels to the intendant. The intendant, enraged, rejected the bribe and declared he would send the case to the county court. Zhao emerged and deceived the plaintiff, claiming the intendant’s demands were exorbitant—unless forty taels were swiftly delivered, the case would be forwarded to the county for adjudication under the statute on unfilial impiety. Terrified, the mother returned home, preparing to sell her property to meet the demand.

Just then, word spread that the wife intended to hang herself. Fearing the liability of a death, the intendant promptly sent the son to the county. Upon hearing this, the wife indeed hanged herself. Several days later, Zhao, napping in broad daylight, suddenly leapt up and rushed frantically into the Eastern Marchmount Temple, crying out along the way: “I was wrong to embezzle fifteen taels from the plaintiff’s family, delaying the case’s resolution and causing the woman’s tragic death. Now she has lodged a complaint against me, and the Marchmount Emperor has summoned me for interrogation—I shall surely die!”

Upon entering the temple, he prostrated himself beneath the altar table, wailing piteously as though being beaten. Then, all fell silent. When they looked, his thighs bore overlapping welts from a cudgel, and his breath was barely perceptible. Carried home, he died the following day.

A certain young man suffered from consumption. Someone suggested that a cat’s afterbirth could be compounded into medicine to cure the illness. His family followed this advice, procured one, and mixed it into the medicine they fed the patient. The disease indeed abated. Several months later, the patient suddenly saw two attendants in black robes arrive with a summons, declaring that he had been accused by the cat. One remained to guard his body while the other led him away.

They arrived at an official tribunal, its halls vast and towering, where the Marchmount Emperor sat facing south in full regalia, his retinue arrayed in solemn ranks. A cat crouched beneath the dais, speaking in human words, and lodged its complaint: it had been wrongfully killed when its afterbirth was taken. The young man argued that the act had been committed by his family without his knowledge. The Emperor ordered twenty strikes to his cheeks as a light punishment, then instructed him: “I now release you, but you must swiftly engage the Daoist priest of such-and-such an abbey to perform rites for the cat, guiding it back to the human realm and preventing further karmic debt in future lives.” The young man prostrated himself and bowed in gratitude, after which the black-robed attendants supported him home. Upon waking, his cheeks were swollen.

He urgently told his family to summon the Daoist priest and arrange the ritual. The priest demanded an exorbitant fee, but the impoverished family could not meet it and temporarily set the matter aside—though their hearts remained uneasy. One day, as the young man laughed and chatted with his family, he suddenly glared and cried out in a frenzy: “The black-robed attendants have returned!” He then fell into a prolonged stupor, his mouth speaking in the voice of one of the attendants: “I was dispatched by the Emperor on a distant errand, and now I remain here to guard your household. Would you begrudge me even a cup of plain tea? If I leave, your home will collapse—how then could you ever return to the world of the living?” With this, he gnashed his teeth in fury. The family hastily served him tea, and he drained over ten cups.

Neighbors, hearing of this, flocked to inquire about their deceased kin in the netherworld. Whatever they asked, the answers proved accurate. After nearly a full day, he again spoke in the attendant’s voice: “Your family member has returned!” With that, he collapsed, and after a moment, revived, his mind clear as before. When questioned, he recounted that he had been taken to the same place, where the Marchmount Emperor rebuked him for breaking his promise. Though the reprimand was severe, he escaped further punishment and was merely ordered to hasten the rites. His family then exhausted their resources to comply. Thereafter, all fell quiet.

On the waters of An’gang River dwelt an ancient soft-shelled turtle, who led his kin in residence, their numbers flourishing. Along the river, fishermen would sometimes catch turtles the size of basins and present them to the unofficially wealthy Zhao. Zhao found them delicious and paid handsomely. Delighted, the fishermen redoubled their efforts, catching turtles by every means and offering them to Zhao, always returning with substantial profits. This became their regular practice.

Over a year later, Zhao suddenly dreamed he was at the Temple of the Eastern Marchmount, facing judgment with a being who had a pointed head and a plump body. This being identified itself as the ancient turtle of the river and accused Zhao of slaughtering his descendants to satisfy his appetite. The Marchmount Emperor questioned Zhao, who confessed the fishermen’s offerings. The Emperor rebuked Zhao: “This ancient turtle has made its home here for years, never harming travelers. What did the fishermen know? You, a man of wealth, instead of releasing life to accumulate merit, have indulged your gluttony, taking many lives. The nether laws cannot forgive you.”

Zhao pleaded for a chance to reform, vowing: “If you release me, I will abstain from killing and release living creatures. I will forever refrain from eating cattle, dogs, or any creatures rarely seen, to atone for my past transgressions.” After repeated entreaties, the Emperor ordered a light caning to appease the ancient turtle’s anger. After the punishment, he admonished Zhao: “A single good thought brings blessings from spirits. If you truly abstain from killing, release life, and forever refrain from eating cattle and dogs, you will receive good fortune. But if you persist in your former indulgence, I will not pardon you again.” He then commanded ghostly attendants to escort Zhao out.

Upon awakening, Zhao found his thighs bruised and swollen with purple welts, the pain lingering for days—only with a staff could he rise and walk. Without concealment or evasion, he recounted the entire ordeal to warn others. From that day forth, he forbade his household from consuming the flesh of turtles, terrapins, water monitors, cattle, horses, donkeys, or dogs, and spared no expense in purchasing creatures to set them free. Within a few years, his family’s wealth flourished, and he became the most prosperous man in the village.

**32. Prefect Cai**
*A single writ, penned by his own hand—*
*He repaid another’s debt of three thousand.*

Now at last he believed in the doctrine of karmic rebirth—
*In court robes they met, bound by ties across lifetimes.*

Master Cai Xiaoxia, while serving as provincial governor of Shaanxi (as the *Book of Songs* says, *"The noble man is a fence, the great state a screen"*—here meaning he held the office of provincial treasurer in Shaanxi), had a subordinate magistrate who, due to old age and illness, petitioned for retirement. This magistrate had misappropriated three thousand taels from the public treasury, a deficit later exposed by his successor. At the time, the laws were exceedingly strict: embezzlement of even a few hundred taels resulted in confiscation of property and imprisonment to compel repayment. If the debt remained unpaid by the deadline, the penalty was death. The magistrate had been incorruptible in office, and after retirement, he possessed scarcely a single tael. Moreover, he was upright and aloof, with few connections (as the *Book of Later Han* says of Wang Fu, *"unyielding and unswayed by worldly customs"*), and among his colleagues, none could be relied upon for aid. He could only await his doom in silent resignation.

Governor Cai, learning of his plight, took pity on him. The next day, he summoned the magistrate and, dismissing all attendants, said to him: *"I know you lack the means to repay the three thousand taels you owe. Prepare a memorial requesting resolution, and I will endorse it to close the case."* The magistrate, stunned, replied, *"I dare not."* The governor smiled and said, *"I do not jest. I pity your integrity and the fact that you were wronged in service to the public. I intend to use my rightful salary to make up the deficit for you. However, this matter cannot be resolved in a day, so I wish first to endorse your memorial to spare you the hardship of prolonged detention."* The magistrate, overwhelmed by this unexpected kindness, could not speak; he kowtowed and withdrew.

The following day, he submitted the memorial as instructed. Governor Cai personally inscribed the date of repayment, affixed his seal, and returned it to him. The magistrate, dressed in his court robes, entered to express his gratitude. Kneeling, he declared in a loud voice: *"I owe you a debt of rebirth. I am old now and can never repay you in this life—after death, I will beg to be reborn in your family to repay this great kindness."* He then departed.

Over a decade later, Governor Cai also retired and returned to his hometown.

Seated in the hall, his vision blurred in drowsiness, Governor Cai suddenly saw the magistrate enter in court robes to express his gratitude—no different from before. He thought to himself: *This is not the Shaanxi provincial yamen, and the magistrate has long since returned home. How could he be here?* Just as he was bewildered, the magistrate strode directly inside. Startled, Governor Cai cried out and awoke, only to be told from the inner chamber that his son had been born.

Governor Cai declared, *"This is the man reborn! He shall revive our family."* Thus, he named him Zhenwu, with the courtesy name Linzhou. Before reaching adulthood, the boy already ranked first among the child scholars. In the bingshen year, he passed the metropolitan examination and entered the Hanlin Academy. Later, as an observer in eastern Guangdong, he earned a reputation for good governance. As a provincial governor and grand coordinator, his rise to prominence came with effortless ease.

**33. The Murdered Maid’s Vengeance**

A wife of a wealthy household in Hangzhou was exceedingly arrogant and fierce. She treated her maidservants with brutal cruelty; the slightest displeasure would leave them flogged until their skin was shredded. Once, she beat a maid to death with a rod. Her mother-in-law, upon hearing of this, sternly reprimanded her, but she refused to repent. Later, she even resorted to the punishment of *paoluo*—roasting with hot iron!

One day, a maid displeased her. Enraged, she heated an iron in blazing coals until it glowed red, stripped the maid naked, and branded her flesh until her entire body was scorched and blistered. The maid shrieked in agony before dying. Within days, the mistress fell into a mad frenzy. The two maids possessed her body, demanding their lives in return. At times, she tore out her own hair; at others, she slapped her own face. Needles pierced her, blades slashed her—all mirroring the torments she had inflicted upon them.

Her mother-in-law came to question her. The possessed woman knelt and addressed her as "Old Mistress," saying, *"Though we are dead, we dare not forget your kindness."* The mother-in-law urged them to abandon their vengeance and promised to invite eminent monks to perform rites for their salvation. The maids refused, saying, *"Had the young mistress heeded your words, we would not have died unjustly. Before you, Old Mistress, we dare not be disrespectful—but we cannot delay her death."* Thereafter, whenever the mother-in-law visited, the woman’s demeanor would briefly clear. Once the mother-in-law departed, the torment resumed, until at last they claimed her life.

---

**34. The Headless Men**

Great crimes demand meticulous investigation; though the execution date draws near, one must not act in haste. A village headman is no match for a grand marshal—how pitiful that the lowly pay for the sins of the mighty.

In the autumn of the *bingchen* year, a vast army gathered in Danyang. Grand Marshal Xiang, the Loyal and Martial Duke, perished in the camp. Governor Yiyue of the Imperial Residence, traveling from Changzhou to assume command, was met by six military officers from Guangxi. Acting on their commander’s orders, they escorted him back. Passing through Lǚchéng, their boat clashed with a civilian vessel. The six officers, wielding their authority, leapt aboard the civilian boat with drawn blades. They struck a man into the water with the flat of their swords and plundered the silver aboard.

The civilians cried out for help. At that moment, the militia of Lǚchéng had gathered along both banks, awaiting the grand marshal’s arrival. Hearing the shouts from the river, they rushed to the scene. The six officers fought desperately with their weapons. Mistaking them for bandits, the militia overpowered them, killing three and binding the remaining three. By then, the crowd had surged forward, blades and cudgels raining down. Reason was no longer possible—they slew all three captives. Only two boatmen managed to slip away and flee, rushing to report the incident to their commander.

The wing commander flew into a rage and sternly ordered the Dānyáng County magistrate to apprehend the criminals and prepare for their execution. At that time, the magistrate of Dānyáng was a certain *Sīmǎ* (a title equivalent to *tóngzhī*, or vice-magistrate), who had been acting in the post for a year. His replacement had already been appointed, following the custom that "when the melons ripen, the relief arrives." Suddenly confronted with this major case—and warned that he would not be permitted to hand over his duties until the criminals were captured and the case closed—the *Sīmǎ* was terrified. He posted a hefty reward for their capture. Within twenty days, five of the criminals were seized, but one, Jīn Ādé, remained at large. The wing commander insisted on the principle of "a life for a life," and no exception could be made for the missing man. Ādé’s elder brother, who served as the village head (*lǐzhèng*, or local constable) of Lǚchéng, had been in the city to deliver the other prisoners. He was seized as well and thrown into prison, where he was executed alongside the five others in the marketplace. The case was thus closed, and the *Sīmǎ* handed over his seal before returning to the provincial capital. No sooner had he arrived at his residence than he fell ill. His household, young and old, all saw a headless ghost following a long-haired figure as they wandered through the halls. On the day of his death, a maidservant emerged from the *Sīmǎ*’s bedchamber and saw the long-bearded man leading the headless ghost straight into the room. She screamed and collapsed to the ground. Those keeping vigil rushed in upon hearing her cries, but by the time the maidservant revived, the *Sīmǎ* had already passed away.

The Master of the Flowery Couch remarked: "When matters arise suddenly, the lives of common folk hang in the balance. Those who govern (*sīmù*, referring to officials at all levels) should argue on principle, plead for the people’s lives, and strive for the highest good.
