Failing that, they should mediate between higher and lower, lighten the severity of the sentence, and ensure that the living are free of grievance and the dead accept their fate—this is the next best course. But to disregard the lives of the people, bowing only to power and authority, leaving the wretched with no recourse until they are led in droves to execution—though their bodies die, their hearts do not. How fitting that they should return as vengeful spirits!

*Three, Five: Vermilion-Marked Examination Papers*
Guard well what you write in life’s span,
For the provincial exams reveal your plan.
Awakened from a dream with three cries of "Wrong!"
Vermilion-marked papers—ten exams undone.

In the autumn of Xīnhài during the reign of Xiánfēng, at the first session of the provincial examinations in Zhèjiāng, a candidate from Yín County had completed his three essays, with both draft and fair copies finished. The sky had yet to brighten, so he sat and dozed. In his half-slumber, he suddenly cried out in a loud voice, "I was wrong! I know my guilt!"

The candidate then scrawled upon his examination paper in large characters: *"For cutting off a man’s lineage, the penalty is ten sessions of provincial examinations barred, and his name shall be recorded in the Register of the Childless and Solitary."* Beside it, he wrote: *"By Order of the Great Demon-Quelling Emperor."* At daybreak, he staggered from his cell to submit his paper. The examiner, seeing the fresh vermilion script splashed across the page, searched his satchel but found no red brush. Observing the man—dazed and witless—they knew a ghost still clung to him. Supporting him out, they inscribed his name upon the rejection list. This account was witnessed firsthand in the examination hall by Gentleman Yáo Yòuqīng.

*Thirty-Six: A Dream of Cranes Dancing*
Why did cranes appear in dreams the night before?
In the vast hall, they seemed so full of grace.
Yet when the cranes took flight, hope turned to vain longing—
Where once there was affection, now only sorrow remains.

A certain eminent scholar of our Háng Prefecture, though poor, possessed such striking beauty that he was called "the Jade Man." After passing the provincial examination, he took up a teaching post in the grand household of Wúmén.

At this time, the grand household was celebrating a joyous occasion, its halls adorned for feasting and well-wishes, with courtesans and musicians arrayed in full display. A renowned courtesan, unmatched in beauty and artistry of her age, dreamed the night before that in a vast gathering, she saw a crane standing on one leg, dancing before her. Its feathered robe swayed in graceful motion, its purity and elegance utterly captivating. A bystander told her, *"This is the one to whom you shall be bound for life."* The courtesan then combed its plumes, and with a flutter of its wings, it soared away—startling her awake.

The next day, as she arrived at the grand household to perform at the banquet, she found the brilliantly lit hall just as she had seen in her dream. At that moment, half the distinguished scholars of Wú Prefecture were present, yet she could not tell which of them was the man from her vision. After several rounds of wine, the guests rose to disperse and change attire. The eminent scholar removed his formal robes and donned a white lined garment, resting one foot upon a chair as he stood facing the courtesan. His bearing was noble and striking, his features as finely drawn as a painting. An acquaintance of the courtesan teased her, *"This is such-and-such a gentleman—do you two make a fitting pair?"*

Though the courtesan merely smiled faintly, she reflected that the dream had aligned with reality, and seeing the eminent scholar’s dignified and composed bearing, she knew he was destined for greatness. Seizing an opportune moment, she secretly conveyed her tender feelings. She invited the scholar to her home, offering herself to him. The scholar, captivated by her beauty and moved by her earnest devotion, could not bring himself to refuse harshly. At the time, his lute strings were broken, and so they pledged a vow of lifelong union. The courtesan, already possessed of considerable wealth, rejoiced in having found her destined match. Once she had given herself to the scholar, she closed her door to all other guests. Whatever the scholar desired, she spared no effort to procure for him.

The following year, the scholar traveled north to the capital for the imperial examinations. The courtesan generously provided him with gold, enabling him to expand his connections. He passed the metropolitan examination and entered the Hanlin Academy. When he took leave to return home, he detoured through Suzhou, where their affection deepened. They pledged that upon reaching Hangzhou to inform his parents, he would prepare betrothal gifts and formally welcome her into his household.

Yet upon his return, he discovered that his father had already arranged his marriage to another family! Bound by his father’s strict discipline, the scholar dared not utter a word. In the end, he broke his promise. The courtesan waited in vain, and when she sent someone to inquire at his home, she learned he had already wed. She wrote to reproach him, even expressing her willingness to serve as his concubine, but received no reply. When his leave ended and he returned to the capital, he passed through Suzhou yet dared not approach her door.

The courtesan, having long since ceased receiving guests, saw her beauty fade and her wealth depleted. Some urged her to take up her pipa again, but she would only sigh deeply and remain silent, ultimately dying in sorrow. When the scholar later heard of her death, he would often exclaim to others, *"Fufu!"*—a cry of deep remorse.

In his later years, the scholar had a son who, upon growing older, proved fond of dissipation and squandered the family fortune. It was said that the moment the child was born, the courtesan had appeared in the room.

The Master of the Flower Seat remarked: *"The scholar, fearing to defy his parents, thus committed the fault of heartless betrayal. If we weigh his intentions, his offense may yet be somewhat mitigated. Yet having once admired her beauty and exhausted her wealth—during their tender and joyous union, did he truly not know his family’s strictness? How could he have so lightly pledged his word? When she, in desperation, offered to accept even the lowest rank of concubine, he might have humbled himself to comply—but he did not even deign to reply. Alas! How could he be so cruel?"*

"37. The Second Wife Struck by Lightning
On her deathbed, she knew her successor’s heart—
With tearful, earnest pleas, she made her last request.
How could she bear to wound him with poison’s art?
Heaven’s thunder severed her arm—a warning dire.

A certain man, Jia, lost his wife in middle age, leaving behind a son only a few years old. Unable to endure widowhood, Jia heard that a woman of the same neighborhood, a Miss Chen, was beautiful, and took her as his second wife. She bore him two sons. Chen was secretly treacherous and harbored hatred for her stepson. Fortunately, Jia’s authority as head of the household remained strong, and she dared not act on her malice. Though her heart was biased, in Jia’s presence she carried, embraced, and raised the boy as if he were more cherished than her own. Before long, Jia also died.

On his deathbed, he took the hand of his first wife’s son and said to Chen: *'Our meager estate, divided three ways, should still suffice for modest comfort. This child lost his mother in infancy; without your care, how could he have grown to this age? Now that I am dying, I beg you to continue treating him well and not let him suffer want.'* With lingering reluctance, he passed away.

After his death, Chen’s evil intentions suddenly surfaced, and she treated her stepson like a slave. In clothing and food, she refused to let him share equally with her own sons."

The "reed-flower heart of blackness"—such evil is without equal. Once, she dreamed her husband sternly admonished her, yet she still did not reform. Her stepson, however, was deeply filial; in all things, he sought to please his stepmother. As he grew older, he apprenticed at a money shop, and whatever he earned, he dutifully offered to her—only to incur her ever-greater resentment.

One day, she steamed rice cakes and called her eldest son home to eat them. Suddenly, a thunderclap resounded, and she was flung to her knees in the courtyard, still clutching the cakes. She confessed that she had laced them with poison, intending to murder her stepson so her own son could seize the entire inheritance. But her husband and his first wife had rushed to the gods to plead their case, and thus she was struck by lightning. Hearing this, her eldest son hurried home and, with his two brothers, knelt and wailed, praying to heaven to spare their mother. After a full day and night, thunder crashed again, breaking one of her arms before she could rise. From then on, she repented and reformed, treating all three sons as her own.

---

**Three Eight: Gu Yunqiao**

*Three branches of the jade tree snapped in grief—*
*To turn from one’s roots, to forsake one’s kin, is to invite such retribution.*
*Ghosts may hunger, but mortal lives are fleeting;*
*For another’s sake, one must not sever another’s line.*

Gu Yunqiao’s father was originally the son of the Liuhe Cheng clan but was adopted into the Gu family. By Yunqiao’s generation, the Cheng lineage had no heir. Relatives from both families agreed that Yunqiao had an elder brother, born of the main wife, who should have carried on the Gu line, while Yunqiao himself ought to have returned to the Cheng clan—both by ritual and by law, this would have been proper. But Yunqiao, unwilling to inconvenience his own interests, refused.

Thus, the sacrifices to the Cheng ancestors were cut off entirely. At the time, Yunqiao had three sons, all bright and promising, with hopes of future success. His eldest son, having just passed twenty, was about to marry when he suddenly fell ill. In his sickness, he saw several men and women demanding sacrifices from him, and he descended into madness. He would cry out for Yunqiao, reproaching him for forsaking his roots and abandoning his kin. All who witnessed this knew the Cheng ancestors’ ghosts were exacting vengeance. They urged Yunqiao to marry off his eldest son and restore him to the Cheng clan, but Yunqiao still refused. The eldest son soon died.

Yunqiao then arranged marriages for his two younger sons, his uncle’s second and third children. Within a year, both sons perished in succession. Yunqiao had each of their widowed wives bear a grandson, but one of them died young. Soon after, Yunqiao himself followed them to the grave. Now, only one young grandson remains, already afflicted with spitting blood. Whether this fragile thread of descent will endure—whether the gods and spirits will secretly aid him—remains unseen.

The Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarked: "I once met Gu Yunqiao in passing. I heard he was a man of considerable sincerity and prudence, yet by a single misguided thought, he fell into the sorrow of the Nine Springs! For descendants, how can one be so blinded by profit as to forget righteousness?"

**39. Oral Retribution**

Two instances must not be held against each other—
Yet within a few short years, retribution arrives in strange guise.
Heed a wife’s words, and the household is sure to decline;
How can a hen presume to take the rooster’s crow at dawn?

A certain gentleman of a great clan traveled to the capital to seek office. From Jiangyou, he brought his wife and children, escorting his venerable mother to Yangzhou. There, his clansman, Prefectural Vice-Director So-and-So, was serving as Salt Controller in the Southern Bureau. The gentleman entrusted his family to the vice-director’s yamen while he proceeded alone to the capital. Before his appointment could be secured, he died in his lodgings.

At that time, the venerable mother was already advanced in years, and the gentleman’s wife was nearing childbirth and often ailing. When the news of his death arrived, the vice-director plotted to conceal it temporarily, waiting until the wife had given birth and completed her month of confinement before making it known. The vice-director’s concubine, a certain Madam So-and-So, had managed the inner household as the senior chamber attendant since the passing of his principal wife. Upon hearing this plan, she alone objected, saying, “Each family keeps to its own affairs—how can a funeral matter be long imposed upon another household?” She promptly went and revealed the truth, urging them to rent another residence so that they might properly set up a mourning altar and don mourning attire. Though the vice-director reproached her, the words had already been spoken, and there was nothing to be done.

Several years later, the vice-director was promoted through recommendation to govern a major prefecture. He departed alone for his post, leaving his family in Jinchang to await further instructions. At that time, the prefect was still in his prime, his record of governance—marked by diligence and integrity—standing foremost among the Three Wu regions.

The singular favor of imperial recognition, bestowed from the nine-tiered palace steps, had raised him to the rank of provincial governor and judicial commissioner—success so assured it seemed within easy reach. Yet his wife, having long overseen the household with the dignity of an equal consort, remained unaware of fate’s swift turn.

That year marked her thirtieth birthday. On the appointed day, she adorned the halls with lanterns and music, accepting congratulations from all her kin, reveling in her own contentment. What she did not know was that the prefect, before even assuming his post, had fallen gravely ill upon reaching Yuanzhou and died suddenly—just one day before her celebration. The news of his death arrived in time.

Her sons and nephews all urged that they wait until after her birthday to announce the mourning and begin the rites of lamentation. But the prefect’s eldest son, who happened to be in Suzhou, stood firm against it, saying, “What manner of grave matter is this? How could we still feast and accept felicitations while mourners stand at the gate?”

The eldest son entered the inner chambers to inform Lady So-and-so of the grim tidings, then personally led the household in removing all lanterns and decorations, changing into mourning garb, and beginning the rites of lamentation.

The Master of the Flower Banquet remarked, “This incident, passed down in common talk, occurred no more than a few years ago, yet it remains a byword to this day. Alas! Women and petty-minded folk, ignorant of great principle, often feign righteous argument to serve their own ends. Though those who suffer such impositions may offer no immediate rebuttal, the resentment festers in their hearts like a bone-deep thorn, lingering long after. What is sown in the earth waits only for its season to sprout. As the saying goes, ‘Words that issue forth in defiance shall return in defiance.’ How true!”

---

**40. The Great Stone Lad**

*Where the cowherd once sought the Dragon King’s grace,*
*All praise the mighty Stone Lad of Chongming’s place.*
*When floods threatened Liu River’s shore,*
*Two lands owed him incense evermore.*

The Great Stone Lad was a native of Chongming. Of forthright bearing, great strength, and courage, he was especially fond of releasing living creatures. In his youth, he herded cattle by the sea, and whenever he came upon shrimp, snails, clams, or mussels, he would gather and release them, never killing so much as a single creature.

Once, a large clam rode the tide ashore and settled on the sand. With every opening and closing of its shell, a dazzling radiance flashed forth, growing even more brilliant at night. Onlookers all declared it must contain a luminous pearl, and that whoever obtained it would gain untold wealth. They clamored to split it open and seize the treasure, but the Great Lad refused, saying, “It is a numinous being—it must not be harmed.”

He then pushed through the crowd, lifted the clam, and cast it back into the sea, where it rode the tide away.

Several years later, the rice fields along the coast were often trampled by some creature. Those who lay in wait to observe saw an ox. When they drove it off, it fled toward the seashore. They thus pointed to it as the ox the Great Lad had herded and reported the matter to its owner to demand recompense. The Great Lad made no defense but thought to himself that the oxen he had herded had never once strayed, so how could they trample others’ fields?

Soon after, another complaint came that an ox had eaten the field’s grain. Enraged, the Great Lad went to the place under cover of night and concealed himself to wait. At dawn the next day, he indeed saw a massive ox, its coat and horns strikingly fine, bearing a strong resemblance to his own ox. It emerged from the seashore and frolicked along the field ridges, wreaking havoc as it trampled.

The Great Lad sprang up and seized it, but the ox, startled, wheeled around and fled. He gave chase, and step by step it entered the sea. Enraged, the Great Lad followed it into the waters. The waves parted, and towering billows stood like walls. Suddenly, he saw a grand estate—its gates and walls soaring high, resplendent in gold and jade. The ox leapt through the gate, and in his fury, the Great Lad pursued it inside. The guards shouted at him to stop, but he refused to yield and charged forward in defiance. Then a young nobleman, richly attired, emerged from within and barked at the crowd: “What rogue dares force his way in here? Seize him at once—do not let him escape!” The guards all rushed forward with all their might to apprehend him.

Amidst the Great Lad’s alarm and distress, the young nobleman eyed him repeatedly, then suddenly asked in astonishment, “Are you not the Great Lad who herds oxen by the seaside shoals?” The Great Lad replied, “Indeed, I am.” “Then you are my benefactor! How have you come here?” He sharply ordered the guards to withdraw, then ushered the Great Lad into the hall. Seated, he explained, “This is the Dragon Palace, and I am the Dragon King’s youngest son. Yesterday, I transformed into a clam and went out for a stroll—had you not graciously aided me, I would have nearly fallen into the hands of children. Your great kindness has long gone unrequited; now that I have fortunately encountered you, it truly fulfills my long-held wish. Yet this place lies deep beneath the sea—how were you able to come here?”

The Great Lad told him the truth. The prince exclaimed in surprise, “If that is so, you may have been able to come, but you cannot leave! What is to be done?” The Great Lad asked for the reason, and the prince said, “The one you just pursued is the Dragon Palace’s rhinoceros-ox. Its horn parts the waters, which is why you were able to follow it here. But now it is over! Beyond this gate, you cannot take even a single step—how could you ever hope to return to the human world?” The Great Lad, in dire straits, bowed deeply and begged for help. The prince said, “I shall plead with my father on your behalf, to repay your great virtue.” With that, he departed.

A moment later, the prince returned, holding a pearl, which he presented, saying, “This is the Water-Repelling Pearl, the Dragon Palace’s most precious treasure. Carry it as you leave the sea, and you shall tread as though on level ground. Yet you must guard it well—let no other take it from you.” With earnest words, he saw him off.

No sooner had the Great Lad stepped beyond the palace gate than he found himself amid boundless mist and waves, with nowhere to set foot. He raised the pearl in his hand and waved it toward the water. Suddenly, amid the surging vastness, a smooth path appeared. Following it, he reached the shore in an instant, his clothes and shoes untouched by moisture. All who witnessed this marveled.

The Great Lad, however, could not restrain himself. He constantly flaunted his ability before others, clutching the pearl as he moved through towering waves and raging currents. The crowd plotted to seize it. One day, six or seven cowherds, spying the Great Lad in a light slumber, rose together and ransacked his belongings. Fearing he would lose the pearl and have nothing to show the Dragon King and his son, he tucked it into his mouth and leapt up to fight. The assailants, startled, scattered in all directions.

The pearl, too, slipped into his throat—he could neither spit it out nor swallow it down, and so he died. After his death, his body was placed in a coffin and left upon the seashore. One night, wind and rain shook the earth, and when dawn came, the place where the coffin had rested had become a great burial mound. The next year, the sea overflowed its bounds, and before the Great Lad’s tomb, a sandbank rose up. Wherever the sea’s waters passed, the land crumbled away, yet the Great Lad’s tomb stood alone, towering and unshaken. The people of the coast took him for a deity and built a temple to worship him. The Great Lad, too, repeatedly displayed miraculous powers.

Earlier, the Liu River had suffered many maritime disasters, deterring merchants from traveling there. After the Great Lad’s death, the residents of Liu River dreamed one night of a divine figure, resplendent in majesty and attended by an imposing retinue, who called out to them and said: *“I am Shi Dalang of Chongming! I have heard that the Liu River is to be swallowed by the sea, and my heart aches for you. Go swiftly and move my coffin to the river’s mouth—bury me there, and you may escape your calamity.”* Hundreds dreamed the same dream that night. Astonished, they hurried to inquire among the people of Chongming and indeed found the tomb of Shi Dalang. When they sought to relocate his coffin, the people of Chongming refused. So they petitioned at the Great Lad’s temple, requesting his processional statue be brought back and buried there. A grand burial mound in the shape of a horse’s mane was raised, and the tomb itself became a temple. No sooner had the work been completed than the sea surged forth—yet its waters halted at the edge of the grave. From then on, the Liu River was spared from maritime disasters. In recent years, its population has flourished, and it has nearly returned to its former prosperity.

The temple of Shi Dalang by the Liu River is no less spiritually potent than the one in Chongming. Each spring and autumn, grand festivals are held in his honor, with magnificent processions. The Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarks: *“Shi Dalang was but a peasant’s son. A single thought of compassion spared him the perils of the waves in life and won him the honor of worship in death. Why, then, should one hesitate or doubt? Why not hasten to do good?”*

A Tale of Forty-One: The Wind Whips the Hemp Skirt—A Snowfall of Grievance

When Prefectural Inspector Liang was serving as magistrate of Funing, he once had business in the prefectural seat. Setting out early, he had already entered Shanyang’s borders when he saw, beside his sedan, a young woman in white mourning robes and a hemp skirt, clutching paper ingots and walking alone with slow, halting steps—clearly one newly bereaved. Suddenly, a whirlwind caught her skirt, revealing beneath it red undergarments, which struck him as most unusual. He ordered his retinue to slow their pace and follow her. Whether she drew near or fell back, the hemp skirt still concealed white beneath; but whenever she strayed too far, a whirlwind would rise and twist her skirt, turning the hidden layer red—now deep, now pale, never the same hue.

After following for about a *li*, they came to a fresh grave. The woman swept the ground, burned the paper ingots, and wept without sorrow. Suddenly, a whirlwind scattered her paper money, some of which fell before the magistrate’s sedan. From afar, he saw her face grow dejected; she knelt and kowtowed repeatedly, praying without end. Sensing something amiss, Liang summoned an attendant to follow her, secretly inquiring after her name, her village, the identity of the deceased, the date of his death, and the illness that claimed him.

The attendant returned with the facts: the dead man was the woman’s husband, who had died suddenly without prior illness. He was hastily encoffined and buried with unseemly haste. Though the woman bore a tarnished reputation, her family was not impoverished. Once Liang had ascertained the truth, he presented himself to the prefect after his audience and recounted all he had witnessed to the magistrate of Shanyang. The magistrate dismissed his concerns with a laugh, paying them no heed.

Incensed, Liang took the matter to the prefect, who only smiled and said, *“Enough, sir! This is Shanyang’s affair—why trouble yourself to overstep your bounds?”*

Still seething with indignation, Liang sought an audience with Sun Jipu, the *jiexiang* at Yuanpu—*jiexiang* being the title for a grand minister sent out as governor-general. He laid out all he had witnessed and declared, *“I draw salary and rank from the court yet stand by while injustice festers, unable to redress it. How can I face the living and the dead? What use is this office to me?”* The *jiexiang*, impressed by his fervor, asked, *“Since you wish to pursue this case, how do you propose to proceed?”* Liang replied, *“Let me issue a summons to the magistrate of Shanyang to join me in exhuming the body. If no wounds are found, I will resign my post. Grant me one month, and I will uncover the cause of death. If I fail, I will accept the penalty for false accusation.”* The *jiexiang* consented.

When the coffin was opened, the corpse had not yet decayed, and not a hair on its body was harmed. The crowd erupted in uproar, all decrying Liang as a meddlesome official slandering the innocent. The magistrate of Shanyang even incited the young widow to block Liang’s carriage and prevent his return. Liang thundered at them, *“As an officer of the law, I have grounds to investigate. If my inquiry is flawed, leaving the living aggrieved and the dead exposed, the law of the land will hold me accountable—not the likes of you.”* With unyielding dignity, he mounted his carriage and departed, none daring to obstruct him.

Upon returning, Liang immediately took up his official seal and journeyed to Yuanpu to surrender it and submit to disciplinary action. The *jiexiang*, who had long respected him, said, *“I warned you not to act rashly. Now that no wounds have been found, what can be done?”* Liang replied, *“I willingly accept punishment. But if you would show me mercy, grant me one more month as I previously requested. I will surely uncover the truth through investigation. My only fear is that, in haste, we may need to apprehend and interrogate the culprit, yet local civil and military officials, rigidly guarding their jurisdictions, might allow the murderer to flee. That is my gravest concern.”* The *jiexiang* said, *“If that is so, take my arrow of command. If you fail within a month, I will not shield you.”* Liang bowed deeply, took the arrow, and departed. Disguising himself and altering his attire, he set out to investigate in all directions. Twenty days passed with no leads.

One day, posing as a cloth merchant, he traveled through the borderlands between mountains and hills—the frontier of Shanyang and Funing. As dusk fell, he had nowhere to go.

..., seeking a place to lodge for the night. After winding along for a little over a *li*, he came to a village of fewer than ten households, all with their doors already shut. Only a few paces beyond the village stood several thatched huts, where lamplight still glimmered. He hastened toward them. The wicker gate was half-closed; he pushed it open and entered directly. An old woman, sewing by the lamp, looked up in alarm and asked, “What brings you here, traveler?” Liang explained his need for shelter, adding, “With night fallen and no other recourse, I beg but a few feet of ground to shield me from wind and dew. I will not quibble over the price of the room.” The old woman replied, “Lodging you is no trouble, but my son is ill-tempered. I fear he may take offense when he returns.” She led him to a room and said, “Rest here for now. If you hear him come back, pray keep silent to avoid a quarrel.” Liang nodded in assent.

He sat on the straw mat, feigning sleep while awaiting dawn. At the fourth watch, a knock sounded at the door—it was the son returning. He heard the old woman scold him: “With the year’s harvest bountiful, you might yet live decently, yet you persist in this! You’ll bring ruin upon your old mother yet!” The son made no reply. Soon came the sound of him fetching firewood and rummaging in the kitchen for food. His mother told him, “There’s a traveler lodging in the thatched hut. You’d best treat him well.” The son took a torch inside, scrutinized Liang, and gave a faint smirk: “Mother knows so little of the world. Luckily, this is a good man—otherwise, it could have been dangerous.” Then he called Liang to rise. Seeing no malice in his manner, Liang stood and exchanged bows. They inquired after each other’s names and origins. Learning Liang had not yet eaten, the son hastily led him to the guest seat, brought out liquor and meat, and drank with him face to face, his speech bold and unrestrained. Liang asked how he made his living, but the son only smiled and said nothing. Liang pressed further: “What do you make of Magistrate Liang’s governance here?” The son replied, “He is upright and cares for the people—but he is in peril now.” Liang feigned ignorance: “How so?” The son smiled: “Because of the case in Shanyang, where a woman surnamed Mou plotted to kill her husband. Magistrate Liang is truly discerning—he saw the injustice in the case. Yet without consulting me, he would never have uncovered the truth.”

Hearing his words carried weight, Liang feigned provocation: “The roads hum with talk—all say this case has Magistrate Liang fond of stirring trouble and framing the innocent. Now you speak as you do—does that mean there truly is an injustice?” The son only smiled and gave no answer; Liang likewise let the matter drop. They drank deeply and talked at length, finding much accord. Liang proposed they swear brotherhood in the bond of gold and orchid. The son did not refuse. They burned incense and exchanged bows, and together paid their respects to his mother. The next day, Liang prepared to depart, but the son insisted he stay. By evening, Liang pressed him again about the case. The son still would not answer. Enraged, Liang said: “Now that we have sworn as brothers, we ought to bare our hearts to one another—how can you still conceal things? Or do you still take me for an outsider? Then let us part here.” He rose in anger, ready to leave. The son smiled and said: “I do not dare conceal anything—what is at stake is too great, so I dared not speak lightly! Now I shall lay it bare for you, but you must not speak of it to outsiders.” He rose, barred the door, and led Liang back inside. Smiling, he said: “Tell me, brother—what manner of man do you take me for?” Liang smiled in return: “A bold knight of the rivers and lakes.” “Just so. When there are those in town or countryside who act without righteousness, I go by night to take from them. I use it to sustain myself and also to aid the poor. I have done this for years, and by good fortune have not yet been exposed. Last month, I heard that in a certain village in Shanyang, a certain household was hiding a guest’s wealth of a thousand taels. I went to take it, but by mistake entered the home of the dead man and perched in a tree in the courtyard. There I saw a man and woman drinking together, their manner intimate and wanton. When they had drunk to mild intoxication, suddenly there came a knock at the door. The woman quickly put away the drinking vessels, hid the man in the narrow passage outside the room, and only then opened the door. Another man entered, his steps unsteady. He went straight into the room and collapsed onto the bed. The woman called to him but received no answer; she shook him, but he did not stir. She helped him up, but he fell again. Then she went out and called in the man with whom she had been drinking. He produced an iron nail and drove it into the first man’s skull through his hair. The man rolled on the ground for some time before lying still. The other man rose, opened the door, and left. The woman then cried out, summoning the neighbors to see. All took it for sudden death by poisoning, and none examined the hairline. Yesterday, when the body was opened for inspection, I was present. I saw the man with whom she had been drinking give a large packet of silver to the coroner of Shanyang. Though the hairline was examined, no wound was reported. That night, when I returned home, I did not breathe a word of it even to my mother. Yet I feel this matter will eventually come to light. My mistaken entry into that house was surely Heaven’s intent, that I might serve as witness.” Liang said: “Indeed.” Then he smiled and asked: “And what manner of man do you take *me* for?”

“He replied: ‘A cloth merchant exchanging silk.’” (The phrase "exchanging cloth for silk" refers to a traveling merchant, these four characters originating from the *Book of Songs*, “Wei Feng.”)

“No,” Liang said, “it is none other than Liang of Funing.”

Hearing this, the man’s face turned ashen. He knelt and kowtowed, begging for death. Liang laughed and stopped him: “Do not be so. Our sworn brotherhood is already settled—how could it be altered? Moreover, you are not the culprit in this case. I am about to embark on a journey of ten thousand *li*. You are my benefactor, and I shall surely repay your great kindness. I merely summoned you to testify during the inquiry, nothing more.” That evening, Liang again lodged at his home, conversing and laughing as before.

The next day, Liang proceeded to Yuanpu, where he called upon the provincial governor and recounted the entire affair. The governor then issued an order to the magistrates of Shanyang, Qinghe, and Funing, commanding the coroners of the three counties to conduct a joint examination. Indeed, a large nail was found embedded in the hairline. The adulteress was brought forward, but she refused to confess. The man was summoned to the tribunal and made to recount the details of that night’s murder—every word as vivid as if painted. Thereupon, she bowed her head and admitted her guilt, also supplying the name of her paramour. When the man was seized and brought in, he made no further denials, his confession matching the woman’s in every detail. Both were sentenced according to the law.

The governor held Liang in even higher esteem and recommended him to the imperial court. Within a few years, Liang was appointed prefect of Huaiyang. He had the man’s mother and son brought to his yamen, where he provided for their care, purchasing fields and establishing a household for them. To the end of their days, he treated them with the same respect as his own elder and younger brothers.

---

**42. Discarding Rice into the Privy**

A vile custom spreads the name of cursed rice;
When thunder roars in autumn’s ninth month’s skies,
The bystander’s remark reveals true hidden grace.

The five grains must never lightly be disdained.
In Pudong (a place name in Songjiang Prefecture, under Shanghai County), there exists a pernicious custom: whenever a person is falsely accused and cannot clear their name, they take a handful of rice, raise it to heaven, and swear, “If I truly committed this deed of which I stand accused, may heaven’s thunder strike me dead. If another has falsely slandered me, may heaven’s thunder strike them instead.” After uttering this oath, they cast the rice into the privy (a euphemism for a cesspit).

No custom is more vile than this. In the ninth month of the *renzi* year of Xianfeng’s reign, when thunder had already ceased for the season, an apprentice in a Beijing goods shop was reprimanded by his master for a missing item. The apprentice protested his innocence. By afternoon, dark clouds gathered on all sides, and thunder rumbled ominously, circling the rooftop without striking. Outside, goods were left to dry, and the master, fearing they would be drenched by sudden rain, ordered the apprentice to retrieve them, forcing him to go. No sooner had he stepped outside than the thunder followed, swirling above his head as if awaiting something.

The apprentice’s mind had already grown dazed. Someone who knew he had been scolded by the shopkeeper over the missing item said, “Young fool—did you not fall into the vile custom of cursing with rice?” Upon inquiry, it proved true. “Hurry,” they urged, “go retrieve the rice, rinse it clean with water, and cook it to eat. There may yet be time to save yourself.”

By then, the thunder roared even more fiercely, and violet lightning coiled around the apprentice’s body. The crowd kowtowed on his behalf, pleading for mercy, and the thunder and lightning eased slightly. The apprentice crawled with the others to the privy. Fortunately, the rice had just been cast in and had not yet been disturbed by filth—only about a *ge* remained, still clumped together. They ordered the privy-cleaner to devise a way to retrieve it, and the apprentice himself picked out each grain, rinsed them clean with water, and cooked them as instructed. Even as they scooped the rice from the privy, the thunder god’s voice still lingered at his side. Only when he finished eating did the thunder fall silent, the clouds disperse, and the moon shine as bright as day.

---

**43. Careless Burial of Bones**
The ancient way of gathering bones for burial was earnest,
Yet subtle distinctions within prove hard to discern.
Let Magistrate Zhou’s case serve as warning—
Dry bones, though senseless, repaid kindness on their own.

In Nanhui, it was customary to leave coffins unburied. Some were covered with straw, others walled with bricks, left along the inner and outer city walls or scattered across the fields. After many years, as descendants grew ever poorer, the coffins rotted and split, exposing the remains.

In the *yimao* year of Xianfeng (1855), my father served as *er yin* of this county. One day, strolling through the outskirts, he saw the exposed remains and was moved with sorrow. He consulted with *Xuebo* Bao Shanfu, and together they donated their salaries to bury them. At that time, I had just returned home on leave from the military camp, and my father instructed me and *Mingjing* Li Yinxiang to personally lead laborers in gathering and interring the bones. Yinxiang then told me: "The difficulty of collecting bones lies in the slightest carelessness inviting disaster. During the Qianlong era, Magistrate Zhou, upon assuming office here, was grieved by the sight of exposed remains. He donated his salary to purchase land and had the bones gathered for separate burial. However, the overseer proved incompetent, allowing the laborers to collect them haphazardly—failing to distinguish between male and female, mixing remains indiscriminately, scattering fragments, and even placing this shin with that elbow in the same urn, or combining a woman’s foot with a man’s skull in a single coffin."

Moreover, there were coffins still sturdy and intact, or only partially decayed, which could have been repaired. Yet those tasked with burial would forcibly split them open, rifling through their contents. What began as a virtuous act of "burying *zhi* and covering *ge*"—where *ge* denotes dry bones and *zhi* putrid flesh—nearly became a tyranny of "tomb-raiding and grave-plundering." (As Chen Lin’s *Proclamation to Yuzhou* for Yuan Shao decried: Cao Cao appointed a *Colonel of Grave-Plundering* and a *Captain of Tomb-Raiding*, leaving no remains unexposed in their wake.)

Upon completion of the work, the overseer fell ill. In his sickness, he saw countless men and women—some missing an arm, others limping on one foot; some men with dainty, bound feet, others women of imposing, masculine stature. Still more bore gaping holes in their backs or chests, cleft lips, or shrunken eyes—no telling how many there were.

The wraiths encircled his bed, hurling abuse—so vexed was the sick man by their clamor that he shut his eyes, only for them to pinch his ears and pluck his brows, tormenting him beyond endurance. Every prayer and supplication proved futile. Soon after, the magistrate himself fell ill, and in his sickness, he too heard the cries of grievance. The clamor of voices was too frantic to discern clearly, but the gist was this: bones had been jumbled, remains wantonly scattered, coffins pried open without cause. They had petitioned the gods, they said, and now demanded the magistrate descend to the netherworld to set things right. In the end, he and the overseer perished in quick succession. Of all who took part in this labor, not one survived the span of a few years.

Master of Sitting Among Flowers remarked: "To do good carelessly is to invite retribution from the unseen realm. One might think this enough to dissuade men from the very thought of virtue—but those who harbor the *intention* of doing good yet act with reckless haste, without sincerity or diligence, bring greater harm than those who do nothing at all. Worse still is to bask in the hollow reputation of virtue while inflicting real ruin upon others. If the dead possess any awareness, how could they not ache with sorrow?"

Case Forty-Four: The Magistrate of Chengde – How Could a National Celebration Outweigh Unrepaid Famine?
To Open the Granary Was a Crime He Would Bear Alone
Yet the People’s Straightforward Dao Still Endured as in the Three Dynasties
Their Love and Veneration No Less Than That for the Mulberry of Duke Shao

The late Mr. Shi, father of Shi Daosheng—Surveillance Commissioner of Heng-Yong Circuit in Hunan—had served as a *xiangkui* magistrate of Chengde County in Fengtian. (The second to fifth place in the provincial examination were called *xiangkui*.) A drought had struck the county: no wheat in summer, no grain in autumn. Famine and displacement followed—nine out of ten households suffered. (Famine: pronounced *jin*. The *Analects* commentary states: “When grain does not ripen, it is called *ji*; when vegetables do not ripen, it is called *jin*.” Here, “famine and displacement” means displacement caused by famine.)

That year, the nation celebrated a grand occasion (referring to major imperial weddings or the emperor’s birthday). The chief minister (pronounced *fang*, interchangeable with *bing*; here, referring to the grand councilor) did not wish to trouble the emperor’s sagely consideration with a localized disaster. The officials of the auxiliary capital all complied with his intent. Mr. Shi repeatedly petitioned for relief, but his requests were rejected three times. They even cited the case of fraudulent relief in Gansu as a dire warning to intimidate him. (Intimidate: pronounced *chu*, meaning to frighten.)

Enraged, Mr. Shi opened the Ever-Normal Granary in full to feed the starving. When others tried to stop him, he laughed and said, “If I am charged with unauthorized distribution of granary grain, the worst I face is confiscation, imprisonment, and repayment upon term’s end—my punishment would be mine alone. As the magistrate of this county, how could I hesitate to trade my life for the salvation of ten thousand people?” After the distribution, he submitted a memorial confessing his offense.

His superiors were furious and swiftly impeached him. (Impeachment: as previously annotated in the chapter on Madame Yan.) In the end, he was charged with embezzlement and sentenced to execution. (Embezzlement: pronounced *shi*, meaning deficit. Execution: as previously annotated in the chapter on Jealousy and Treachery.) He fell ill and died in prison. (Illness: pronounced *yu*. The phrase is from the *Book of Han: Annals of Emperor Xuan*.)

At the time, the magistrate’s wife had already passed away, and his son—the future provincial inspector—was still young. None among his colleagues inquired after them. Adrift, they wandered to Liaoshen, then drifted into the capital. At fifteen or sixteen, the boy worked as a hired laborer in a tavern to support himself. One day, several patrons were drinking at the tavern when the young man overheard their accents and recognized them as men from Chengde. He mimicked their speech to engage them in conversation. The patrons exclaimed in surprise, “Are you not our neighbor?” He replied, “No, my family is from south of the Yangtze, but I grew up in your homeland, so I can imitate your way of speaking.” “Then what is your surname?” they asked. “My surname is Shi,” he answered. The men all rose to their feet and said, “There was once an official who served as father and mother to our county—how do you address him?” The young man’s tears welled up, and he choked with sobs, unable to speak.

The patrons ceased their questions and said, “After the second watch tonight, when the tavern closes, come find me at such-and-such an alleyway—do not fail to keep the appointment.” The young man agreed. When evening came, he made an excuse and went. He had not taken more than a few steps from the tavern door when a man in formal attire awaited him on the road and asked, “Are you Master Shi of Chengde?” “Yes,” he replied. The man then helped him into a carriage. Upon reaching the alleyway, those awaiting his arrival lined the road without end. As they entered the gate, every door stood wide open, and the lanterns blazed with dazzling brilliance. No sooner had he alighted from the carriage than over a dozen men in formal attire supported him as he ascended the hall, clustering around him as he took the seat of honor. They prostrated themselves in rows and addressed him, saying, “We have sought you for years! That you should have drifted into such straits—this is all our fault.”

"Fortunately, the spirit of your late father guided us to seek you in the capital, and now we have truly met—could this be anything but Heaven’s will?" At that moment, the young magistrate was still a child when his father had passed, and he had been seven or eight years old at the time. Having drifted in destitution for so long, he was suddenly honored and revered by these men of rank, so unexpected that his eyes widened in stunned silence, unable to utter a single word.

The guests then explained to him: "Your father distributed grain to relieve famine, willingly bearing the weight of capital punishment to save tens of thousands of lives. We are all among those disaster-stricken people who once ate that grain. Year after year, as the harvests ripened, we longed to repay his great virtue. We learned that your father had perished in prison and heard that you, his son, had wandered through Liaodong and Shenyang. We dispatched dozens of men to search everywhere, but no trace could be found. Yesterday, the abbot of the local temple dreamed that your father had arrived to take office and revealed your whereabouts to him. Thus, we were able to come to the capital to seek you."

Thereupon, they bathed him, dressed him in new robes, and prepared the main bedchamber to lodge him. The next day, they set out wine and music, taking turns to offer toasts in celebration.

A certain official, the Dao Overseer, also arrived that day. Addressing the assembly, he said: "My entire family of eight had no steady livelihood. When sudden famine struck, none but your late father could have saved us. In the year my own father passed away, he took my hand and said: ‘Magistrate Shi brought this extraordinary calamity upon himself to rescue the people, scattering his family like stars. You are fortunate to have earned an official rank—if you fail to repay his kindness, you are no son of mine.’ I have borne this charge for years now, and it weighs upon me day and night, yet I have found no means to fulfill it."

This day, fortunately, we behold the young master’s noble bearing and striking appearance, and know he is destined for prominence and renown, to carry forward his late father’s unfinished aspirations. I beseech you all to entrust him to my care. In the future, when the young master achieves his meritorious fame, I alone shall take responsibility for it.” Thereupon, the assembly entrusted him to the care of Chengde.

Previously, after the master’s passing, his family had hastily prepared his burial, abandoning the coffin in a neglected shrine. Now, the people of Chengde selected a proper site for his interment and also established a dedicated shrine in his honor, endowing it with lands for sacrificial offerings. On the very day the Prefect arrived for inspection, the shrine’s construction was completed, and all marveled at the coincidence. They then settled the young master within the shrine, where his clothing, food, and daily needs were overseen by an elder, all provided from the communal funds—always abundant, always generous. Furthermore, they engaged a renowned tutor to instruct him. Yet the Prefect, having endured hardship from a young age, began his studies late; though he labored diligently, his progress was slow. After several years of reading, while he gained a rough understanding of literary meaning, he remained utterly lost in the art of examination essays.

The Daoist master, having heard of this, summoned him to the capital and arranged for him to serve in the Bureau of Strategy and Tactics. He also brokered a marriage for him with a family of noble lineage and promoted his reputation among the high officials. Ultimately, through the Daoist master’s influence, the young man obtained an official post and soon thereafter joined the military campaign in the southern lands of Chu. There, he roused himself to diligence and discipline. Within a few years, he rose to the rank of Prefect and was later recommended for promotion to the position of Provincial Inspector.

Upon petitioning to retire, he was heard to have become a man of Chengde. The Master of the Flower-Adorned Seat remarked: “In governing the people, he spared no effort in saving the multitudes—truly, he did not betray the trust of being a parent to the people! Yet while he rescued those beneath the meanest eaves, the prison’s inmates alone met their doom. His spirit-chariot was forever abandoned in the overgrown shrine, and his young children were reduced to menial drudgery.”

Long in such straitened plight—how could those who do good not tremble with fear? Yet in death he became Yama, King of the Underworld, and in life he left behind a legacy of love. The people of that city, having buried Zhu Yi in Tongxiang, then sought the young master in the capital and Luoyang, moved by dreams that bridged waking and sleep, and bore word of him home. Raised from the muck and mire, he was set upon the heights of azure clouds; his clothing and food were made abundant, his household made whole. They nourished him with poetry and books, guided him with teachers and guardians, spurred him toward merit and renown—until at last he spread his wings in the open sky, lashed his steed upon the royal road, and the scion of a house once noble regained his former station. The favor bestowed was deep indeed, and the recompense was full. Though the love of the sweet pear-tree alone might have stirred their innate devotion, their reverence for virtue and praise of benevolence endured long, ever seeking repayment through his son before they could rest content. In this land, the customs of loyalty and righteousness—might they yet uphold the ways of old?

A certain scholar from Wanjiang—Wanjiang being Anhui—was traveling to Jinling for the examinations. Delayed by contrary winds, he moored his boat along the riverbank and went ashore for a stroll. Wandering into a village, he saw several people conferring on the road, sighing and lamenting as though at a loss for any recourse. Faint weeping reached his ears. After walking a few paces more, the sobs grew nearer, their tone steeped in grief and resentment. He then encountered another group wringing their hands and stamping their feet, their distress no different from the first. Only one among them, standing to the left, remarked, “If only some great-hearted soul would generously provide the cost of a coffin, today’s affair might yet be resolved for both sides.” The others replied, “That is precisely the difficulty—how easily you speak of it!”

The scholar stepped forward, cupped his hands in inquiry, and asked, “Why do you grieve so deeply? May a passing traveler hear the cause?” The group turned to the man on the left, saying, “Surely this is the very man of great compassion you spoke of! Why not tell him?” The man replied, “How can we be certain?” He then explained the matter in full:

“This village is home to a single extended clan. A cousin of mine has died, leaving behind a widow who has steadfastly maintained her chastity. Their son is but a few years old. The widow serves her parents-in-law with filial devotion, spinning and weaving to provide for their needs. A month ago, her father-in-law fell ill, and in seeking physicians and purchasing medicines, she exhausted every resource. Yesterday, he passed away. With no means to obtain a coffin, the family is now forced to consider selling the widow to pay for his burial. She grieves that she cannot remain chaste to the end, yet she mourns the thought of leaving her parents-in-law, who cling to her in their dependence, and she cannot bear to part with her child. From yesterday until now, her sorrow has caused her to faint several times. As for the rest of us, years of famine and drought have left us penniless. We lament that such a woman of loyalty and filial piety should be lost to us, and so we sigh in despair.”

The scholar, moved, asked, “How much silver is needed to secure a coffin?” The group replied, “Thirty taels would suffice.” The scholar then invited them aboard his boat, produced his silver, and said, “I carry forty taels for this journey. I shall give you thirty and keep ten, which will be enough to see me through the examinations.” The villagers were deeply touched; some wept, exclaiming, “Truly, this is a man of great compassion!”

Just then, the wind shifted favorably, and the boatman hoisted the sail, eager to depart. The scholar hurriedly ushered the villagers ashore. In their haste, neither side had time to inquire after names, and they parted with cupped hands and words of gratitude.

Upon reaching Jinling, the scholar found himself short of funds. He borrowed from a fellow townsman trading in the city, thus avoiding destitution. When the examinations began, he entered his assigned cell to find an old man already seated there, motionless. Surprised, he asked where the man had come from, and the old man replied, “I am your cellmate.” The scholar was perplexed, for the cell seemed spacious enough for both to share a cot without crowding. They exchanged names and hometowns, discussed the examinations, and conversed on literature and scholarship, finding mutual delight in their exchange.

As they prepared for sleep, the old man said, “Rest well; do not concern yourself with me.” At the fourth watch, the examination topics were distributed, and the scholar saw the old man seize his brush and write with swift precision. Attempting to rise, the scholar felt as though pressed down by a nightmare and fell back into a stupor. When he awoke the next day at noon, the old man had vanished. Examining his own examination paper, he found it already filled with draft writing. Astonished, he unfurled it to read—only to discover prose of grand depth and poetry of refined elegance, all four compositions complete, truly the work of a top-ranked candidate.

Marveling, he asked the cell attendant, “Where has the old man who shared my cell gone?” The attendant replied absently, “He has left.” The scholar did not press further. Though he and the old man had been strangers, how had the latter composed such masterful work for him? He read and reread the text, praising it without end. Taking up his brush to compose his own thoughts, he could not produce a single original word and so copied the draft in full.

When he entered the examination cell for the second session, the old man was already there, greeting him with a smile: “It seems we share a karmic bond from a past life, for we meet again in the same cell.” The scholar expressed his gratitude for the first session’s assistance, reproaching the old man for leaving without a word. “Why did you vanish without a trace?” he asked. The old man laughed and said, “In a former existence, I owed you several compositions—this was known to the spirits. Why thank me? Tonight, simply rest well and ask no further questions.” The scholar followed his advice.

The next morning, the old man had vanished once more, yet all five classical texts were complete. The same occurred in the third session. After the examinations, the scholar sought the old man’s lodgings, but no one knew of him. Fearing the strangeness of the matter, he dared not speak of it to others.

Upon returning home, as he prepared to cast off from the dock, a man suddenly rushed toward him from the shore, begging to board the boat. It was the old man from his examination cell. Clapping his hands, he exclaimed, “Truly, we share a karmic bond—we meet again!” Overjoyed, the scholar hurriedly welcomed him aboard and asked about his belongings. The old man replied, “I am alone, with nothing to my name.” When asked where he lived, he said, “You will know soon enough.”

As they reached the previous mooring, the old man pointed to a village on the shore: “That is my home. Please, do not scorn my humble dwelling—will you not visit?” With that, he hastened ashore. The scholar followed quickly, but as he entered the village, the old man moved swiftly, vanishing from sight. Hesitating in confusion, he encountered the man who had earlier sought donations for a coffin. The man bowed by the roadside and said, “Sir, have you returned?” The scholar urgently asked for the old man’s name and lineage. The man gasped in shock: “He was my clansman’s uncle, who passed away over a month ago—the very man you once generously funded for burial.” The scholar exclaimed in astonishment, “What do you mean? This gentleman shared my examination cell for all three sessions!”

"After the examination, he again shared my boat on the return journey, seeing me ashore and watching as I entered the village. Now this—could I have encountered a ghost?" The man grew even more alarmed: "Then it must truly have been my uncle’s spirit! My uncle was indeed a *su ru*—a scholar of profound learning—renowned for his vast knowledge and literary skill. Yet he languished for years in the examination halls, dying with his aspirations unfulfilled. In early autumn, my aunt once dreamed of my uncle coming to bid her farewell, saying, ‘I am bound for Jinling to repay the kindness of the coffin and burial. Through his literary blessings, I shall at last reveal that my decades of failure in the examinations were not for lack of merit—*it was not the fault of the battle.*’"

"'Now, sir, what you have encountered aligns precisely with the dream—how extraordinary! My uncle’s wife and daughter-in-law were deeply moved by your kindness to the bone. They repeatedly urged me to wait respectfully by the riverbank, hoping to have the good fortune of inviting you for a visit. Since this strange coincidence has occurred, would you accompany me to their home to settle the matter?'

The scholar thus went with him. Upon arrival, they found a thatched hut with a wattle gate, its walls bare and desolate. Before the hall hung plain white curtains and white candles, with a portrait suspended high. The man who had commissioned the coffin pointed to the scholar and said, 'This is the spirit of my uncle.' Drawing nearer, the scholar saw it was indeed the elderly candidate from the examination hall.

He then recounted in detail their encounter in the hall and how he had drafted the examination essay on his behalf. The man went inside to report, and his uncle’s wife, daughter-in-law, and orphaned son all came out to kneel in gratitude. The scholar, moved, modestly declined, saying, 'I am deeply indebted to your late husband’s noble virtue. My small act has been repaid a hundredfold—my gratitude will never fade. If I should pass the examination, I shall share my wealth and rank with you all, for I dare not betray such great kindness.'

With that, he bowed before the spirit tablet to express his thanks and took his leave."

The announcement of the examination results came, and indeed, he achieved high honors. The following year, he passed the metropolitan examination and was appointed magistrate of Wu. He continued to provide for the family of the elderly scholar, summoning his son to the yamen and engaging a renowned tutor to instruct him. Later, the son succeeded in his studies and entered the Hanlin Academy.

The Master of the Flowery Seat remarked: *"Goodness born of utmost sincerity is true goodness. When this scholar divided his gold to purchase the coffin, did he harbor even the slightest thought of seeking fame or expecting repayment? His grief in weeping was enough to deepen his emotion; the filial piety and integrity of the deceased were enough to command his respect. Thus, from genuine compassion, great mercy arose—truly, he was a man of perfect sincerity! In the Nine Springs, gratitude transcended the barrier of the unseen realm; by composing the examination essay on his behalf, he secured success in the spring and autumn examinations. A small act met with abundant reward—why should men hesitate to do good?"*

**Parallel Prose (Four-Six Style), Cold Armor:**
*A chance lodging held a fate unseen,*
*Yet greed for gain led to fields and gardens bought.*
*Who knew a wolf’s heart would meet a tiger’s lung?*
*By New Bridge’s river, grievance was wrought.*

In the eastern countryside of Qu’e (Qu’e being Danyang County), there lived a man named Leng Jia, poor and of no virtue. One evening, a traveler sought lodging at his home and bestowed upon him generous gifts. After several days, the guest became intimate with Leng’s wife, coveting her wealth. Leng, aware of this, feigned ignorance and allowed it. Soon after, the guest departed, only to return half a month later. He produced gold, pearls, silks, and other treasures in great abundance, giving a portion to Leng to purchase fields and a house, while entrusting the remainder to his wife, instructing her to bury it in a pit. Thereafter, the guest came and went frequently, and Leng grew prosperous through this association. Over time, his comings and goings aroused suspicion, and Leng realized he was a bandit, though he bore no ill will toward him.

Later, the guest vanished for months. One day, a messenger arrived in haste, delivering a letter from the guest to Leng. It revealed that he had attempted to rob the household of a great gentry family in Wu, had been captured, and was now imprisoned in Wu County, his confession already recorded. Fortunately, he was not the ringleader, and with clever planning, he might yet escape death. He urgently begged Leng to bring silver to Suzhou and arrange matters on his behalf, his pleas filled with sorrow and desperation.

Upon receiving the letter, Leng secretly consulted his wife. Fearing that sparing the bandit’s life would bring lifelong trouble, they set out for Suzhou under cover of night. When Leng met the bandit, he deceived him, claiming he would devise a plan to secure his release and promising a full report. In secret, however, he bribed the authorities to solidify the bandit’s guilt, shifting him from an accomplice to the principal offender, and had him executed at once.

More than a year after the bandit’s death, Leng entered the city and walked along the New Bridge riverside with several companions. Suddenly, he cried out, *“There he comes!”* Then, *“He’s thrusting a fork at me!”* He shrieked, collapsed to the ground, and died.

**Postscript**

Perusing the accounts in this book, one sees how aid rendered in a moment of dire urgency may yield boundless retribution. Many admire such deeds yet lament the rarity of such opportunities. Yet the ways of doing good are innumerable—what one lacks is not the means but the heart.

The rewards of accumulated virtue follow as closely as a shadow clings to form. To benefit oneself, one must first benefit others. Nowhere are urgency and hardship more acute than in the ravages of fire and flood. Often, when the Fire God rages unchecked, the poor and humble are suddenly struck by calamity, their suffering so grievous that the eye cannot bear to witness it. Or when floodwaters surge to the heavens, homes are swept away, and the desperate wails of the doomed rise in chorus—left with no choice but to await death.

For those who wish to sow virtue, such moments present the finest of opportunities. One should investigate the households afflicted by disaster and, in secret, dispense silver to offer relief. A few coins may save a single life; a few dozen may preserve an entire family. The wealthy, of course, ought to give generously, but even those of modest means should strive to persuade others to contribute, joining together in the cause. How limitless, then, would their merit be!

To rescue lives below is to move the heart of Heaven above. The Dao of reward for virtue and retribution for vice never fails. The Five Blessings and Three Abundances may be attained without effort—why, then, regret the infrequency of such occasions?

Truly, extraordinary good deeds beget extraordinary merit. The Way of Heaven delights in reciprocity, a truth verified time and again throughout the ages. Thus, I append these few lines to the end of this work, earnestly exhorting the benevolent gentlemen of the world.
